THE RED PAGE.
The Forest Flora of Neiv South Wales, Part I,
by .T. H. Maiden (Sydney ; N.S.W. Govt.
Printer ; Is.) is dated 1902, and was delivered for
review on Feb. 18. If published in 1903 it should
be so dated; if published in 1902, that rate of
transmission is too slow.
vZ
Generally speaking, the Australian Govern-
ments have many more good servants than you
would expect from the tendency of “the Govern-
ment stroke” to slacken zeal and discourage
initiative. There are barnacles, of course ;
but one meets good men continually, and
still with a slight reaction of astonishment.
J. H. Maiden, N.S.W. Govt. Botanist, appears to
be a thoroughly good servant. We like his alert
mind, his lively enthusiasm for bis calling, and
the spirit with which he defends Sydney Botanic
Gardens from utilitarian attack.—Which is meant
for a word of praise in season (and the carp at
home in this aquarium will be found in the next
paragraph).
jZ J*
The Forest Flora is to be issued in shilling
parts, number not stated. It is designed to give
both scientific and popular information about
N.S.W. trees, in both the aesthetic and the commer-
cial aspect; and the writer does not disdain rele-
vant matter because it is merely interesting.
The object is good; and the work is good.
The botanical commentary is written with
care and knowledge, and practical notes are
added. Part I. deals with silky oak, rusty fig,
turpentine and pittosporum. We suggest, first,
that a list of the trees considered be printed on the
outer cover, by way of contents-table, and that
the inner cover bontain a specific invitation to
readers to add to the stock of the writer’s
knowledge, admittedly incomplete. Second, that
a running subject-heading be added to the pages :
for convenience. Third, that the “ explanation ”
of Miss Flockton’s excellent illustrative plates be
printed on the plates: it is inconvenient to turn
over several pages for reference. Fourth, that the
Govt. Printing Office be adjured to supply a com-
petent reader: there are several blunders in
spelling and grammar— e.g., homogenous, p. 12;
“ The following appear to us militate against the
growth of cobra,” p. 24; “There is one portion
which, from long experience and observation, I
must remain at issue, ” p. 26 ; —and so on.
vZ jZ
The Forest Flora represents a valuable idea and
useful labour; and it is relinquished to readers
with quotation of this pretty aboriginal variant of
a world-myth, reported from Prairie Creek, N.Q.
It was a long time ago. No one lived in the country
then ; but pittosporum bush grew along this creek, as it
grows now. One day a seed-pod opened, and out of it
came a beautiful young gin. Very beautiful she was, and
stately, and she lived alone on the creek by the water-
holes, hunting sugar-bags and trapping duck, and getting
fat grubs from the trees.
It was a long time ago. She lived thus for months, it
may be years ; but all the time she was pining for a mate.
And one day she was strolling past another patch of
scrub, and looking at a pittosporum bush, when, lo ! a
seed-pod opened, and out of it came a handsome young
blaekfellow.
He was well-built and manly ; he was of her own age :
and without any ado she bade him good-day, and entered
into conversation. He walked with her to her camp by
the waterhole, and they became good friends, and lived
there together. After a time she gave birth to a son and
daughter, and when these grew up they went lower
down the creek, and settled there And they were happy.
It was a long time ago.
For in those halcyon days marriage by capture
was unknown ; and .. . the lady spoke first. The
blaekfellow, it should be added, pointed out the
pittosporum bush that was the mother of the gin,
and indicated that the bush-mother of the young
man was also known.
*
The investigation of flora reminds us of an old
grievance : to wit, we have but a scanty poetical
nomenclature for our flowers and trees. And . . .
contributor E.M.S. continues —
The foreign bard has at command a store of beautiful
and familiar flowers and plants, possessing well-known
names which he can introduce into his verses for similes,
and which go straight to the hearts of his readers. The
Australian writer, on the contrary, finds often the most
common objects destitute of vernacular titles, or if pos-
sessing them, they are usually an echo of an old-world
name—native rose, native bluebell, native violet, native
daisy, 4cc. Suppose I burst into song—a love song—an
Australian love song, freed from all foreign influence, you
get something like this :
Gentle maiden, hear my singing t
There is none 1 love but thee :
Sweet as smilax glyciphylla
Are thy dear lips unto me.
From between them, lightly parted.
Doth thy balmy breathing go:
Not boronia ledifolia
Doth such blessed fragrance know.
0, believe thy fond adorer
When he tells thee, maMen meek,
That epacris longiflora
Would grow pale beside thy cheek t
Paludosa microphylla
With thy white neck cannot vie,
And the modest wahlenbergia
Is not bluer than thine eye-
Dost remember where I wooed thee ?
Where our plighted vows were made t
Ah, those kisses, those caresses
In the pittosporum shade 7 . . .
As with fond, tenacious purpose
To its chosen , well-loved tree,
Clings Mgrsdenia suavelens,
All my life I ’ll cling to thee !
S
The grievance is stated ; but the remedy is not
easily found. There is no denying that familiar
poetical names for flora are among the most effec-
tive weapons of the poet. Many old-world names
have been worn into beauty by centuries of attri-
tion : uncouth in their origin, they have become
admirable in form and cadence by dint of sheer
hard usage. Then, old-world names are thrilling
with associated ideas ; they are keys to unlock a
million doors, of memory. As a distinguished
writer has pointed out, “ The rose is a beautiful
flower, but the most beautiful only because thou-
sands of years of care and cultivation have been
lavished to bring it to perfection, because thou-
sands of lovers have breathed its perfume, thou-
sands of poets have apostrophised its exquisite
form. Give the same care and cultivation to a
hundred modest bush flowers, draw them from
obscurity as the rose has been drawn from the
parent wilderness, let them be worshipped
and adored through centuries of sentiment—and
we have here the rivals of the Rose herself.”
Only, before we can worship and adore, we want
names; and those names must be known, not
only to the poet-priests, but to the vulgar,
“ Native’’-something won’t do: it is not a name,
but a label like “The Unknown God.”
This is suggested. Language is being created
continually at haphazard : let us create it delibe-
rately. A substantial body of a hundred thousand
Australians, labouring persistently in speech and
print, would soon supply our long-felt want; and
that number of devotees is not beyond the dreams
of poetry. The Australian Natives’ Association
might be diverted for a while from its business of
machine politics and mutual admiration, in order
to further the great aim. A water-tight, beer-
tight pledge might be mentally made by every
adherent of the league : “I, A B , solemnly
vow that I will name the so-called ‘native rose’
Lamio, rtow and for evermore, Amen. So help me
Australia!” Sporadic individual usage once
created, a national epidemic usage would follow
naturally ; and bv-and-by we would accumulate
associated ideas and broaden down from precedent
to precedent.
First we need the names. These should be
compounded chiefly of long vowels and liquid
consonants, with just sufficient variation to
give them individuality—following Bacon’s idea
that “there is no excellent beauty that hath
not some strangeness in the proportion.”
A dactylic or trochaic disposition of the syllables
would be the most useful and permanent, though
choice monosyllables would be valuable. As far
as possible, the colour and sound of the word
should imitate the flower. Vowels and consonants
have distinct colours, singly or in combination ;
though, unluckily for our purpose, colour impres-
sions differ.* Still, there may be a statistical har-
mony. As a beginning The Bookfellow will con-
sider and classify everybody’s lists of not more than
ten well-known Australian flowers whose claim to
poetical glory is impeded by harsh or unsuitable
names. When possible, lists should give botanical
names, habitat, and current misnomers. Flowers
such as the waratah, which are not too heavily
handicapped, should be passed over.—This may
be a dream: it depends on your interpretation
thereof.
+

The poetical litigant ’s amusing. Melb. Practice
Court was amused last week by a litigant who
mouthed his plaint against his father in lines like
these (quoted from Sydney Telegraph’s report)—
“ My r o d. I charge defendant here with a wrongful use
of power
In selling as a mortgagee in mode unlawfully,
To wit, tuo mortgages. He sold of titles different
For one lump sum and in one lot unto one purchaser,
At undervalue of deep gross, through bad faith bitterness ,
And with ill motives to oppose myself as mortgagor."
“What’s that?” interrupted the judge, in astonish-
ment. “ Dear me ! is it blank verse ? ”
“ It enables me to state my case,” said the plaintiff,
and, waving his arm, he went on—
“ A Full Court case, Volume the eighth, of Y.L.R. My
Lord,
Cn : Ross, against the Vi dorian Building Society ”
A rude outburst of laughter drowned plaintiff’s voice,
and he immediately took the opportunity of handing up
to the judge a copy of his epic, and continued—
“ My lord , in the year 1881 Great Britain passed a law
Named the Conveyancing and Law and Property Act."
(Laughter.) . . . There was much laughter during the
reading of many other stanzas. Coming to the close of
his oration, plaintiff, in a fine declamatory style, re-
ferred to the ill-will of his father in the following terms :
“ So bitter he tha/t when 1 was but V years,
Fair with my mother's care, he sadly to>k his home
To Melbourne from Geelong, that. I might no more dwell
with him.
May this pugfice t 'll the suit be heard. As to his bitterness
With motives ill conjoined. And then as to the ninth
paragraph,
It forms a link in the chain of fraud and cannot be
struck out ”...
At the end, dropping into prose, plaintiff said : “ I
claim substantial damages, each side to pay the costs of
its application now.”
His lloßour was apparently unmoved by the poet’s
effort, and simply said he would have to amend his state-
ment of claim as asked by striking out Ibur paragraphs
and altering the others, but, he could attach his poem to
the claim if he liked. (Loud laughter.)
“Can I have a week to amend my claim?” asked
Yes, a fortnight if you like. Costs three
cuineas.* ....
,-oet put his poem tenderly in his bag and went
happily away with a smile of triumph on his face.
This plaintiff was lucky in appearing before
Judge Hood merely. If he had arisen in another
court, with Hermione —
Your honours all ,
I do refer me to the oracle:
ApoUo be my judge ! —
the deity might have- handled him worse than
Marsyas was handled.
The Neto Idea, a Melb. monthly for women, is
welcome. Women want that kind of inaga., and
there is no reason why they should go abroad for
it. The staple is triviality, of course; but the
triviality has profound significance. Straw-hats
show which way the sex-instincts blow, and the
love of personal adornment is commendable not
only in itself, but also as the source of decorative
art.
J* J*
Besides, The New Idea aspires to be the Aus-
tralian woman’s mouthpiece. The new idea of
giving woman a mouth piece may seem, like gild-
ing refined gold, a “ wasteful and ridiculous
excess ” ; still, in our developing world, the
public need may exist contemporaneously with
the private satisfaction, —and that is seen par-
ticularly in connection with the case of woman.
It is not desirable to lure woman awa> from her
primal function by polishing her brain too sedu-
lously ; for enlightened opinion returns to the
ordinance of Solon and the compromise of Athens.
But the Review of Reviews Co., which publishes The
New Idea, is doubtless right to gij. e opportunity
for argument; and, as we are assured by many
clamorous platform orators that man has the best
end of the social stick, it is pleasant to think that
woman can always rely upon the clergy to keep
her end up.
Jt
The February number of The New Idea has a
capital interview with P. M. Carew-Smyth,
art inspector of Victorian schools, regarding
“Brush-work in Education ” —that is, the use of
the brush in order to train hand and eye, and to
teach the rudiments of decorative design, Here
is a design by P. M. C.-S. based on our own wild
honeysuckle, and its decorative value is apparent.
This design, the end-paper drawn by Percy
Lindsay for The Bulletin Story Book, and one or
two other things of the kind, may represent the
beginning of Australian applied art. Lucian
Henry, years ago, made some designs for metal-
work and sculpture ; but they did not seem com-
mercially profitable. An Arts and Crafts Club
has been founded recently in Sydney, and one
hopes it will prosper. Now . . the commercial
enterprise of constructing fabrics and furniture
based on Australian designs is recommended to
investors. There is already a demand for Aus-
tralian carpets and floor-cloths, mats, wall-papers,
dress-stuffs, and so on ; and, as the idea of The
Australian Nation grows, that demand will in-
crease. It is time somebody with business brains
set about meeting it. We are weary of the Lion
and the Rose. Begin with a wall-paper and a car-
pet : produce them well ; and they will sell. Our
fauna and flora are especially adapted for decora-
tive use. Lindsay’s design, just referred to, is
a good end-paper; but it would look possibly
better as a frieze. Why, when an Australian
goes house-furnishing, should he be restricted to
the patterns and emblems of countries that
probably he has never seen, when his own original,
charming continent lavishes inspiring ideas for
the adornment of his home?
Then, turning the pages of The New Idea with
that noble notion, the Ewig-Weibliche led
suddenly to this ignoble issue—•
Pimples ...
Purple pimples are chronic . . .
“Purple pimples are chronic ! ” It lingers, it is
haunting, talismanic. . . . Where do women get
these purple pimples? Where do editors get
these purple phrases? ... It fastens on the
brain, and gnaws, and gnaws . . . Must lay the
ghastly ghost . . .

“ Ada Cambridge "is publishing Thirty Years in Aus-
tralia— described by the publisher as “ A highly interest-
ing account of a generation in Australia by a distinguished
writer. Mrs. Cross’s style is picturesque, and the book is
more attractive than many novels. The early difficulties
of Australian,settlers, life in the towns, and life on the
farms are vividly described."
Memoranda —
“ L’acre parfum des eucalyptus.” That is the second-
no, third time we have met that acre parfum. Will ie
Courrier Francais kindly tell France that the perfume ol
the eucalyptus at home is piquant, troublant, seduisant,
but never acre to the true Australian soul until bottled
for influenza. (Possibly it changes on the Riviera, where
rastaquouerea go to exhale the acridity of Parise.)
D.F. : Melb. Argus, advertising(l4/2/3) the sale of seven
different translations of Homer, sets them out each time
as “Horner’s Iliad,’’ “ Horner’s Odyssey." Melb. Age,
comparing (14/2/3) one of Wellington’s lieutenants with
Napoleon’s dashing Murat, speaks of “ the Marat ol
the British Army,” and says further on that “in
his magnificent uniform . . . he was no less conspicuous
in the field of battle than Marat” That absurdity surely
would have drawn a smile from even the sour, squalid
“ friend of Che people."
Picture of Christian baptism in Australia, by Canon
Jones, Sydney :--“A number of women, with their chil-
dren, gathered round the font, no sponsors present, no
congregation, the women themselves with no realisation
of the solemn covenant to be formally entered into, often
not knowing what they have to say in response to the
clergyman’s questions, but requiring to have the answers
put into their mouths by the clergyman or verger; no
attempt to carry out the direction about dipping the
child or pouring water upon it, and sometimes, when the
child is old and vigorous enough to struggle when taken
into the arms of the clergyman, considerable doubt
whether water is applied at all.”

BALLADE OF PURPLE PIMPLES.
Moons are a fickle fry ;
Suns are escaping gas ;
See Pan-Creation die
Of enlarged pancreas 1
The universal mass
Lacks even curchitectonic:
God is Man’s looking-glass . .
But purple pimples are chronic.
The amaranthine lie
Arraigns its purple class:
Beauty must Helen sigh ;
Valour, Leonidas:
Where’s Tyre ? where Hippocras ?
1 ask with pen ironic
(In purple ink, alas !) . . .
But purple pimples are chronic.
And Chronos, throned on high
Above the vast morass,
Sees matter form and fly,
Sees Maya gleam and pass :
The worlds are moron as grass :
His scythe, a leech laconic,
Shrives all—riefas aut fas . . .
But purple pimples are chronic.

Tout passe, tout lasse, tout casse ,
Even the rat bubonic :

But purple pimples are chronic.
That, surely, is a stake driven through the vara-
pire-heart !

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Grass Widower.
Oh, a cove feels awful lonely
When he’s by hisself a spell ;
If you asked him what’s the matter
Ten to one he couldn’t tell.
Food ain’t right, the bread is doughy,
Meat is tough as bloomin’ clay ;
Nothin seems to go kerreckly
When the missus is away.
Cows to milk at early mornin’,
Bloomin’ poddies then to feed ;
Pigs an poultry squeakin’ round ye—
Farmer’s life is nice indeed !
Then you have to get some breakfus’—
Dash it! have to bake to-day !
Batchin’s gettin’ mighty multy
When the missus is away.
Crows is takin’ all the chickens
While yer ploughin’ down below ,*
Hark ! goanners in the hen’s nest!
Hens is cacklin’ high an’ low.
You must get that ole goanner,
If it takes ye half a day;
Strange these bloomin’ things will ’appen
When the missus is away !
Neighbor’s sheep is through the bound’ry—
S’pose the bloomin’ wires is broke ;
Bloomin’ dog is out a-huntin’—
This is gettin’ past a joke '
Bloomin’ cows is in the barley—
Bust ’em ! let ’em bust, I say !
Ain t it funny, ’ow it ’appens
When the missus is away ?
Bust it all ! I’ll catch ole Bally,
Canter over to the post.
If this game goes on much longer
I’ll be poor as Caesar’s ghost.
Wimmen is so bloomin’ selfish !
They don’t care a rap, not they,
How a bloomin’ feller suffers
While his missus is away.
By the hokey, here’s a letter !
From the missus, too, all right.
Wonder what can be the matter !
Gives a cove a sort of fright.
If she’s goin’ to stop much longer,
There’ll be the dooce to pay;
I ain’t goin’ to do the graftin’
While she’s flyin’ round all day.
“ Dear old Tom, you must be lonely ;
Good old chap, to let me go;
But I miss you every day, Tom,
For I love you, Tom, you know.
I am leaving here on Thursday ;
Meet the coach on Saturday.”
That’s to-day—why—get up, Bally !
Hip, hip, hip, hoo bloomin’ ray J
n s.w.
JOHN C. WEARNE.
H.M.S.
Three thousand tons o’ metal,
Two hundred hearts a-score,
Ten thousand winds a-beatin’,
Ten billion seas a-roar !
Ten furnace-doors a-bangin’,
Ten sweaty chests a-steam,
Ten souls a-cursin’, cursin’,
With pain and heat o’ steam !
She ships it down her funnel—
Splash on her boiler steel!
We get it on our bodies,
In livid scar and weal !
We get it when she walk wa
Down under water-.-pi re.
We “get it ” when tht ocean
Pours down and drcw.is her f rs 3 !
“ Why ain’t she steamin’ faster ? ”
King Gold-lace thunders down ;
With scorched and parboiled bodies,
His Maj.’s stokers frown.
Why ain't she steamin' faster !—■
Because we get no coal
To drive this cursed hell-box
To aught but Davy’s goal!
Because our fuel’s measun d
So many tons per diem ;
Because our fires are out, an’
Our souls are scorched with steam !
Because she’s took to drinkin’
The salt stuff thro’ her stack ;
Because her engines funk it;
Because her heart’s a-rack !
“It’s bully in the navy,”
Chips Johnny Mercantile;
“ It’s bully in the King’s ships,
With lazin’ shafts to ile ! ”
It’s bully down in hell, then,
If it is bully here !
A man can leave a liner —
A King’s man cannot clear.
You’ve got to stop and stand it—
The torture of the steam ;
You’ve got to clean her boilers,
With heart and soul a-scream.
“ It’s bully in the navy ! ”
Yah ! bully when she ships
The seas to boil and scald you,
Down thro’ her funnel lips 1
It’s bully when your flesh is
Boiled soft as tender lamb ;
It’s joyous when the stokehold
Is used for picklin’ ham !
It’s better in the Navy ?
Gimme the Mercantile !
A King’s man’s got to stop, an*
Graft under Sheol’s smile !
Gimme the liners’ stokeholds,
A man can clear from them !
You cannot when you’re stokin’
For Crown an’ diadem !
It’s Hell to be a King’s Man,
With lazin’ shafts to ile;
You’re parboiled in the Navee !
Gimme the Mercantile !
GRANT HERVEY.

A Doctor’s Present.
A Thrilling Story of Suffering
A Silver Lining to the Darkest Cloud.
The Case of Mrs. A. JOSEPHS.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
There is no suburb that has changed its
status as completely during the past twenty
years as that of Woolloomooloo.
the bay bearing that name, with its
picturesque walks along the foreshores, and
with Hyde Fark, the Botanical Gardens,
and the extensive Domain recreation
grounds in close proximity, Woolloomooloo
is one of the most attractive localities
around Sydney, and in it, at Iso. 18 Wood s
Lane, lives Mrs. Annie Josephs, whose in-
terview with our reporter is reproduced
below. v ~ „
“ £ have lived at tliis address,” said Mrs.
Josephs, “for the past eight years, and
when I had been here about half that time
I thought I was going to die. It was after
a confinement that I suffered from blood
poisoning, besides which I lost a great
quantity of blood, and became so weak
that I could not put a foot to the ground
for nearly twelve months. For almost a
year I was in bed, being nothing but a
helpless and hopeless invalid, for I must
tell you that all the treatment I had
received did not lead me to believe
that I would ever get better. There
seemed nothing for it but to lie and
wait patiently for death to relieve me of
my sufferings, as I kept on wasting and
wasting away, and I was almost paralysed
with agony.” . . . ~
“ Where were the pains principally
located ? ” enquired the pressman.
“They were in every part of my body,
and my head swelled till it was about twice
its natural size, besides aching so intensely
that I scarcely knew what was going on
around me. Sometimes I became quite
stupefied with pain, and as I never could
sleep at night it is not to be wondered at
that I got so worn out that I used to lie
like one in a trance. All my limbs were
swollen and terrible pains afflicted them as
well, and when the severity of my aches
became so intolerable that a change in my
position was necessary, I had to have a
hoop bound round with wadding put under
my legs, because I was too weak to keep
them bent for a second without having
something underneath to support them.
There was no doubt that the longer 1 lived
the worse I got, for my nervous system was
frightfully strained, as 1 could not keep my-
self from shaking, and my mind was always
in a state of fear and anxiety. By some
means that 1 cannot explain, because I was
not enlightened on the subject myself, my
vision became very dim, and I nearly lost
the sight of my right eye. As an operation
was indispensable, I went under it, and
although my sight was saved, yet the strain
on my feeble constitution was almost too
great for me to bear. I got more nervous
than ever, and could not be left alone in the
house. sThe shaking of a window or the
creaking of a door sent a thrill of terror
through me, and the next sensation
that I usually experienced was giddiness,
as though things Were moving about in the
room. And you would hardly credit how I
felt if I ate any food, although I should say
‘drank’ instead of ‘ate,’ because I was
only allowed the lightest of diet, and it
was invariably liquid. All the same, my
stomach could not digest it, and the Yvay 1
used to swell out with wind was something
awful, my chest also being troublesome
with a feeling as if a weight was inside me.
My skin was as yellow as a guinea, owing
to my liver being diseased, the doctors said,
and my face was so thin and disfigured that
many of my friends did not know me.
Having previously been very stout, they
could not realise it was me when they saw
nothing but a shadow before them, and the
ominous shakes of their heads made me feel
very downhearted. It was evident they
thought I was dying, and some of them
have since told me they felt sure I could not
“You mentioned something about doctors
just now What did they say about your
prospects ? ”
« For a long time they tried to cheer me
by uttering kind and encouraging words,
but they did not do me any practical gooa
with their medicines, so then I submitted
myself for treatment at several hospitals.
The physic prescribed at those institutions
took no effect, so I sank down to the lowest
depths of despair, because I thought my
fate was sealed. Later on I thought 1
would make a final effort, and I sent for one
of the doctors who had been attending me
before, but he said that he could not do
any more for me than what he had
previously done, excepting to advise me to
take a course of Clements Tonic. He said
it was au excellent medicine, and soon after-
wards he good-naturedly made me a present
of two bottles.” t
“ How did you feel then, Mrs. Josephs ?
“I felt as if my whole life depended
upon Clements Tonic, and in doing so I am
sure I did not exaggerate the seriousness
of the situation, as every other treatment
had failed. When those two bottles of
Clements Tonic were finished there was
more life in me than there had been tor
twelve months. I could then take a little
food and enjoy it. Before taking Clements
Tonic everything I tasted was as it i baa
put a penny in my mouth, which left a
coppery flavor behind ; but that unpleasant-
ness was soon remedied, and, besides, the
coating over my tongue was cleared away.
Constipation had troubled me, but Clements
Tonic soon remedied that, and all the time
I was getting more light-hearted than ever,
because my pains were decreasing. 1 hese
improvements gave me a renewal of hope,
and, as was prudent, I clung tenaciously to
Clements Tonic. The next thing to cheer
my drooping spirits was to observe the
swellings in my head and limbs were being
reduced, and the pains were much less
severe than they had been. It was a very
unusual thing for me to wake up in the
night, but if I did I soon went to sleep
again, and rested peacefully till morning ;
and another great benefit that I derived
from the use of Clements Tonic was that
my nerves were toned to their normal con-
dition. Those of my friends who had daily
expected to hear of my death could haidly
realise the fact that I was being earned
further away from the grave every day by
Clements Tonic, and although four years
have passed since then, yet we often have a
chat about the narrow escape i had, and
Clements Tonic is the household remedy of
us all. I was trying to tell you how 1
progressed. Well, in a week or two more 1
was able to eat solid food without the least
compunction, as my digestive orgaus weie
as healthy as they could be, for there was
no flatulence or chest pains to inconvenience
me, and there was not an ache in any part
of my system. When my strength and
health were completely restored I devoutly
thanked God for my happy release, and as
there is no doubt that Clements Tonic saved
my life you are at liberty to publish my
story in any way that the public may hear
of it.”

SATURDAY, MARCH 7, 1903.
Notice to Quit.
London, Tuesday.—Addressing 7 the members of the
Capetown Chamber of Commerce on Saturday, Mr.
Chamberlain impressively declared that the autonomous
colonies must see the necessity for realising their position.
He declared that the burden of the Empire was becoming
greater than the Motherland could bear alone, and as the
colonies grew in influence and wealth they must either
abandon the idea of forming part of the Empire or take
their full share in its responsibilities.— Cablegram.
Australia has received notice to quit. It
is not, so far, a definite notice, but Joseph
Chamberlain gives this country and the
other self-governing sections of Greater
Britain warning that, unless certain con-
ditions are fulfilled, they must gather up
their joint and several carpet bag and leave
the Empire. The fact that the notice was
given at Capetown tends to show that Joseph
is sorrowfully lacking in a sense of humor—
but then nobody suspected the man of the
orchid in the turned coat of any great sense
of humor. A large section probably
a majority—of the people of Cape Colony
recently manifested a desire to adopt
the policy that Joseph of Birmingham
so vigorously recommends. They tried to
abandon the idea of forming part of the
empire, aud to join their brethren in creating
an Afrikander republic. Some of them were
hanged for it, and some were shot, and some
were imprisoned, and many were disfran-
chised, ilnd many had their property con-
fiscated, and all were denounced by the
raging Jingo as something worse than the
Father of Iniquity himself—all this for
attempting to do what Joseph Chamberlain
now tells them to do. Joseph Chamberlain,
in his new character, is a preacher of sedition,
secession, insurrection and treason, aud an
apostle of all the political doctrines which
were mostabhorred in the grand old Mafficking
days, when Sydney got drunk for joy aud
Melbourne upset tramcars for the glory of
England. This is a curious reversal of
things, and shows Joseph as an incon-
siderate, hasty aud loud-mouthed person
who rushes in where angels fear to tread.
• * • *
Certainly, if “ the colonies" choose to
remain part of the empire, aud have a chance
ro remain on fair conditions, they should
rhare i:s burdeus and responsibilities. But,
while they are giveu no choice iu the matter,
the question as to their position admits
«f a great deal of argument. If the Govern-
ment, of which Joseph Chamberlain is a
Jf-rge component part, will assure “ the
wuiouies ’ that they may deal seriously and
practically with the question of secession
which he has raised, and may do so without
anybody being executed or imprisoned for
it, and if, after considering the question,
they decide not to secede, then it will cer-
tainly be reasonable to ask them to look at
the defence problem in a new light. And
after that the real difficulty will only
begin. For, if Australia and Canada
are to he responsible, equally with Great
Britain (in proportion to their popu-
lation) for the defence of the empire, then
they must have a corresponding share in
deciding the policy of the empire. England
must climb down to some extent (to a
great extent, indeed) from its haughty posi-
tion as the “predominant partner.” It
must share the Government of the Empire
with “ the colonies.” It must reckon on a
possible combination of Cape Dutch, French
Canadian, Australian and Irish legislators in
a new Imperial Parliament making England
some day a mere side issue, and outvoting
its politicians, and upsetting its traditions.
The Crown must either give up its veto on
“ colonial ” legislation, or else the Crown’s
lost and obsolete veto on British legislation
must be restored to make things even. The
high and dignified offices of the army, navy,
and diplomatic service must be shared with
“ mere colonials,” and the lofty, blastiferous
British military person who now boycotts
“colonial” officers out of the army
must learn that there are other
pebbles besides himself on the beach.
The British Cobdenite must prepare for
the possibility of having his blessed Free-
trade gospel upset by the votes of “ colonial ”
legislators added to those of the growing
Protectionist party in the United Kingdom.
He must even be satisfied to contemplate the
possibility of the seat of Empire being re-
moved from London altogether when some
of “ the colonies ” grow to such dimensions
as to over-shadow England in point of
population. He may see his Irish coercion
devices reversed, and his Japanese alliance
knocked endways, and his House of Lords
treated with contumely. The English
Imperial Federationist only contemplates a
state of things in w'hich “ the colonies ” will
supply men and money to back up his
traditional policy of opposing Russia, prop-
ping up the Sultan, and extending the
already bloated Empire all over Asia and
Africa. But he must also contemplate a
state of things in which the “ colonial ” vote
might turn the scale and decide that the
Empire’s nigger possessions had better be
abandoned, and that Russia can have
Constantinople any day so far as the British
Empire is concerned.
• • • •
To the average Englishman all this would
be a horrid and impossible dream. His
idea, in a general way, is that he will
make war or peace, according to the interests
of England, will decide upon alliances, will
direct the whole Imperial policy as afore-
time, and that the other sections of the
Empire will humbly supply their contin-
gents and pay their share of the bill. The
fact that the existing unsatisfactory position
arises mostly from his own fixed resolve to
run the whole show hasn’t yet dawned upon
him. He utterly fails to realise that the
principal reason why Imperial Federation is
impossible is that England really wants to
federate by itself. At present Britain has to
carry the principal burden of the Empire’s
wars because they are solely Britain’s wars.
It has to pay the bulk of the Empire’s mili-
tary and naval charges because it insists on
being sole owner and director of the army
and navy. If it is willing to face the possi-
bility of an Australian admiral heiug iq
charge of the navy, or a French-Canadian
acting as Commander-in-Chief of the army,
or a Cape Dutchman being Governor-
General of India, or a Tasmanian holding
the Imperial Premiership, then all things
may be possible. Scotland aud Ireland
share the burden of the hill with England,
and a Scotchman has several times been
Premier of the United Kingdom, and an
Irishman has been the greatest Commander-
in-Chief the Empire ever possessed. Canada,
South Africa, and Australasia can all com-
pare with either Scotland or Ireland in popu-
lation. If they are to share the burden aud
the bill, the Australian Imperial Premier,
or the Canadian Commander-in-Chief, or
even a possible accidental predominance of
“ the colonies ” in the Imperial Cabinet,
must all he admitted as possibilities.
• * * •
If this question of sharing the bill, and
(consequently) sharing the control and the
responsibility, is to be taken seriously, there
must be a really Imperial Parliament which
will be legislative over-lord of the Empire.
“The colonies” must have a voice, accord-
ing to their population, in deciding how
that Parliament is to be constituted, e\ efi if
Britain’s sacred House of Peers goes under
in the scrimmage. There must be an end
of the theory that the Briton who happened
to remain in England is of superior clay to
the Briton who didn’t happen to remain in
England, aud the former Briton must climb
down to the common level. The mere
“ colonist,” in addition to bearing a part of
the bill and bloodshed of empire, would theu
have a voice iu deciding whether a good deal
of the empire is worth the cost and the
bloodshed involved in its retention. And
other new questions will arise. Every
State has, admittedly, a right to call on all its
citizens for defence purposes in time of need,
and to tax all property within its borders to
provide the means of defence. In Australia a
very large proportion of the property belongs
to absentees, mostly resident in England,
anu a very considerable proportion ot Aus-
tralia’s contribution would naturally fall on
them. A very large proportion of the Em-
pire's naval expenditure is incurred to pro-
tect shipping interests, which are almost
wholly British, and “the colonies,” under
the new regime, would have a considerable
voice in deciding how far this shipping was
worth such costly protection, and how far
the Empire, as a whole, should contribute to
the protection of a mercantile marine, which
doesn’t belong to the Empire as a whole.
The “ colonies ” would want a share in the
profitable swindles attached to army and
navy contracts. They would have an idea
that the profits of naval shipbuilding
should not he wholly confined to
the United Kingdom. They would cer-
tainly propose a division of the pro-
fits of the great silver coinage swindle.
Their votes in the Imperial Parliament might
even turn the scale in favor of a decision not
to steal the next great foreign goldfield that
may be discovered, on the ground that it
wasn’t worth their while to pay, perhaps,
£40,000,000 as their share in the cost of
ejecting the previous owner, seeing that the
Jew of London has always such an infernal
knack of getting ahead of them in the
division of the plunder.
• * * *
So far Australia has cost Britain prac-
tically nothing for defence. It has involved
Britain in no war and has brought it no fresh
enemies. It has helped Britain materially in
one war with which Australia had no concern.
It has provided convenient headquarters for
the fleet that is employed to protect
British shipping in these parts and to
spread the terror of the British flag through
Polynesia. It has paid something towards
the maintenance of this fleet. It has
supplied Britain with about £30,000 a year
of the profits arising from the silver coinage
fraud. And it has received notice from
Joseph Chamberlain to quit the Empire
unless things are placed on a more definite
footing. It remains for Joseph Chamber-
lain to explain—which he hasn’t done yet—
how things should be put on a more definite
footing. Even Joseph of Birmingham would
scarcely be so arrogant a fool as to suggest
seriously that Australia and Canada should
accept the responsibilities of Empire with
none of its powers or dignities. It is his
business, as the person who is moving in the
matter, to formulate a scheme for the fair
division of these powers and dignities. And
that can’t be done without England giving
up all idea of running the Imperial show as
it has done in the Parliament of the mere
three kingdoms. It must consent to be a
factor of diminishing importance, with the
chance of one day being a factor of very
greatly diminished importance. If it isn’t
prepared to face that position, then what is
Joseph of Birmingham driving at ?
The Empty Cradle.
That strenuous man President Roosevelt, of the
United States, has been speaking severely to the
sons and daughters of Uncle Sam about the
declining birth-rate of their mutual country. The
subject is a familiar one in Australia, and in many
other countries as well, but owing to certain side
issues, of which the nigger is the chief, the United
States has got the trouble in a specially bad form.
Between the census year IS9O and the census year
1900 the colored population of the States showed
a greater percentage of increase than the white,
and this despite the fact that the white population
was reinforced by over 5,000,000 immigrants
while the black had to depend almost wholly
on its natural increase. If there had been
neither white nor black immigration the increase
of Ham would have loomed up so enormously
by comparison with the very slow progress or the
practically stationary 7 attitude of Japhet, that the
possibility of the United States becoming a Black
Republic would have begun to look a very serious
possibility indeed. And though there is still a
respectable increase in the white population of the
States by excess of births over deaths, it is mostly
owing to the large families of the newly-imported
immigrants. There is a suspicion that the old
American race—the descendants of the origi-
nal Dutch, Swedish, British, French and Spanish
settlers—the people with a long antiquity of three
centuries to look back upon—are actually dying
out. There was hardly time to develop a real
American type before, according to present
estimates, it began to pass away. Anvhow, the
nigger element represented a little less than 11 per
cent, of the community in 1890, and a good deal
over 12 per cent, in 1900. And it was only the
rush of white immigrants which prevented H am
being a good deal over 13 per cent, of the com-
munity in 1900.
• • • •
Immigration, too, is not what it was. In 1890
much more thau half the immigrants who entered
the United States came from Britain, Germany,
and Scandinavia —the best kind of immigrants
that the world can show. In 1900 two-thirds of
them were Italian, Sclavonic and Magyar—a
sturdy enough race, it is true, yet the people of
almost the poorest aud most ignorant regions of
Europe. Considering the increasing sterility of
the old elements of the population—the descend-
ants of the original British, Dutch, Swedish and
Spanish pioneers; the fact that the immigrants
of to-day are mostly Sclavs, Magyars aud Italians,
and that the natural increase of the white popula-
tion is, to a very large extent, an increase of Sclavs,
Magyars and Italians; and further taking into
account the remarkable growth of the colored
races—summing up all these things President
Roosevelt wants to know if the nigger and the
Dago are to tight out the destiny of the Republic
between them, and if the armies of Cesar and
Sambo are to meet at the new Battle of the Wil-
derness to decide the ownership of the land.
There is some satisfaction in staking a copper or
two on Cesar as against Sambo, but even that
doesn’t alter the fact that the outlook is a serious
one.
• • • •
All over the world the Cesar aud Sambo
problem—the problem which has its basis in the
tendency of the poor and ignorant races to increase
faster than the cultured and affluent—faces the
community. It isn't, after all, so very difficult to
explain why the increase is so v ery one-sided.
To the toil-hardened female who follows the
plough, child-bearing is probably, less painful and
dangerous than to her sister who has been
lapped in comparative luxury ; at all events
she heeds the pain and the danger less. The
woman of luxury regards the child-bearing period
as one of trouble and deprivation, because it cuts
her off from all manner of social enjoyments ; the
other woman has few social enjoyments from
which she can be cut off. The poor and ignorant
woman knows little or nothing of the disastrous
use of preventives. She marries younger, as a
rule, partly because marriage is the most acces-
sible dissipation in the lives of the poor; partly
because it isn’t worth her while, or her prospective
husband’s while, to postpone marriage, as many of
the more affluent classes do, till a certain amount
of wealth has been accumulated, for neither of them
has any real hope of ever accumulating anything.
Religion (or superstition) has a greater hold on the
ignorant, just because they are ignorant, and the
doctrine of increase and multiplication is a strong
element of almost every religion. The well-to-do
limit their families so that there may be sufficient
wealth to divide among them ; it doesn’t matter
to the poor, who have nothing practically to divide,
whether nothing is divided among three or among
ten. Moreover, among the very poor, children
become wage-earners and assets at a very early
age. Also the poor do not look far forward,
simply because their hand-to-mouth existence
destroys the habit of foresight. _ It is no use taking
heed for the morrow if it is impossible to make
provision for the morrow.
m • • •
So long as poverty is not actual starvation
which kills off the rising generation wholesale,
and ignorance is not utter lawlessness and barbar-
ism which reduces the population by constant
strife and slaughter, even the heavy death-rate
that prevails in the insanitary dwellings of the
poor doesn’t press so closely on the birth-rate as
does, in most cases, the smaller death-rate among
the more affluent classes. The future of the earth
seems to lie with the poor and ignorant—or with
the ignorant even when they are not strikingly
poor. Whenever a race grows affluent and cul-
tured and comparatively luxurious, its fertility,
apparently, tends to diminish. How far the pro-
cess may go we can’t say, for this grim world has
seen so very little of general affluence as yet. But
we know that the greatest increase of population
in Europe is in Russia; in Canada it is the in-
crease of the French Canadian; in the United
States it is that of the negro and the Italian,
Sclavonic and Hungarian immigrants; in South
Africa it is that of the Boers —or at least it was in
the Boer’s more ignorant and patriarchal days.
• • • •
There would be no special reason to trouble
about the slow growth of the more civilised
populations if it were not for the indecent haste
of some less civilised populations to grow up and
overshadow the globe. The day must needs come
when the earth can comfortably hold no more.
Then the more educated races, who fancy they
can make a better use of the earth than others,
may possibly decide on the painless extirpation of
certain more backward branches of the human
family, either by some vast scientific process of
preventing their reproduction, or by some more
sudden and off-hand method. It may be decided,
for instance, that the Chinese are superfluous, and
that China is wanted for other purposes ; and the
Congress of Highly Civilised Nations, going care-
fully 7 through the list of races in a congress at the
Hague, may order the clerk of the tribunal to put a
tick against the name of China as one of the com-
munities which is to go. Or, again, when the crush
becomes unpleasantly dense, the Congress of Less
Civilised Nations may do the ticking-off process.
The increase of population constantly hastens the
coming of that possible trouble; yet if the highly
civilised nations delay the calamity by ceasing to
increase they are all the more likely to be among
those ticked off in the day of trouble and in the
hour of consideration. In any case there is a dis-
coursing element about the idea that as soon as
Civilisation has painfully hauled a race on to
the top rung of the ladder it should begin to
wither, and that the hauling process must start
afresh that the future should always lie
with the unlettered individual at the tail
of the procession, and that no sooner is he
caught aud washed and cultivated than a fresh
semi-barbarian should begin to loom up at the
tail in his stead. Anyhow, to return to the origi-
nal subject of discussion, the trouble of the
United States is a solemn lesson on the evils of
admitting colored immigration. If the Teutonic
element in the States falls behind, and the front
place is taken by the Latin or the Sclav, there is
no irreparable harm done. The Latin and the
Sclav are white men, with all the white mans
possibilities, and an Italianised or Russianisen
republic might be as great an influence for good
in the end as a Teutonic one. But a republic in
which the negro element predominated wo«”c'. oe
hopeless, and the multiplication of Sambo is the
one American question to which there seems to
be no answer—at least no answer that could be
published in detail except in a purely scientific
work.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Seal Asset.
Attorney-General Wise, of N.S.W., is reassur-
ing the British money-lender altogether too much.
For a long, long time the uneasy Bcll-Cohen
has had it explained to him with great persistence
that the assets which made his bill quite safe on
the day of payment were our somewhat worn and
not too productive railways, our telegraphs, roads,
sewers, bridges, our dredged rivers and old mili-
tary stores, our thinned forests and shifted sand,
and all manner of other things which Buix-CoHKN
couldn’t seize in case Australia defaulted, and
which wouldn’t pay for removal if they "' en j
seized. And Bcll-Cohen has of late looked
upon these things and remained unconvinced.
Now Attorney-General Wise has addressed him-
self to our creditors in a different streak ot
eloquence, and informed them that “ the real asset
of Australia is Parliament’s power of taxation,
and Bill-Cohex inspects this new security
also with a long nose and a suspicious
eye and refuses to be comforted. Attorney-
General Wise evidently felt that he was strug-
gling with a woefully tough ease when he offered
that final explanation. Parliament, for taxation
purposes, means the Assembly, and the Assembly
in N.S.W. means the people. Therefore, the F®*-
liament’s power to tax the people is simply tn
people’s power to tax themselves —in other words,
their power to pay up if they feel inclined, and 1
they have the money. That is the new security
which Attorney-General Wise offers to the ex-
traneous creditor. It is a very ordinary secuntyj
every country and every individual offers tne
same; it was exactlv the same security tna
Whitaker Wright and B. Fixe and Lbk*?*
Tkkah HooLKYgave to their creditors, aud m
each one of these cases the creditors fell in heavily
Be u. Cohen, very sensibly, remarks tnat to
guarantee is good enough if, when the real pine


°°mea, AustnUia has the money and is willing to
part with it. But he sees that Mr. Wise’s State
would rather charge about £900,000 a year of its
ordinary, unproductive, everyday expenses to
loans than pay taxes or practise economy. He
sees that it would rather have about £200,000 a
year deficit than pay taxes or practise economy.
He also sees that it would rather borrow the trust
funds, and cover its deficit by the issue of Treasury
bills, and do almost anything under the sun than
pay taxes or practise economy. And when he is
assured that his real security lies in the fact
that the people can tax themselves sufficiently
to meet all claims (provided they feel like
doing so and have the money) he naturally
asks when the feeling is likely to manifest itself,
and whether it hasn t become so rusted with dis-
use as to be practically worthless. Also, he pro-
bably remarks that there was exactly the same
security behind Mr. Micawbkr’s IO U. Under
a despotic Government the power of taxation does
amount to something ; you have only to persuade
the despot, and he can hang the people up by the
feet till they pay, if they have anything to pay
with. But when it is a case of the people hanging
themselves up by the feet till they pay it isn’t
exactly the same thing. The question is just
this : If the supply of loan money ceases, N.S.W.,
to take one State as an example, will have either
to make great economies and vastly curtail its
publio works policy as well, or to pay about twice
as much in taxes as it does now, if it is to keep
faith with its creditors. And Bull-Cohen is ask-
ing himself whether people who have been accus-
tomed to so vastly luxurious a form of Government
will, should the trouble come, think the honor of
keeping faith with their creditors worth the
struggle. He isn’t asking the question offensively,
so far as we know, but he is asking it.

“ The Bulletin ” Votes for Bewis.
The Lewis Ministry goe3 before the free and
enlightened Tasmanian publio next month for re-
election or rejection. Its position is a curious
one. It is opposed by a small, but vigorous
knot of democrats for its lack of progres-
sive tendencies, though _it has shown a certain
number of good intentions—quite an unusual
number for Tasmania. It is opposed by a very
large and slothful and retrogressive and flabby
mass of conservatism because it has some ideas
that are too progressive for Tasmania—mainly
because it laid the hand of sacrilege on the Holy
Ark of Fat by passing an Income Tax law
to make good the loss of revenue arising
through the abolition of inter-State customs,
instead of adopting an unflinching policy
of sweating and highway robbery, or else borrow-
ing more money. If these two sets of opponents
succeed in ousting Lewis, then it must be decided
whether that section of the Opposition which is
better than Lewis, or the one that is a darned lot
worse than Lewis, will inherit the fallen poli.
tician’s billet. According to all present appear-
ances, the party that is a darned lot worse than
Lewis will get it, through sheer weight of
numbers. The Opposition consists of a very small
party of reformers, who want the Lewis income-
tax replaced by a graduated land-tax which will
break up the big estates that lie like a
nightmare on Tasmania; and a very large
party of Bloated Fat, which demands that
the income-tax shall be abolished as an
insult to people with a stake in the country,
but which would fall down dead with rage at the
idea of replacing it with anything—a graduated
land-tax suggestion would make this party fall
down dead twice. The great mass of the
Opposition has no declared policy except denun-
ciation of the man who income-taxed Tasmania
instead of sweating the Government employees on
a larger scale than before, or adding the shortage
to the public liabilities ; and the anti-income-tax
howl seems to constitute three-fourths of the
visible Opposition policy. Unfortunately, the
little democratic party in Tasmania, like that of
N.S.W., has never made the squaring of the
public finances a front item of its policy, as it
should have done. It ought to regard Lewis as
at least something of a democrat by reason
of his efforts to stop the financial drift—
but it doesn’t. It ought to urge him to further
efforts in the same direction —but it doesn’t, at
least, not to any great {extent. It ought to de-
nounce every man who proposes to abolish the
new taxation without being able to show exactly
and definitely and to the last penny how the gap
that would be thus created in the national finances
can be made good, as, in that respect at least, a
bloated and disgraceful Tory—but it doesn’t. The
Bulletin has received (and regretfully lighted its
pipe with) voluminous complaints from sundry
respected Tasmanian democrats on account of
its support (half-hearted as the support was)
of the Lewis Ministry. The point where it
differs from these remonstraters is that, in
its opinion, the first principle of democracy
is to square the finances and to check the
policy of drift, thriftlessness and big loans
that is making this country the abject bond-
slave of Bull-Cohen in London. Premier
Lewis has tried to do something in that way, and
promises to do more, and this paper respects him
—in moderation. If he had absolutely committed
himself to the total abolition of the borrowing
policy it would respect him without any modera-
tion at all, and would sooner pay his official salary
itself than see him out of office. He may be Tory
in many respects, but on what ought to be the first
principle of democracy he is at least, according to
all evidence, much more sound than the principal
section of his opponents. If he falls by reason of
the opposition to his income-tax policy it will be
a solemn warning to other politicians to let the
deficit slide. Therefore, The Bulletin votes for
Lewis. It is sorry that he isn’t a better man. It
is still more sorry that many of the other fellows
should be so much worse.

His Honor’s Joke.
The grimmest of grim jokes was that perpetrated
by the grim Judge Boucaut on Osborne, found
guilty at Adelaide of coining, and of shooting the
arresting constable. The cases were taken separ-
ately, and for the shooting the sentence was 21
years and a whipping. “He had always held ”
(said His Honor ; Register report) “that only in
the extremest cases should a life sentence be accom-
panied by a whipping, as to imprison a man for life
and to whip him savored too much of severity He
was, therefore, not going to pass sentence of
imprisonment for life in the present instance, as
he felt it to be his bounden duty to whip the
prisoner.” Sentence of 21 years and 20 lashes
accordingly. Now, four days earlier Boucaut
himself had given this same Osborne a life
sentence on the coining charge, and when His
Honqr went out of his way to drag in his remark
aboui how he didn’t belie v. in combining
flogging and life imprisonment, his laboured
and ghastly joke was irrelevant as well as
premeditated. A judge who can sentence a
man to imprisonment for life, with 21 years
additional imprisonment and a flogging on top
that, isa practical humorist whoshouldn ttry to add
verbal humor to his other horrors and accomplish*
ments. Many a man has lost his opportunity in this
world through not laying to his book ; a good gaol-
nogger, for instance, may neglect hiB flogging
business through trying to be a bad poet, and
between the two he may become nothing at all.
In like manner, a Judge who has a great capacity
tor being horrible shouldn’t try to be funny also ;
between the two he may only contrive to be a
grotesque Aunt Sally in his declining years.
The Victorian Reform Position.
The position in Victoria with regard to constitu-
tional reform still lacks definiteness. Premier
IRVINE has certainly avoided the mistake of dis-
solving the Assembly and making a useless appeal
to the country, and he has restored the reform
clauses which the House of Landlords cut of his
liill, and firmly returned the measure to that blatant
body for further consideration. But he has not
yet stated in any definite manner what ha really
intends to do, in the probable event of the House
ot Landlords cutting out these clauses a second
time. Ihe arrogant Chamber of proud flesh is to
be given the opportunity of repentance ; and it is
just possible that, as with the Probate Bill,
it will decide to repent. At the worst it has, after
all, only the strength of inertia, the strength of
the fat and swollen dog which persists in
remaining in the manger mumbling its toothless
jaws, though its master threatens to kick, because
of a belief that in spite of threats he really won’t
kl .° k - There is always the chance that the dog
will lose its fat complacency and get a terrifying
idea into its head that the man may kick; and
then it would, of course, scramble out. But the
House of Landlords in Victoria has been threat-
ened with the boot so often, and has so often
found the threat a vain one, that the chances of
its taking a reasonable view of the present situa-
tion are rather remote And if it refuses to
budge, what then? Mr. Irvine promises to "take
all constitutional means possible to give clear
effect to the wishes of the country.” That is com-
prehensive enough, and if Irvine realy means it
Reform is as good as accomplished. Two strictly
constitutional” means ha vealready been suggested
by The Bulletin to settle the difficulty. One is to
bribe the Upper House into acquiescence. This
disreputable, but still “constitutional,” method
was adopted to secure the union of England and
Scotland, and to sweep away the old disreputable
legislature of alien oppressers which once called
itself an “Irish” Parliament at Dublin. So it
has precedents. Another constitutional means is
to “ tack on” to the next Appropriation Bill a
few clauses dipping away from the Council all its
powers of obstruction. As far back as the reign
of William of Orange, the British House of
Commons adopted this expedient in dealing with
the House of Lords; and the Lords promptly
caved in, rather than face the ordeal of riot and
revolution which an obstinate attitude on their
part would have ushered in. Does Mr. Irvine
propose that ? Until he explains exactly what he
means by “exhausting every possible con-
stitutional means ” to overcome the House of
Landlords it is impossible to express an opinion as
to his attitude. But if he does show such firmness
as to sweep the obstructing Council aside, The
Bulletin will be inclined to forgive him every-
thing, aye, even Bent.
The Swindled " Bobby.”
The Vio. police force is just now engaged in
quietly discussing its grievance against the Irvine
Government, which involves also a charge of foul
financial play against Governments past and gone.
Fifty years ago (1853), the Vic. police force, hav-
ing then severed all connection with the original
N.S.W. constabulary, started a superannuation-
fund—to be formed by an annual levy of 2 per
cent, upon the pay of all ranks, plus the fines for
misconduct and half of all the penalties recovered
by police action. At that time, the retiring age
for a j‘peeler” was fixed at 60 years, as in
N.S.W In 1870, Parliament appointed a Royal
Commission to inquire into police workings, and
the result of this long-winded inquiry was a new
Act, under which the Goverment took control of
the Superannuation Fund, and fixed the retiring
age at 55 instead of 60. It would seem that the
Fund, on becoming mixed-up with finances in
general, was considerably embezzled by the State,
as trustee, and because the retiring constables
had, so to speak, begun to outrun the pension
provision, another Act of Parliament raised the
superannuation levy to per cent, of salary
instead of 2 per cent. Then the middle-aged
policemen, with only 25 years of service in front
of them, and the young recruits who calculated
their chances of promotion on the 55 years limit
for their elders—they all thought they knew
exactly how they stood. In 1895, however, a new
regulation shifted the retiring age back to 60.
The Force did' not make any particular murmur
about this breach of faith. It put them
on the same terms of service as the police
in other States. They worked away con-
tentedly and ran in the drunk and the hoodlum
as usual. But in 1902 the Superannuation
Fund, owing to the depredations of bygone
Treasurers, was absurdly insufficient to meet
the demands upon it. Chief-Sec. Murray dis-
covered that the Fund’s present income and ex-
penditure was £45,000 a year, and Mr. Murray, as
the representative of a Govt, which is “pledged
to retrenchment” at anybody’s cost, coolly
manipulated an Order-in-Council whereby the
age of retirement for policemen was raised to 65,
an age when men of even specially selected
physique are no longer fit to grapple with an
able-bodied Sykes. By this simple breach
of contract the Chief-Sec. will save £4OOO a year
to the State, he calculates, and for the sake
of that poor but dishonest retrenchment he is
content to have every policeman in the land com-
plaining of bad treatment. Veterans of 60, who
should now be pensioned off, are sulking on their
beats. The juniors’ prospects of promotion have
faded five years further into the distance. The
force begins to wonder whether Parliament will
take a hint from the Psalmist, and fix the working
age limit at three score years and ten. Anything
is possible to a retrenchment policy. Another
Order-in-Council and a new Act may be sprung
upon these myrmidons of an unstable Law.
Meanwhile, they are preparing to bring their case
before Parliament. Their industrious solicitor,
Mr. T. Woolf, has collected a mass of evidence
which ought to make any very young members of
the House blush for the tricks of legislation.
Honest Mistakes.
The Bulletin notices, with pained surprise, that
certain persons in different States of the Common-
wealth have just been submitted to all the in-
dignities of police court prosecution—and in some
cases have been actually committed for trial to a
higher court —on charges of robbing the Post
Office. Yet it surely must be within the know-
ledge of the law officials of the Crown that the
Post Office is now a Federal department, and that
to rob a Federal department is a clear proof
of honesty. It has been established (by the
daily newspapers) that fraudulent forgeries
designed to swindle the oustoms are in-
variably “honest mistakes,” and are made the
subject of police-court prosecutions only because
of the intolerable tyranny of Mr. C. C. Kingston,
and hia deep-laid conspiracy to ruin Sydney and
Melbourne and Adelaide and Brisbane, and to
make this continent a desert without a single
oasis of a shady customs transaction. Frauds on
the Post Office are surely on the same plane. Pro-
secutions for honest mistakes as regards owner-
ship of letters and money-orders are, without a
doubt, proofs that Mr. Drake is unfortunately
fashioning his actions after the arbitrary and
reactionary example of Mr. Kingston. The
Chamber of Commerce should at once give its
attention to the matter. The proper method of
dealing with persons suspected of robbing the
Post Office is to ask them to call and talk over the
matter in a friendly fashion with the Minister,
and the Minister should “shout.”
Again, the Liverpool Tubercle Farm
A recent visit to the Liverpool (N.S.W.) Tubercle
Farm showed that its operations are being ex-
tended. There used to be an average of 100
sufferers undergoing destruction by the phthisis
bacillus ; there are now from 130 to 140. A new
“ ward ” has been opened, and, to accommodate
sufferers, it has been found necessary to locate
some of the cases in the general dormitories,
where indigent inmates not affected by the disease
sleep. So far as possible, the medical superin-
tendent, Dr. Beattie, takes precautions against
this (under the circumstances unavoidable) trans-
gression of an elementary rule of hospital practice,
infecting others ; the phthisis cases are put
among the oldest inmates, and the very old
don’t contract tubercular disease of the lungs.
Whether they are liable to be infected with
tubercular disease of the bowels is a
matter of opinion. In spite of the new
ward, _ and in spite of the location of many
cases in the general dormitories, the unhappy
consumptives are still the victims of overcrowd-
ing. The beds are set four deep in one ward and
between the beds are the dining tables at which
the patients feed. The air in a room housing 40
consumptives, packed so closely that if they were
all sound men there would be grave danger to
their health, is deadly. It cannot, under the best
of circumstances, in the most favorable of weather,
be less than noxious; in the hot summer it is
simply murderous. Of course, under the circum-
stances, the death rate (about 40 per cent, per
annum) is steadily maintained. The authorities
at Liverpool point to several patients who have
survived for a number of years after their ad-
mission to this so-called hospital, as proof that it is
not the death-hole it has been described. But most
of these “tough” cases are those of men who
have taken advantage of the times of improve-
ment which come to most consumptives—even in
such a place as this—to leave the institution for a
while : they have been only intermittently under
the deadly influence of the air in the Liverpool
wards. That influence kills surely enough in
from two to three years—the average death rate
proves that —though exceptional cases may survive
for a very much longer period. It must, in fair-
ness, be admitted that there is no fault to find
with the personal administration at the Liverpool
Tubercle Farm. The matron and the nurses (too
few of them in the day and none at night) do their
best; the medical superintendent, Dr. Beattie,
is an exceptionally kind-hearted man, and
treats the patients with what is, under the
conditions, the greatest possible skill and gener-
osity. It is the system that is at fault—
the crowding of 140 consumptives into the wards
of an old men’s asylum, in an utterly unsuitable
building,"without any park-grounds whatsoever,
by the side of an almost stagnant river, which for
some hours of the day pollutes the air with a
noisome stench—it is that which is murderous.
Liverpool gets, as a rule, “bad cases” (no con-
sumptive who can possibly avoid it goes there);
and among these patients, whatever their treat-
ment, there would be a high death-rate. But, in
view of the fact that an English sanitorium for
consumptives can record 80 per cent, of cases in
which the disease was arrested or cured, the
Liverpool figures are appalling—4o per cent, of
deaths per annum and no cures ! The Govern-
ment which permits this to continue is morally
indictable for the slaying of the unfortunates who
cough out their lives in the Liverpool wards; a
lethal chamber for poor phthisis oases would be
really less cold-blooded.

A Civil Service Clerk.
A regent action against the Victorian Railways
Department by an ex-chief clerk goes to show that
the Government is—or at least was—a much more
desirable employer than a bank or an insurance
company as far as clerical workers are concerned.
Plaintiff entered the department a 3 shorthand
writer at the end of 1881. In ’B4 he was promoted,
and in ’93 he became chief clerk at a salary of
£5OO a year. In ’96 the new Chief Commissioner
(Mathieson) “appeared to be dissatisfied with
him,” and changed his billet. The employee
evidently didn't please boss Mathieson. His
application for three months’ leave of absence was
refused. He again applied for leave, on the
ground of ill-health. Finally, in July, ’97 —•
after less than 16 years of service on the
railways—he got a few weeks leave of absence
and then, as he refused to retire, the department
ceased paying his salary into the bank. An
emissary from the Commissioner advised him to
retire, in which case he might have been pen-
sioned. Instead of coming to a final settlement
with Mathieson in ’97, the invalid stuck to his
alleged “rights” and after an absence of 20
months proposed to resume work as before. Then
the department told him to consider himself dis-
charged from the Ist January, ’9B, on a gratuity
of £967. Plaintiff claims that he is still an em-
ployee of the Railways, “entitled to £5OO a
year salary, and to £1291 for arrears of salary;
or alternately that he is entitled to a super-
annuation allowance from the time when he
was induced, by stress of circumstances, to
accept the gratuity aforesaid.” An ordinary
city clerk, whose position depends upon the
approval of one boss, and who is bound to
lose his position if the business comes to grief, can’t
be expected to have any sympathy with a £5OO a
year clerk who refuses to be retired on a pension
by a disapproving boss. If the Civil Service
clerk is entitled to his “rights,” it is clear that
the private service clerk must be a deeply wronged
individual. Also the private employee is a man
to be pitied, in so farthat he runs the risk of pos-
sible poverty in his old age. No man gets his
“rights” in tins world unless his income is guaran-
teed to increase steadily until he is 60, and his
declining years are soothed by a liberal super-
annuation allowance.
The Whitewashing of Mr. M'Kenzie.
On the motion of Premier Irvine, the Victorian
Assembly has dismissed the troublesome M'Kenzie
scandal with a lame and non-committal device. It
accepted the report of the select committee,
which found MKenzie guilty of maladministra-
tion—and inflioted no punishment. The House
did not even put itself right with the country by
censuring M'Kenzie, or by dismissing from office
the Premier who had made himself constitution-
ally responsible for M‘Kenzie’B honesty. It has
been thus established as a working political prin-
ciple in Victoria that a Minister of the Crown
may use the great trust reposed in him to further
his own interests at the expense of the people, with-
out incurring any other risk on being found out
than[that of an interruption of his nefarious actions.
It is a vicious precedent to set up. Applying the
same system of ethics to other branches of wrong-
doing, it would have to be admitted that the bank-
robber and the pickpocket should not be interfered
with if, having been discovered at work and re-
monstrated with, they agreed to desist from the
enterprise in hand and handed in their resignations
as bank-robbers or pickpockets respectively.
Australia needs to be particularly careful in safe-
guarding political honesty. Without having, as
in England, the chance to drawits politicians from
a large class which is, by circumstances and by
training, not easily amenable to ordinary tempta-
tions of dishonesty, it “sweats” its public
men shamefully. The living to be gained
by a devotion to politics is a dis-
gustingly poor one; and the probabilities of
politicians adding to their rightful incomes by sur-
reptitious and dishonest means is accordingly
great. If, then, abuse of political trust is con-
doned when discovered, the ohances of corruption
must become disquietingly numerous. To have
made a stern example of squatter M‘Kenzie was
the obvious duty of the Victorian Parliament; but
the squatter and the landowner and the Kyabram
“reformer” who repudiated his liability for irri-
gation expenditure are the three great props of
the Ministry, and a prop must be spoken to gently
in these hard, scarce times. v .
Letter from a surprised Victorian pressman
Premier Irvins had a strange and unexpected lapse
into Liberalism when dealing with the amendments made
in the Constitution Reform Bill by the Legislative
Council. As the Bill was passed through the Assembly
it was an odd mixture of Toryism and Liberalism. It
cam* back from the Council with the Tory elements
strengthened, and absolutely devoid of every one of its
original Liberal provisions. If Irvinb had lived up to his
past history as a good Tory he would have meekly
accepted the dictation of the 48 Rich Landlords. All his
Tory friends expected him to do that. But he came
down with a bold, bad proposition to reject nearly every
one of the Council’s amendments. It caused the many
Tory gentlemen who have been sitting so solidly
behind him to shudder. Madden, M’Outciieon, Down-
ward, Joseph Harris, Swinburne, and others
huddled themselves into the Ministerial corner while
Irvine was speaking, and displayed the feelings
of a man having a large molar drawn. Irvine not
only moved for the rejection of the Council’s amend-
ments, but he made an irresistible attack upon that
Chamber. He charged it deliberately with treating the
mandate of the country with “ ignominy and contempt,"
and was even rude enough to say that it was a non-
representative body—that it did not even represent the
opinions of its own constituents. Madden M'Cutchbon
and co. could scarcely keep their seats when these dread-
ful remarks were made. They held their breath, expect-
ing every minute to see him struck dead. Nothing
happened, and it is a fair assumption that Madden,
M’Cutcheon and co. ended the night as atheists. If there
is one thing more than another calculated to make Tories
unbelievers, it is the fact that the fate of Ananias and
Sapphira is not instantly meted out to people
who talk disrespectfully of the Legislative Coun-
cil. Irvine’s shockingly irreverent language did not
stop there. He asserted that the Council was a delusion
and a sham, in as much as it was “incapable of perform-
ing the most useful and important duties entrusted to it
—those of revision and review.” When a man with a
glorious Tory past such as Irvine’s says things like these
about the House of RichLandlordshe ought to be believed.
Being so long on the inside running he certainly should
know, and throughout his speech Irvine gave one the
impression of knowing a good deal. Even then he was
not finished. He heaped derision on the 48 Landlords
for not having had the courage to get into the
open and protest against the Reform Bill outside
their own Chamber. Indeed, in this respect, he
hinted that they were a set of cowards. And,
last of all, Irvine actually sneered at the Council as the
“ bulwark of the Constitution and the liberties of the
people.” He twitted that Chamber with having passed
all those measures now on the statue-book which are said
to have caused the downfall of the State. Each of his
statements was proved in a cold-blooded fashion before
he proceeded to the next, and when he sat down Irvine
looked as good a Liberal as any man in the House. Can
it be that the Irvine has just entered upon the chrysalis
stage—that he has just shed the grubby covering of the
Tory and taken on the bright new form of the democrat.
If his present attitude towards the Council proves per-
manent, if he does not sneak back to his mouldy Tory
shape when the real fight begins, the present writer will
take back most of the hard things he has thought of
Irvinb and all the bad things he has written of him.
«.
A recent debate in the Melbourne Trades Hall
emphasises the necessity for securing Federal con-
trol of industrial legislation. It was alleged that
a big tobacco combine, now operating in Aus-
tralia, had transferred a great deal of its cigar-
making business to Adelaide, where it was pos-
sible to employ cheap girl labor. In Victoria the
operations of the Factories Act, in N.S.W. of the
Arbitration Act, made cheap labor production
impossible. So the work went to S.A. and the
product went free all over the Commonwealth.
Under such a system the States witli humane
industrial legislation are penalised and the benefits
of Protection abrogated. There must be Federal
labor legislation to enforce identical wage condi-
tions in all States.
«
Adelaide rag merchant, C. H. Goode, of the
cherubic countenance, the other day got very
angry about Kingston’s administration of the
Customs Department. At a farewell to another
rag merchant, David Murray, cherubic Goode
cried indignantly: “Under the present brutal
regime of an ex-South Australian, Mr. Murray
and myself have been pulled with drunks and
disorderlies to the police court and charged with
crimes of which we were not guilty. I hope there
will soon be a change, because it i 3 demoralising
to the country when respectable men are brought
up and charged along with drunks and dis-
orderlies.” This is a fearful outburst from a
mild-looking little man like Goode, who is boss
elder at the North Adelaide Baptist Church.
Goode might have been absolutely innocent
of the horrible crimes with which he was charged
by the brute Kingston, but judging from the
number of deliberate frauds which Kingston has
managed to sheet home on “highly respectable
traders ” all over the Commonwealth within the
last year or so, it would be unwise to take any
notice of the so-called respectability of any indi-
vidual, or of any firm of merchants or individuals.
It is no doubt a terrible thing to have the black-
coated, trim, respectable Baptist person hauled
up with drunks and disorderlies; but, after all,
drunks are often much smaller criminals than
importers.
