“Sweet Nell of Old Drury” is doing
a revival, which promises to be all too brief,
at Sydney Royal. And “ Nell ” should be
able to stand a good many revivals, provided
Nellie Stewart is there to represent the orange-
maiden of the old muddy bye-street, and the
beetle-browed King is up to his present standard.
It is a play with no particular ending, and its
morals are strangely and horribly gone wrongs
but there is a fascination about it that would
assuredly not be there if its morals went right.
Nell Gwynne did a vast amount of good with the
money that the King gave her for being bad, the
King having generally stolen the money in
the first place. They make a handsome and
romantic pair when he comes to her house
worried with thinking what he can steal
next, and she bangs the wages of iniquity in the
shape of the harpsichord to charm away his load
of care. When Jeffreys invades Nell Gwynne s
house in search of the good but persecuted young
baronet, and finds the bad King conoealed there,
and is thus diverted from his pursuit, the fact
must be admitted that if Mistress Gwynne had
been a lady with scruples as to the sort of king
she kept in her house, Charles wouldn’t have been
there, and Jeffreys wouldn’t have been diverted.
If Charles had been a good king he would never
have had that dazzling smile which looks as if he
had swallowed a lighted lamp ; it was only by
the persistent showing of his magnificent front
teeth to a long series of new girls that such a smile
could have been acquired. A good King Charles
would have looked like Barrett’s Alfred of Engle-
land, and a king who looks like that is better
dead. Charles the Joyous, with his three Totties,
and his sins, and his irresponsibleness, and his dog
and his tall boots and his strange swears is poetry.
A better Charles would certainly have been a
brown frump and possibly a Sunday-school teacher.
The new performance is, if possible, better
than the old one. It is the identical company in
all essentials: Nell, and the King, and the
Judge with the clashing laugh and the caver-
nous mouth are in the same capable hands as
before, and all three—Miss Stewart, Harcourt
Beattv and Albert Grau—seem to have added
a finishing touch here and there to their
old fine performances. The fluffy dog is just
the philosophic animal he was before. A
new man—Vernon Steel—plays the persecuted
baronet, and plays him, on the whole, better than
his predecessor. It is, unfortunately, a very
brief revival, for “ Zaza ” is to present a different
and more explosive phase of immorality shortly,
so if there are any people in Sydney who have not
yet seen Charles affably borrowing his subjects’
money, they are hereby reminded that time is
short and kings are only temporary.
** * *
“The Wrong Mr. Wright” at Sydney
Palace proves itself to be the right sort of play for
the public, judging by the patronage it has
attracted. Mr. Wright himself (the gentleman
who was wrong) is a gay and debonnair old dog,
who is inv‘ bly young in heart, though his
G. H. Reid hair suggests that he has reached the
age when stodginess is excusable; and there is
always a great amount of attractiveness in the
spectacle of a gentleman in advanced middle age
being “a bit of a lad.” It makes the elderly
hope, and gives renewed confidence totbosewhoare
not so voung as they were. The Palace show is
therefore popular with the somewhat bald classes
who deliver weighty opinions in public vehicles ;
and with ladies in whose hearts there is generally
hidden the hope that man’s wickedness will
survive to the sixties. When Mr. Wright retires,
another farce new to Sydney is promised.
** * *
Bland Holt’s “Cotton King” has not
made so favorable an impression on Sydney as his
“Drought.” The descent of the lift is somehow
or other a less exciting episode on the stage than
it reads in cold print, aud the audience feels that
it could see a good many people rescued just in
time from that invention without feeling duly
excited. Probably Poe’s hair-raising anecdote of
the man who was about to be flattened between
walls that closed in on him wouldn’t look much on
the stage. In the last act the villain’s numerous
iniquities are found out all in a bunch, as usual,
to the accompaniment of peaceful, green scenery
and the goodwill of the happy villagers, who, as
usual, leave their work and their babies and pre-
paring the old man’s dinner, to be present at the
psychological moment.
*» * *
“ Widow O’Brien ” goes cheerfully enough
on its way at Sydney Criterion, kept up by
Sheridan mostly in a barrel-like skirt with extra-
ordinary lattice-work adornment round it. The
interpolated man who sings “ Surely Not ” and
makes observations on King Edward rather
improves on acquaintance, and Miss Celia Mavis,
whose voice is getting lamentably shrieky, makes
something of a successful effort in “ The Love-lorn
Lily.” Carden’s song,. “ Nellie McNally,” or
words adjacent thereto, is not a well-chosen ditty :
but the rest of the performance is much as usual.
The airy dancing of the brief-skirted stewardesses
has been varied once or twice of late through a
too active damsel accidentally falling into a pray-
ing attitude through excess of vigor. “The
Widow” will be succeeded on Saturday by
another old drama, “ When the Lamps are
Lighted,” wherein Johnny Sheridan plays six
characters.
** * *
Amateur manager Philip Lytton had a
field night at Sydney Royal on Friday when
“The Players” gave a first-class rendering of
Muskerry’* grimly natural tragedy “The Iron-
THE ENLIGHTENMENT OF THE FIJI HEATHEN.
master.” Every part of the house was filled with
a crowd in evening dress, and the rustle of fans
made a sound like unto the rustle of a cornfield, and
the place looked like a vast garden of many-colored
poppies waving in a gale. Philip Lvtton s Players
have caught on in Sydney. "The Ironmaster' is
the story of a French young lady of high degree
and manv francs who is engaged to the Duke de
Bligny. The Duke is a mighty gambler, and when
he learns that Clare de Beaulieu has lost her
shekels in a law-suit he prepares to sell himself
and all his nobility to the daughter of Pontius
P. Pedgift, the ' American millionaire who
had made his cash out of Sunshine Soap.
Muskerry evidently had a fierce and bitter hatred
of the American plutocrat, and made Pedgift a
weird combination of meanness, social ambition,
and ghastly ignorance of the proprieties. Finding
she is jilted.' Clare de Beaulieu makes haste to
accept the Ironmaster. Philippe Derblay, who has
long worshipped her from afar, and mames him
in short order. Then, on the bridal night, she
orders him from the room, and offers him the for-
tune she does not know she has lost as compensa-
tion. The grizzled Ironmaster goes out with
bitterness in his heart, and plans how to break his
wife’s spirit. He is a terribly serious, man, that
Ironmaster. Meanwhile the Duke, having secured
the soap millions, grows utterly sick of the parvenu
duchess, and comes back to make love to the Iron-
master’s wife, and that inane man of metal helps the
Duke in his nefarious design all he knows how,
by rejecting the advances of the wife, who has
learned to love him. The Ironmaster .flirts with
the Duchess, and the Ironmaster’s wife rejects
the advances of the perfidious Duke. She has an
explanation with tne Duchess and orders her to
leave her house. The Duchess refuses, the hus-
bands are drawn into the row, a duel is arranged,
and when they meet they are to fire at “six. '
The Duke fires at “four,” just as the Ironmasters
wife rushes in to stop the duel. She stops the
Duke’s bullet instead, and the curtain comes down
with a rush over a ruined Duke, a broken-hearted
husband and a dying wife.
* • • •
The acting was good throughout, but
Mrs. Lytton and Owen Bancroft excelled them-
selves in the bridal chamber scene in the second
act. As the Duke, Lincoln Jones went, to the
dogs in a most natural way. W. J. Curtis made
the Murkan millionaire all that author could have
desired ; and Misses Booth, Lonsdale and others
did good work. The orchestra was not up to the
standard of the players, and may consider itself a
drawback, a blot and a back number, or something
thereabouts,

Sydney Tivoli continues to present a pro-
gramme of even (almost monotonous) merit, to
which John Coleman, Wallace Brownlow, G. W.
Hunter, Mark Anthony, Maccann and the Mon-
troses are the most popular contributors. On
Saturday, it is understood, the game of pushball
will begin to rage, and some other new features
will be introduced. The Tivoli just now pines for
new features.
** * *
“ The Fortune Teller,” lately discussed at
length in this journal as a Sydney production, is
now happening at Melbourne Princess’s. It began
to happen on Saturday night, and the local public
rushed the show with enthusiasm. "The Fortune
Teller,” in its loudest orchestral moments is grand
opera, especially in the finale of the second act.
and the story belongs to the old opera-bouffe
order of narrative that was popular before W.
S. Gilbert brought satire into the business. Per-
haps the music has a distinctly Hungarian twang.
Anyway, it is not French and sparkling, or
German and dreamy, or rich Italian, or Bohemian
Girlish, and as the scene is laid at Buda-Pesth, the
title of “Romantic Hungarian Comic Opera”
fits the case fairly well. The romance of the plot
might be more interesting under brighter
acting conditions; the music of the principal
parts would undoubtedly have more charm for the
average citizen if the voices were sweeter. Very
little of the best music in " The Fortune Teller ”
is superficially tuneful or "sugary.” to use the
scornful term of the Wagnerites. There are ex-
ceptions, notably the gipsy basso’s serenade in the
second act; but, as a rule, the airs—or, rather,
the effects of the airs—are what the singers make
them. That is to say, the melodies of composer
Victor Herbert are tuneful or otherwise according
to the quality of the voices that produce them.
• • • •
Neither Madame Slapoffski nor Lempnere
Pringle sang charmingly on Saturday night.
Madame’s voice came as a disappointment, for it
had apparently lost color and vivacity since she
was heard in “ Mignon ” two or three years ago.
Pringle was a greater disappointment than the
Srima-donna. His voice had a depressing effect.
Iverything it touched was turned to dullness. He
was not recognisable as the basso who sang
Mephisto to Ella Russell’s Marguerite. Looking
at Pringle’s tame performance of the angry brown
Gipsy, and Madame Slapoffski’s earnest endeavors
to be gay and sprightly, a lot of people must have
turned their thoughts back to the original Emilie
Melville and big Bill Verdi. “The Fortune-
Teller ” needs two such artists as they were.
m• 9 • •

Polish count, looks rather like a lady-barber, and
Foster Courtenay is an aged father in an un-
accountable nigger-minstrel hat. The orchestra
chorus and scenery are the mam strength of the
attraction, judging from Saturday’s results. The
spectacular musical climax of the 2nd act is
something out of the ordinary way of comic

• At Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) the revived
‘ ‘ Geisha ” is putting in time until “ The Emerald
Isle” comes along. Arguments for and against
“The Geisha" are pretty well known in Mel-
bourne and they carry about the same weight as
of yore. The music is good of its kind, but
the whole of it was written by one Jones,
whose style grows monotonous after the tenth
ballad. Also the ditties have a consider-
able sameness and the poet rhymes Geisha
with “Asia” at the slightest provocation
But if the lyrics are puerile, the prose portion of
the book is comparatively smart and the insane
conduct'of a young person named Molly threatens
to make everybody blush for the consequences,
which is a pleasing threat, anyhow. Finally
Mimosa San extricates Molly from her awful
predicament by a hollow device, and the gaudy
entertainment stops short. Take it for all in all,
“ The Geisha ” is as well worth revival as any of
the kindred frivolities that have come out here
with a London reputation. There was a gap to
be filled, and “the Queen of the # Geisha-the
Geisha” (otherwise “the Jewel of Asia—of Asia )
fills the vacuum picturesquely.
• • • ■
The Williamson Opera Co. doesn’t 6hine
to special advantage in this instance. Miss
Young is a robust mezzo Mimosa, whereas she
ought to be a small soprano San. Miss Came
Moore makes too many eyes, and Maud Chetwynd
is not a bit like a French interpretess in a heathen
tea-shop. And there are others who have been
more happily situated than now, but as they will
soon get situations in another opera, the present
matter is of no consequence.

Rev Alf. Woods, the hot-gospeller, is
still chasing Glory Quayle for the good of her soul
at Melb. Bijou. This is the second week of The
Christian.” and the first week after Shrove Tues-
day, which is the Feast of Pancakes and the full-
ness thereof. When the righteous Woods turneth
away from his morality and droppetb into the
embrace of a fine, red-haired Manx girl, who
crieth “ Kiss me ! ” then is it clear to the meanest
understanding that the shepherd ought to shut up
shop and take the goods the gods have provided
for him. But instead of staying with Glory
when he has caught her, the misguided clergy-
man goes looking for trouble, and trouble be-
falls him on the back of his head, likewise
on his boko. Then he marries the girl and dies,
whereas he might have married her and lived.
Rev. Alf. Woods, alias John Storm, is about the
silliest hero on record. If the drama bad a
moderate sense of humor it would carry on the
narrative after the parson’s decease, and show
how his widow became engaged to Mr. Drake on
their way back from the funeral.

condemns Woods to dwell in solitude on the sad
shore.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of 10.|in. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*
• • * *
It is not generally known that in the treatment
of ore as much of the valuable minerals is often lost in
the tailings as is saved by the appliances. This is due
in most instances to the inefficient machines used and
the difficulty in saving the fines and slimes. The best
machine to treat the latter classes are Luhrig Vanners.
These machines are in successful daily use in the principal
gold, galena, copper and tin mines in Australia and
Tasmania, proving their superiority over other so-called
slime machines. Messrs W. & J. Lempriere, 506 Little
Collins-street, Melbourne, are the general agents, and full
particulars can be obtained from them.*
• • • *
A. Kozmmsky, 106 Queen-st., Melbourne,
having returned from England, is now prepared to
purchase paying mines, and mining properties (not
merely surface shows), situated in any part of
Australasia requiring further capital for development and
machinery. Only genuine and legitimate ventures,
capable of bearing the strictest examinations will be
entertained.*

• * * *
The North Gippsland Proprietary Company,
Grant, has been engaged for many months past driving
a long tunnel in very hard country. The tunnel is now
some 2500 ft. in length. The last 1500 feet has been
driven with the aid of the Gardner Electric Rock Drill
which has given the manager every satisfaction ; only one
drill has been in use and it is still working in as good
condition as when first started. About 35 feet of drilling
is done by it every shift, so the installation of the Electric
Drill must be regarded as in every way successful. The
drill requires less than two horse power to drive
it as against a.t least ten horse power required for
an air driven drill doing the same work, besides dispensing
with compressor, receivers and air pipes. The fact that
this Electric Drill has been working so satisfactorily in
one of the most inaccessible parts of Victoria is sufficient
proof that the plant is not liable to derangement. The
sole agents for the Gardner Electric Drill are the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne, who will
be pleased to answer all enquiries.*

The net earnings would have been larger but for the fact

The ordinary earnings (£97,931) represent £7 14s. sd. per

at the commencement of the half-year. Deposits stand
at £15,261,294, while the reserve of and liquid
securities is enormous, amounting in all to over
£6,700,000.
Some years ago, when the Union Bank made an
overhaul of it* securities, it was decided that a writing-
off of £250,000 would probably be necessary in order to
provide for all known losses. This sum was not definitely
written off at the time, but at various times £200,000 has
been provided to meet these losses, in addition to
£lO,OOO for the purpose of writing down the bank’s office
property account. This makes £210,000, in all, written
off since 1899, and if the original estimate holds good,
there now remains only £50,000 to be provided. Another
year should do this, without interfering in any way with
the 8 per cent, dividend.

Balmain New Ferry Co. has had very moderate luck since
it bought (in July, 1900) the lumbering old paddle-arks of
the original Balmain Ferry Co. and extended its connec-
tion to Mort’s Dock.
* » * »
Victoria has £5,000,000 of old per cent, loans
to renew at the end of this year, and nearly £500,000 of 4
per cent, loans also. They might be renewed even now
at a comparatively cheap rate on the Federal credit, but
Premier Irvine, apparently for the mere preservation of
his own small State dignity, says “ We are going to look
after the matter ourselves,” and the renewal of that big
loan on the State’s dilapidated credit promises to be a
terribly expensive job. Yet there is one way by which it
might, even in those bad times, be done on reasonable
terms. Let Irvine announce, in the prospectus of the
renewal loan, that £245,000 a year, being about the
amount of the interest payable on the old loans, is set
aside for the service of the new loan, and that the £50,000
a year or so which will remain after paying interest will
be deposited with the Bank of England (where Shiels
can’t get at it) as a sinking fund. That would do more to
rehabilitate Victoria’s credit than all the purple streaks
of eloquence that Treasurer William could get off in a
year.

This co. pays only 3 per cent, interest to its depositors,
cut even at that low rate it keeps drifting steadily to
leeward. There is not much capital still intact, even on
paper, to meet further losses, and while the visible
deficit grows rapidly, the progress made at reducing the
liabilities isn't great. On 31st January, 1896, the co.
owed £108,847 on deposits, overdraft and amounts due
on buildings. At 31st January, 1903, it owed, when its
ba “* credit balance was deducted from its liabilities,
£94,241. A reduction of £14,606 in liabilities in seven
years isn t much of a set-off against an increase of £34,730
m the visible deficit; and, on paper at all events, the
situation is growing steadily worse.
* • • •
The Maoriland Government, or rather, Premier
Seddon, begins to seriously reconsider the State’s position
as regards the Bank of New Zealand. When Private
Management ran that big institution so heavily on the
rocks, the State took control and advanced £2,500,000 to
pull the concern through. It advanced this money at
exactly the interest that the State itself had to pay. It
made no profit out of the deal; and gave its guarantee,
and took an enormous risk, for absolutely nothing. At a
time when the B.N.Z. couldn’t have sold a new issue of
shares to any private capitalist the State took them up
without even stipulating that it was to get anything at all
for saving the private shareholders from ruin. It didn’t
even clearly stipulate that it wasn’t to be kicked out
with ingratitude as soon as it had finished saving the
old proprietors from the consequences of their own
mismanagement. But now that the B.N.Z. is becoming
a profitable institution again under State management,
and the excitement has had time to calm down, the
position is being reconsidered in a more sober fashion.
It is variously proposed that the State should insist on
the £2,000,000 it still has in the bank being re-
garded as permanent capital instead of temporary
aid, and should thus be permanently the chief
partner in the institution. Another idea is that the
State should buy up the institution bodily. Still
a third is that the B.N.Z. should be allowed to repay the
Government advances when it is able to do so, but that
the State should retain control as an unpaid trustee for
the shareholders in order to prevent any repetition of the
old disasters. Probably the best suggestion of all would
be for the State to buy up the shareholders’ interest in
the business, and make it a State Bank pure and simple.
Certainly the present position is very indefinite and un-
satisfactory. Its indefiniteness arose from the fact that
the Bank of New Zealand rushed the Government with
a demand for aid within 24 hours, and in the alarm lest
the institution should close its doors next morning and
fill Maoriland with panic the State’s interests were very
little considered at the moment.

• * • *
The Bulletin receives an anonymous leaflet,
with no imprint, about “ the fall in the A.M.P. Society’s
bonuses.” Presumably it is the work of one of the A.M.P.'s
business rivals, and in a time when all bonuses are falling,
the malice is more obvious than the point. The Bulletin
trusts that the person who issued the document will be
fined the usual amount, with the alternative of three
months, for circulating literature with no imprint
thereon.

• * • *
Queensland Meat Export Co. (Premier Philp is
one of its directors, a Cabinet colleague another, and the
shareholders are on the whole very “ political ”) owes the
State £26,647, balance of a loan of nearly £40,000, granted
by the Meat and Dairy Board to see the co. through its
struggling days. Now the Q.M.E. Co. pays a2O per cent,
dividend, but it is in no hurry to pay back its liability to
the State. And Queensland people nod significantly.
The inevitable row ’twixt local authorities and
the Brisbane Tram Co. is just coming round the corner.
The South Brisbane Municipal Council wrote to the co."
complaining of the overlapping of sectional charges on a
new line, and the co. replied in its best style that it
would see the council further before it altered
to suit the people’s representatives, and further said that 5
in the future, it intended to adopt a Tnove consevvative
policy. That probably means that, having chased all
competitors from the field, it will proceed to keep a still
keener eye on the profits. This is bad enough, but a
worse danger exists. Recent electrical experiments made
by competent men prove that electrolytic action is begin-
ning to take place ’twixt the ground currents of the tram
co. and the water and gas pipes laid in the main streets.
This means that the pipes will have to be shifted at
enormous expense (the tram co. isn’t doing any shifting)
or some fine day a big soakage or explosion may wake the
city fathers from their aldermanic maunderings. Modern
electricians place the margin of safety between return
current and metal piping at 10ft., and in some Brisbane
cases the distance is about 10 inchest. The Adelaide rate-
payers who supinely allowed the Snow tramway grab
should paste this in their hat for future reference
• • • •
The jump in lead past £l2 has set silver stocks
moving and tongues discuss the chances of the re-starting
of the nailed-up B.H. mines. It looks, though, as if the
market has a good way to go yet before some of them
can hope for profits. The Junction North shut up at
£l5, Junction and North at £l2 Bs. 9d., British at £l2 ss.
Block 14 at £lO 7s. 6d. These figures cannot, however*
fairly stand as they are. Junction North has had moder-
ate developments underground since then ; probably it
could run for a while at £l3 10s. Junction, when it
stopped, couldn’t produce at £l6 ; it has cut £3 off that
North is in very little better position than it was!
It has improved its plant; but, then, silver has
slumped 4d. an ounce, and that is about a fair set-off.
British could not have continued to produce at £l2 ss.’
but could do so now, though with very little to spare.’
What Block 14 can do is very doubtful; it certainly hung
on till lead got to £lO 7s. 6d., but it had been losing
money by the shovelful for 12 months, though how its
mining and ore-dealing respectively panned out its
accounts don’t tell. As for the mines that are not shut
up, a £1 lead rise means to the B.H. Prop, ahout £24,000
a half-year ; to the South, about £BOOO ; to the Sulphide
about £ll,OOO ; and to Block 10, £6OOO. ’
* • • •
The mean of the annual average prices of lead
for 25 years is a small fraction under £l3, and for the past
10 years (this period including the two worst lead slumps
on record), £l2 6s. In the boom of ’93-1900, when the
price got close up to £lB, much more metal was, no doubt
produced than could be absorbed, and a big but uni
certain quantity went into stock ; in two years a large
hole has most likely been made in this, and upon how
big that hole is mainly depends the future of the market.
Australians interested in the business have been alarmed
to see that the American output has been about main-
tained in spite of the slump ; but the fact is there hasn’t
been any slump to speak of in the U.S.—the tariff
steadied the market. There isn’t, in fact, any sign at all
of’Murkan producers being able to “flood the market”
at late European prices ; and altogether the lead outlook,
though statistics have not been particularly favorable is
a bit better than fair. Unfortunately, though, for
Broken Hill, the prospects of the silver market are poor
The B.H. Prop., for instance, could, in the early nineties*
have worked at a profit if lead had been nothing a ton •
now, with silver at Is. lOd. instead of 45., even that
great co. must get £lO for its lead to make tucker.
* • * *
An inspection of the Crowl Creek mine, Shuttle-
ton, near Nymagee (N.S.W.), which writer made last
week, fully confirms the good reports which had been re-
ceived from disinterested parties. The mine presents
appearances which justify the assumption that it will
become one of the large copper producers of N.S. W. It is
obvious that during the term of the working option the
policy of the purchasing syndicate was to root out every
ounce of high grade ore, and progressive development
work was neglected. This is now being proceeded
with on legitimate lines. Where the lode was
touched at the lowest point it was 20ft wide
of solid yellow ore, averaging 22 per cent.*
A crosscut is now being driven 40ft. lower down which
will intersect the formation 50ft. south of the winze
This drive should be into the lode within a few days, and",
on indications, it will be surprising indeed if a strong
body of ore is not found. Co. intends to adopt reverb,
system of smelting, and tenders have been invited for
the manufacture of the necessary bricks. It is stated
locally that it is proposed to erect the furnaces near a
waterhole some two miles distant from the mine. This
will be mistaken policy. Better to cart a ton of water to
100 tons of ore at the mouth of the mine than to cart 100
tons of ore to 1 ton of water two miles awav.
••* J •
Much activity prevails at Cobar consequent on
recent rains having given the Co.’s full supplies of water.
Great improvements have been made in the surface
arrangements of the big copper mine since G. H.
Blakemore took charge, and it requiies no expert to see
that there is still room for vast alterations having
efficiency and economy in view. The new furnace
erected by Mr. Blakemore is putting through close upon
300 tons of ore per day, or nearly four times as much as
any one of the old furnaces was capable of treating. It
ia stated that the mine is looking: wonderfully well in the
lower levels, but in view of the negotiations for the
purchase of the mine no official information is available
on this or other points.
* • • •
Cobar Gold Mines battery is in full swing.
Local officials are as dumb as oysters, but a report comes
from London that much improved prospects have been
met with lately. Australian shareholders in thisco. have
a serious grievance. They are denied information as to
the yields or progress of work until reports have passed
through London office and that office transmits the in-
formation to Australia, where it arrives nearly three
months afterdate. The initial mistake made in locating
the big battery is now more than self-evident. Co. is
hemmed in on all sides with stacks of tailings, and a long
tramway has had to be constructed to remove the worth-
less material as produced. The expenses of handling
and re-handling must be heavy and in themselves preclude
any possibility of large profit being made unless the
grade of ore becomes higher. Had the battery been
placed on top of Fort Bourke Hill there would have been
every natural facility for the gravitation of sand and
slimes on the eastern slope without any interference with
miners’ residences. As things are now there are several
miners’ homes which are walled round with tailings, and
lovely habitations they must be to live in on a windy
day.. One advantage may be that the prevailing dust
provides ample thickening for soup. Co. is apparently
making no effort to get down with the shaft
to prove the cupriferous zone. Whenever it makes
up its mind to do so, it will be well rewarded, as it
will find that it has one of the most valuable gold-copper
mines in the State. Shareholders make no complaint
about this dilatoriness, and the suggestion is made that
certain persons who hold a large number of the shares
are content to lie low in the hope that ultimately they
will get the whole show into their hands.
* * * *
Chesney Cobar is busily engaged in pumping
water which is running to waste. Why it is not utilised
by some of the other mines is incomprehensible. It is
understood that Mr. Woolcock will return to the mine in
a few days to resume active operations. Alterations are
to be made to the concentrating plant to increase its
efficiency. Mr. Woolcock is confident? that he can make
a profit-earner of the mine as a concentrating proposition,
and if energy and application will accomplish this he will
assuredly be successful. The old tailings heap at the
mine has been purchased by a cyanide man who laughs
at the suggestion that the copper contents present any
difficulty to successful and profitable treatment.
Occidental battery has been working continu-
ously since Dec. 1 last, and up to 19th Feb. had produced
14500 z. of gold, and from cyanide £2BOO had been won.
A3d div. is payable on the 17th inst. Co.’s half-yearly
report and balance-sheet have just been issued. ‘ They
are artistic productions. They have been designed to
give as little information as possible, and, in this respect,
could not be improved upon. Tonnages treated by bat-
tery and cyanide are carefully omitted, and to find out
what the working expenses really have been, shareholders
must work out their own calculations. Directors state
that they propose to prospect the big lode at the copper
zone, and they express the belief that it will be
a great source of profit if the price of copper is main-
tained at the present figure. They do not state, however,
how they propose to provide for this extra expense. The
policy suggested is the right one to pursue, but to carry it
out there will have to be a diminution in dividends.

The camel-man is in luck : driven out of part of
West Queensland and N.S.W. by the rains, he gets
at once to work on the long, dry track to Arltunga, the
new Centralian goldfield.

A leading hotel which has got into the
habit of publishing week by week a list of its
visitors in the press, now finds itself in a fix.
The guests are bombarded, of course, with the
usual crop of advertisements'and trade circulars,
but, in addition, the male portion is being favored
through the post with the card of Flossie and Co.
As Flossie and Co.’s business card falls impartially
on all alike, the result is often embarrassing to the
married men whose wives open their letters. It
is easy to say that the firm of Flossie and Co. is
quite unknown to the invited one, but not so easy
to say it convincingly, especially if the defendant
has previously visited Sydney on his own, and
looks back on nights that were obscured by a haze
of champagne, and about which it is practically
impossible to remember anythifig.
A correspondent discourses on Bruce
auctions: —
It is not generally known what a Bruce auction (now
proposed in connection with the N.S.W. Drought Relief
Fund) means. Many think it a “ Dutch” auction
beginning high and ending low. It originated in Mel-
bourne with Mr. J. V. A. Bruce, of the firm of Cornish
and Bruce, railway contractors. The Melb. Hospital was
in want of funds, and Mr. Bruce suggested that as com
was scarce, goods, things not wanted in the house, might
he collected and sold by auction. The idea caught on ;
every van man in Melb. gave a day free to gather goods.
The sale was held on Easter Monday, in the early 60 s, on
reserved ground on the Heidelberg-road, at I itzroy. All
the auctioneers in Melbourne gave their services gratis,
and many thousands of pounds sterling were realised.
Mr. Bruce’s hat was sold seven times over by Mr. Gemmell
at a guinea a time. The last purchaser refused to part
with it, or it might have been selling yet. " a \ waB
peculiar to Bruce : white (stove-pipe) felt, with the long
nap brushed the wrong way.

The deaths, within a few months, of two
lady doctors in Melbourne and two in Sydney
have raised again the theory that medicine is no
profession for women. _ The life begins with hard
study, and the rest of it is mostly hard struggle.

The same weary old tale of mistaken iden-
tity came from Wagga (N.S.W.) last week, when
a young man was up on the same weary old charge
of assaulting the same girl whom we know so well.
The same girl was positive as to the identity of
her alleged assailant at first, but afterwards stated
that she wasn’t so certain. Then, when the
accused \i&di'pvoved that he was at home in bod at
the time the alleged offence was committed, the
same old girl, on again being asked if she had
made a mistake, calmly allowed that she had.
Accused was at once discharged, as, usual,
“ without a stain on his character,’ and he s going
to be made much of, and have his costs paid for
him, and be presented with a souvenir. But, it
he’s wise, he’ll thank his lucky star that somebody
could prove that he was in bed that night, and
take care, in future, not to venture out after dark
without the protecting care of a maiden aunt or,
at least, four reputable male friends.

S.A. Register of Feb. 20th contains par.
to the effect that Lord Tennyson’s sons obtained
permission from ma to have a “shy at Aunt
Sally at Uraidla Show, and one of them succeeded
in knocking over a doll “to the great admiration
of the onlookers.” A tale of three generations:
The father wrote “In Memoriam.” The son is a
governor. The grandson is a moderate shot at
“Aunt Sally.”
More casualness. In 1887 a young man
in Southland (M.L.) took his gun and strolled out
into the bush, and disappeared. He had a nice
little selection and money in the bank, and was
engaged to marry a very nice little girl in Dunedin,
and all was well with him. The search was kept
up for months, but no trace of him was found,
and the insurance co. that had a risk on his life
paid up, on the young man’s relatives guarantee-
ing a refund in the event of his resurrection any
time prior to the last trump. The sequel has just
occurred. A relative of the young lady met the
lost bridegroom of 15 years ago face to face in
Dunedin, and said, “What ho, Bill!” and then
they had a drink. The explanation supplied is
simply that a desire for change came over Bill, and
he left. He was just tired, casual and monotonous.
“ S.D.” : Concerning a
recent par re the cleanliness
of barmaids as certified to
by Turkish bath proprietors:
It is a solid fact that clean-
liness and godliness are diver-
gent as the poles. The pagan
Roman was a cleanly person,
but directly the Christian got
the upper hand, he demo-
lished the baths and cursed
them solemnly, strewing their
sites with salt. In the middle
ages, loam on the human
body was glorified as a posi-
tive virtue, and the monks
vied with one another for
supremacy in picturesque
dirtiness. While not desiring
to asperse the character of
the barmaid generally, it
will be found that in modern
times cleanliness and friski-
ness frequently go together.
I remember overhearing a
virtuous matron telling
another how she had packed
“a Hussy” off her premises
for impropriety, adding that
she had always thought the
girl was no good when she
found herchanging her under-
clothing twice a week.
Further, I remember another
expulsion, this time from a
Temple of Venus in one of
the suburbs. A new acolyte
had been admittod, and
vanished again, and a week . ,
later the presiding priestess explained what had
become of the new girl: “We had to tell her to
go. She wouldn’t wash herself; so she’s turned
respectable again.”
• • • •
The “New Market," Hobart, celebrates
its jubilee this year. The first stone was laid by
Gov. Denison in 1853. The tenacity with which
the old ramshackle place has been called the
“New’’market for half-a-century is characteris-
tic of Tasmanian objection to change of any sort.
There’s no “old’’market —hasn’t been one for a
generation. And the New Market is no market.
In it there’s a slop-clothes shop, an ice-factory, a
skating-rink, and an auctioneer’s store —and that’s
all.
• * *
N.S.W. Fall Court, in an appeal case,
has again wrestled with the subject of what a, hus-
band must endure from his wife before he firmly
slams the front door on his troubles. Petitioner
married a boarding-house keeper. He had an
income of £4 a week, “every penny of which he
gave to her ”; and that ought to have secured him
at least most-favored nation treatment in the
boarding-house concert. But one day
he told her she was getting friendly with one of the
boarders ; he had not the slightest suspicion, but she had
ladies staying in the house, and she would find that they
would object to it. The respondent thereupon got into
a temper. On the following morning he had occasion to
visit the city, and on his return found that his private
belongings had been put out of the respondent’s room
into a servant’s room at the back of the house. He took
them back to the room, and while doing so met the
respondent, and asked her the cause of her behavior.
She replied that she would have nothing further to do
with him. He tried to draw her into the bedroom,
but she screamed out, and the servants and boarders
came from all round the hall. She then sang out that she
would have nothing more to do with him, and ordered
him to clear out of the house, or she would have him
kicked out by the boarders. He stayed there that night,
and left next morning.
Subsequently he wrote to the lady, but she did
not answer him; and he reckoned that she had
deserted him. But the Court didn’t think so.
Mr. Justice Owen said he could see no reason whatever
for the husband leaving the house, under the circum-
stances. He would have thought it his duty to have
stayed there to protect his wife from any familiarity on
the part of the boarders. As to his being afraid that the
boarders would kick him out, the law would protect him,
even if he could not protect himself.
It is a pleasing judicial fiction that the law is
always at hand to protect everybody, yet, as a
rule, when a man is kicked by 12 infuriated
boarders, it takes him two hours to find a police-
man to whom he can tell the belated tale of what
happened to him.

Hr. Ashburton Thompson, the self-im*
portant gentleman who administers N.S.W,
Health Department with so much empressement:
No person is a leper until the Board of Health has de-
dared him to be one.
Plainly, then, the leper who successfully conceals
himself from the Board of Health will never be a
leper, even if he is one. And when he dies of
leprosy —having died of a disease he didn’t have—
he won’t be dead, and his burial is an outrage.

The sordidness of the cow-prodder—
an advt. from a Rangitikei (M.L.) paper :
-jV/TATRIMONIAL. —A young man, who has just
-“'- L started dairying, wishes to meet with a widow,
with four to six children, with a view to the above.
Age no great consideration, but children must be able
to milk.
No romance or nonsense about love’s young
dream in that—only a cold-blooded proposal that a
possibly elderly and ugly woman should supply
him with a quantity of unpaid (and probably
severely-whacked) child labor, and act as his
housekeeper as well, all in return for his name
(which may be only Simpson) and some kind of a
home.

A pretty bad tram-car smash which occured
in Sydney last Saturday arose from the fact that,
just when the driver reached in haste for the
handle to turn off the current, < a heavy and
somnolent drunk collapsed across it and blocked
the way. There is no time to remove drunks
under such conditions.

“ Bunny ” : Cardinal Moran has put his
“splodger” into it again. Recently he made a
stirring (strife-stirring) Bpeech at St. Mary’s
Cathedral, and said, among other things, that
“he saw about 50 men, with placards in their
hats, taking down the names of those who went
in to patronise the R.C. fair at Auburn.” His
Eminence gracefully added that he thought the
men outside the hall were “ the most contemptible
set of scoundrels he ever saw.” Now, the muni-
cipal elections happened to be held on the
day of the Cardinal’s visit to Auburn. The
placards in the hats of the men were simply the
cards of the municipal candidates. The sus-
picious-looking lists were the ratepayers’ rolls,
which were being used forelection purposes. The
Cardinal should, from past experience, be better
up in electioneering.

THE STAR CHASE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As the softly-swelling murmur of the sighing: wind
acroon
Seemed to fan the jewelled brightness of the stars to
brighter gleam,
And a fleecy, snow-white cloud-bank lent a shading to
the moon
I was resting (this was youth-time) and I dozed into a
dream
Of the stars. I had selected one that seemed to me the
best.
Lightly touched it, then saw others seeming brighter and
more fair;
Chose another and another—'tv/as the same with all the
rest.
Till at last, as if in answer to a feebly-muttered prayer,
One I found that kept my fancy long enough to work its
fall,
For I held it till it tarnished and its glitter all was gone;
Then the pleasure of the star-chase turned to wormwood
and to gall,
And I left it for the darkness there to wander on and on.
Ah 1 I’ve lived that star-chase over in the days long since
gone by,
Where the stars were women fairer than those dream
stars of the sky.
N.B.W,

* * * .
Judge Docker (N.S.W.) joined in the
mania for judicial obiter dicta, for which an
appallingly hot summer, or something of that
sort, is probably responsible, with these remarks
at Orange the other day :
He was told that the Australians in South Africa were
the most inveterate thieves. In one town after the Aus-
tralians had left for South Africa he found that there
were no cases set down for trial, and on inquiry of the
police as to the cause, he was told that “ all the criminal
had gone to the war.”
Which was a cold truth and—seeing that it was
uttered in sentencing a returned cohentingenter—
really not out of place. But it has given grave
offence in some quarters, where the men who
nobly went forth to burn the Boer’s farm and
steal the Boer’s cow are still regarded as
“ heroes ” ; and it is the business of a judge to
avoid giving unnecessary offence. He has to be
beastly offensive to a good many people, when
sending them to gaol, and should confine himself
strictly to the inevitable unpleasantness of such
occasions: there is no sense in going out in search
of extra unpleasantness
• • * •
Despite all the laws to legalise marriage
with a deceased wife’s sister, the deceased hus-
band’s brother remains just as much outside the
pale as ever. A very rare case has just been heard
in Ballarat, where a lady applied for an order of
nullity of marriage. In March, 1896, her first
husband died, and in November of the same year
she was married in haste to his younger brother.
The latter gentleman’s disappearance made her
feel that she was tired of that family, and the
court annulled the marriage as one made inside
the forbidden relation limit. As a matter of fact
there is no real demand for a Deceased Husband’s
Brother Bill. Either the lady doesn’t want to
marry the brother, or the brother won’t have the
lady at any price. Yet the widower’s desire to
marry his late wife s younger sister is an emotion
that seems to pervade every white race. Nobody,
by the way, hears of him rushing her elder sister.
EXPLICIT.
LADY HELP (to her friend): “Them’s our two
daughters. The one with the ruff’s the
MARRIED one. She ought to he looking as she
might have looked if she hadn’t looked the
way she does, poor dear.”
The natives of Cook group apparently like
to sample a new toy. When Col. Gudgeon visited
Aitutaki lately he found a large number of divorce
causes awaiting adjudication. The fee for un-
coupling was 225. 6d., and after having paid this
for the pleasure of figuring in the court —an ex-
perience that will provide them with food for
gossip for years to come—a great many couples
re-married within an hour or two. Of course
there were a number of genuine and permanent
cases.
C.N. ’ : Lovely woman isn’t always
so idiotic as to risk human life through any
false modesty. Last year I was one of a
small picnic party at Sandringham (Vic.),
where the mixed bathing is, and, while
lunching on the sands, noticed a party of
girls a few yards away enter the scrub to
don their bathing dresses. Three of the
party had entered the water and were
rolling in the shallows when wild screams
came from the ti-tree, and the balance of
the bathers dashed out into the open
“ mid nodings on,” bawling lustily for
assistance. Apparently frenzied, they
turned to our party, and in a couple of
seconds were standing among us, all talking
at once and gesticulating wildly. It took
a full minute to grasp the fact that they
desired to save their companions in the
water from being deyoured by a shark
which, they alleged, was swimming around
them. Another minute elapsed before the
supposed shark revealed the fact that it
was a porpoise, and it was not until the
• trio were convinced that the porpoise was
harmless that they looked at one another
and suddenly realised that they were naked
and, up to that moment, unashamed.
Then, with the usual interpretation that
woman puts upon modesty, they covered
their faces with their hands and fled for the
scrub.
Premier Barton has advised the Gov.-
General that he cannot appoint a royal commis-

thereof.

“R.A.K.” : Strayed into the back seat of
a Melbourne church, Sunday night. Parson was
trying to impress on a comfortable, well-taken-
care-of crowd that, although the Almighty has the
whole Universe, with its countless worlds and
peoples on His mind, He also has each one of us
under His particular care at the same time, and
can busy Himself about such infinitesimal details
as our individual He illustrated his
remark by saying:
Take the case of men with mighty brains, like Napoleon
and Mr. Chamberlain, for instance. They can plan a
campaign and attend to the minutest detail (or arrange-
ment) at the same time. So it is with the Almighty,
etc., etc.
And there’s been no prosecution for blasphemy !

* • * *
The verdict in the Eurimbla-Wakatipu
collision case went dead against the Eurimbla
(the outward-bound vessel), as was anticipated
from the start. Various remedies for averting
collisions such as this in Sydney harbor have been
put forward, including a scheme, alleged to be
O’Sullivan’s, to bodily remove Bradley’s Head.
But the fact remains that no rule of the road,
however perfect in conception, can avert accidents
when its interpretation rests on human agency.
The interlocking gear of the railway system is
mechanically perfect, yet an engine-driver now
and then, as in the Redfern disaster, forgets all
about the rules, and in some unaccountable
manner wrecks his train. Thero are collisions at
times even in the open sea, when the two ships
have all the available space between Chili and
Australia to dodge in, and if Chili and Australia
were both dug up and removed to make more
room there would be collisions still. Even the
Ark, which wasn’t crowded up by shipping,
collided with Mount Ararat.

The Delhi Durbar, that cruel and vulgar
pageant which was inflicted on famine-stricken
and plague-infested India, cost the Indian
Government nearly £1,000,000. The bills to be met
by the various native princes sharing in the
barbaric spectacle ranged from £lO,OOO to £lOO,OOO
apiece (the latter sum was spent by the Nizam of
Hyderabad ; £lO,OOO supplied but a poorshow for
a Begum, Raja, Khan, Jain, Bang, Sneeze, Snore,
Ras, Maharao, Holkar, Nawab, Maharaja,
Akhoond, or other weirdly-named potentates).
The total cost probably ran to several millions.

show by London Express was “Barnum out-

A tall, greasy-looking gentleman, with
long hair, a flowing robe, and wearing on his head
an arrangement like an artistic waste-paper-
basket, recently descended on Maoriland in search
of subscriptions to maintain a number of Arme-
nian orphans whose parents had been butchered
in the horrors of some few years back, when
Abdul the Damned was a little worse than usual
—if such a thing can be. Later, however, it
leaked out that the total amount the reverend one
had remitted was £96, and in reply to indignant
demands as to where all the rest had gone, he
smiled a Christian smile, and, crossing his hands,
humbly explained that he only sent Sunday’s
collections to maintain the orphans. The money
collected through the week he used to maintain
his parents and to provide for his own old age.
Maoriland subscribers now begin almost to sym-
pathise with Abdul the Damned —the potentate
with the huge beak, and the long horse coun-
tenance, and the criminal ears, and the terrified
eyes, and the look of one who is Lost before his
time.

Significant newspaper item from Mel-
bourne :
His Honor (Chief Justice Madden) briefly referred to
the criminal features of the prisoner’s career, and con
tinued, “ Ho sooner did you come out of gaol than you
committed three other offences.” The prisoner was then*
sentenced to five years imprisonment with hard labor.
For almost the first time in his life His Honor had
to be brief, for the prisoner had just broken loose
in the dock and cut his throat in four or five
places. But even then His Honor managed, in a
condensed way, to refer to the leading features of
the prisoner’s career, the prisoner being held up
in the dock meanwhile. Evpn if the prisoner had
cut His Honor’s throat as well as his own, The
Bulletin is firmly convinced that the oration
would have been got off somehow. His Honor
would perish at the stake rather than not get off
his oration.

“Vul.”: What an inglorious mess the
average woman makes of things when she starts
out in the criminal line ! Melbourne’s lady bur-
glar, who was, t’other day, found guilty on two
charges of house-breaking, provides a case in
point. First, her modus operandi was of the
shoddiest and most transparent description, and
could deceive only a most simple householder.
Then, on being brought"to book, she committed
the further foolishness of defending herself. Her
defence consisted of the time-worn alibi, which
she essayed to uphold with such loose and trans-'
parent lying-that Judge A’Beckett said he felt
much inclined to commit her for perjury. Her
husband had cleared out with another charmer as
soon as his lawful wife was arrested. Altogether
—though she’ll doubtless repudiate the suggestion
with scorn —woman isn’t a success at living by her
alleged wits, and had much better stick to her old
occupation of spanking Tommy and cooking the
old man’s dinner for a while vet.
'* J.A.C.” : He “ 6 x B’s ” par. (B
21/2/’O3) about the Gippsland (Vio.) girl who,
because she was “ afraid to be seen naked,” let her
■sister drown. Very different was the behavior of
a young Maonland girl, as related to me by an
Auckland friend. One hot summer’s day he was
■with a party of picknickers on an islet in the
Hauraki Gulf, and two of the girls, separating
themselves from the others, went to bathe in a
small inlet. One of them got out of her depth
and was drowning, when the other, seeing that
she could render no assistance, ran, in the “alto-
gether as she was, to her companions for help,
with the result that the drowning girl was rescued.
Her friends voted her a heroine—and so she was.

The latest S.A. tragedy is almost as sordid
as its immediate predecessor. Once more a man
past 80 years of age has been done to death, it is
alleged, for the sake of his money. Unlike the
victim of the Stirling murder, he was a reputable
farmer, and the worst fault that could have been
urged against him is that he had married three
times. He seemed to have got a good wife on
each occasion, too. The scene was Penwortham,
near the pretty town of Clare, which sent Trades
Minister Kingston’s father to Parliament half-a-
century ago. Joseph Ninnes was supposed to
have been burnt to death and economy is so ram-
pant in the Wheat State that he was buried with-
out an inquest. But rumors of foul play became
prevalent, and on Feb. 17 (12 days after his death)
JL)r. Ramsay Smith went up to Clare, had the body
dug up, and made a post mortem examination,
which proved nothing. But the story as well as
the body was stirred up, and quite a number of
weird side issues began to crawl, worm-fashion,
into the case.
* • * *
The fuss about Bible-burning in Fiji,
which is being so industriously raised by some of
the religious leaders in Australia, must amuse
such of the world’s heathen as are capable of
undo" ending the position. Whether the Fijian
natives burned Bibles and took rosary-beads to
their bosoms, or burned beads and enthroned
Bibles as their fetishes, is not quite clear; seldom
is anything quite clear in a sectarian controversy,
because otherwise estimable and upright men lose
their heads and their mental balance, and become
frantic and irresponsible asserters of anything
and everything, when a religious question is
on deck. . But what does seem to be true
—for it is alleged from both sides—is that
the . conversion ” of Fijian tribes to Chris-
tianity- is a matter of political (or other) nego-
tiation with the chiefs. rA black buck chief
nros short of rum, or of beads, or of decorations
’'for his harem (if he has one), or whatever the
heathen in his blindness most desires, and he deals
with the missionaries for the religion of himself
and tribe. The fact that there is more than one
brand of missionary competing in the market
tends to raise the price of a tribe’s adherence. 1 f
he can’t deal on satisfactory terms with the
Methodists he has the Roman Catholics and the
Seventh Day Adventists to fall back on. So a
chief who is desperately short of beads, or bear’s
grease for his head, sells himself and his whole
tribe to a new church, unless the church which
has already purchased him will come down with
more bear’s grease in order to retain him.

TO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Song, due for graver goal, delays
A restful hour with you.
Its will to thread the whirlwind ways
Of clouded Freedom's might, it stays.
Your perfumed lips subdue
To wistful moments, treason sweet,
Its sombre wings and iron feet.
My mistress Freedom’s eyes are blue,
While yours are midnight’s glass ;
To her I could not be so true,
Were I not sometimes false with you,
Who gifts with love’s cuirass,
For war ’gainst Freedom’s infidels,
A mere ascetic of her cells.
ALCHERINOA.
THE INFERENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I knew the first night, dearest,
When you were sitting there,
I knew by lots of signs, dear,
The darling man you were ;
The length of time you fondled
The little bunch of flowers ;
And when I brought the album
You nursed it close for hours.
Then when I pointed out, love,
My doggie’s silky charms,
You let him nestle snugly
A long time in your arms ;
You then held little Willie
So nice upon your knee—
These little signs told plainly
You’d soon be holding me.

To-day (Thursday) is the centenary of the 1
Sydney Gazette and New South Wales Adver-
tiser, the first paper published on this continent
since the subsidence of Lemuria broke up the
Atlantean printing presses in the days when
Adam was young. It was the sth of March, 1803,
that a most deplorable sheet, printed on grocer’s
wrapping-paper, made its first appearance, and for
20 years it wriggled under the broad, heavy,
ignorant thumb of official censorship. By some
remarkable accident, the origin of which will
always remain a profound mystery, the editor was
not a convict. His descendants have suffered
accordingly. He seems, however, to have suffered
a worse fate. He was his own reporter; he col-
lected his own advertisements, set up the type,
worked off the 200 copies that made up his limited
circulation, delivered them personally, and spent
his few remaining moments of wakefulness trying
to collect from the subscribers the few pence that
enabled him to buy lamp black for ink and the
grocer’s wrapping-paper with which to let the
gossips know that the Governor insisted on them
keeping their pigs out of the Tank Stream, The
name of this martyr was George Howe. The
name deserves to be rescued from oblivion at least
once in a century. m

VARNISHED BOARDj
A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, March 3, 1903.
Mt Dear Arini,—
Hobart is an Elysian field for the young bachelor.
The girls exalt him in reverence, give him picnics
and dances and afternoon teas, and consider him
and his pocket to such an extent that they won’t let
him even pay a tram fare for them. The con-
sequence is he soon gets a head as big as Mount
Wellington. I heard of a typical picnic lately.
A lot of girls invited an equal number of young
men. Girls all went but in a body to the picnic
spot, making baggage-mules of themselves; the
men followed half-an-hour or so later, and found
not only the billy-fires going, but that the host-
esses had all had lunch ana were ready to wait
on the lordly men.
Winter after winter the same set of men go to
dances got up by girls, and go home with the girls
to sample their papas’ distilled grain, and never
give a return hop or even take their young
hostesses to afternoon tea. There hasn’t been
a bachelor’s dance for many years. I was
several time 3 into the “fashionable” tea-room
of Hobart—one small room —and so marked
was the absence of men that I asked if males were
debarred from entering. My hostess, of the hour,
explained that men had no need to spend an
orange Pekoe sixpence on girls in Hobart. Girls
were waiting 10 deep in pretty drawing-rooms, in
Srettier suburban gardens, quite satisfied if only
,eggie and Jack would eat and drink and be
amused at their expense. And Reggie and Jack
are never asked for a subscription or commanded
to buy tickets for this and that frolic or charity as
in Melbourne or Sydney. “O, for goodntsi sake
don’t put the men to any expense—if you do they
won’t come again,” that’s a constant exclamation.
The pathos of it 1
Another Hobart hostess of an hour told me that
of eight men who had dined with her about Xmas
time only one had paid his dinner call. One youth
who was suffering mild reproaches from a damsel
for not keeping a Sunday musical evening
appointment languidly turned on her with “Yes,
I know I promised, but that was a week ago.
You shoula have kept me up to the mark by tele-
phoning or writing. I’ll always come if I'm
nailed."
These surfeited-with-too-much-attention persons
are not all born Hobartians, you know. The
majority are from Adelaide, Sydney, Melbourne,
and other places. They go to the island as average
modest dogs, and generous according to their
means, but a few months in Hobart turns them
into—well, they turn pupmatic. The girls com-
plain bitterly of their demeanor and meanness but
the apotheosis of Jack and Reggie continues, so
they nave only themselves to blame.
I attended the most important “drawing-
room” in Australia—saw Tattersall’s drawing for
the Newmarket Handicap. I wanted to get at
the bottom of the roguery that did me out of the
first prize on the two occasions on which I have
put five shillings into the sweeps. But it was all
wearisomely honest and simple. A huge barrel-
like machine containing 100,000 marbles. The
straps around it were sealed with the huge red
seal of the Tasmanian Treasurer. The keys were
in an envelope also officially sealed, and a Treasury
officer was present to break the bonds. Mr.
George Adams, the captain of the industry—
“ King of Hobart,” as he is called —stood in the
background watching the drawing. He greatly
resembles Carnegie. A strong-necked, strong-
eared man, of few words, with grey hair, still
abundant, that won’t be brushed flat, but bristles
erect as if ready for action. He and his wife now
live chronically in Hobart —atHighfield Hall, one
of the hotels whieh he owns. It is difficult to
stumble into an hotel or swell boarding-house in
Hobart which G.A. does not own.
The Austrian Royal family seems to be
ad lioted to a sort of social democracy. It is
always causing a “scandal” in court circles by
radical marriages. A complete list of the lapses
from royal hauteur by this generation of the
Hapaburgs:
The Archduke Ferdinand secretly married Philippine
Welser, a lady not of royal rank.—Archduke John married
Anna Plochal, daughter of a post-official. This lady was
afterwards raised to the peerage as Countess von Aleran.
—Archduke Henry married Leopoldine Hofmann, an
actress, who afterwards was created Baroness von
Waldeck.—Archduke Ernest secretly married a woman of
humble origin. His son, Baron von Wallburg, is now
the head waiter at a caf6 at Budapest.—Archduke John
Salvador (John Orth) married a dancer, Emily Stabel.
Both are supposed to have perished at sea.—Archduke
Ferdinand, heir to the throne of Austria-Hungary, married
Countess Sophie Chotek, one of the Court ladies—
Princess Elizabeth married Baron Seefried, and Crown
Princess Stephanie married Count Lonyay (from whom
she has been separated.)
A humorous sight at Manly, the other morning,
was a very large journalist escorting a much
smaller journalist (in an abbreviated bathing-suit)
up and down the beach, looking for the latter’s
clothes. The smaller journalist had been courting
the rippling, glistening wavelets and composing
great thoughts in the water when he suddenly
came to—to find it was robing time and he had for-
gotten where he had placed his un-poetic outer
garments. Then he held counsel with his large
contemporary, and a mighty search commenced.
The situation was saved by a lady squeaking
“oh !” and jumping up and flying off the beach.
She had been draining herself on the pressman’s
Sunday suit.
Consequent on a proposal to turn the old
Exhibition Building in Prince Alfred Park,
Sydney, into municipal swimming an
examination has been made of the place, with the
result that it has been found to be nearly past use
through attacks of white ants. After the non-
success of the Natatorium in Pitt St. and other
similar ventures, Sydney City Council would be
ill advised to start more baths on such lines.
The clean fresh open sea, where the prawn and
the shark and the squid are, is the real place for
swimming baths, and surely there is enough
accessible foreshore.
A lady writes this, to all appearance quite
seriously :
Mothers make a mistake in expecting their girls to
make a good match by meeting the man, becoming en-
gaged, and married in the space of a few years, say from
20 to 23. Up to the time girls are verging on 20 mothers
will not speak to their daughters nor hear anything from
them concerning this very important subject. I believe
that, when a schoolgirl, of say 15 or 16, attracts the
attention of a boy a year or two older, that is the time
for her mother to make the fullest inquiries as to his
character and future prospects (taking care she does not
go to his mother for the information), and, if the boy is
likely to be worth following up, the girl’s mother should
do all she can to keep him hovering round her daughter.
This is (fhe mother's duty at such an early stage of her
daughter’s career, as the boy must not be seared away by
a girl’s forward advances. I have two girls, just begun
school-life, and my present intention is to start match-
making on their account as soon as they leave. In fact, I
have one or two nice little boys in my mind’s eye already,
whose parents are a guarantee that their sons will turn
out all right.
Alas for man; is he not to enjoy a close season
fromsporting mammas even in his tender boyhood ?
The London Hartopp divorce case wasn’t
without a special Australian interest. One
of the many fair dames who figuied, beauti-
fully dressed, in the witness-box was well-
known in Sydney, especially in theatrical
circles. She lived here unostentatiously, if not
rigorously, for some years, and as the lady’s family
belongs to London’s smart set, was able to supply
society with choice bits of gossip from the cold
country. The lady is an Imperial pensioner, for
military services rendered by her late lamented
papa, who was a general.
To-day, Saturday, at 2.30 p.m., Miss Mary
Robinson will give the first of her monthly piano
recitals at “Minnesota,” Johnston-st., Annan-
dale, Sydney. Misses Maud Dairymple, Julia
Davis, Annie Perry, and Messrs. Robyns and
Lewis Scott will lift up their fine voices in solos
and duets when the piano is taking a well-earned
rest.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Flemington meeting on Saturday might have
been a Cup week aftermath. Same warmth, same
crisp lawn, same clothes —nothing new. The
boldest dared not drag out an autumn novelty
to blister (as friezes do blister) in the sun.
Dress competition was limp. The two returned
giobe-trotters, Mrs. Andrew Chirnside and Mrs.
Fred Payne, showed two remarkable rig-outs.
Mrs. Chirnside’s was cream lustrous silk, spread-
ing into a two-decker skirt, as contrast to a tomato-
red velvet Zouave of blinking brilliancy. Touches
in the same fruit tone were introduced wherever
necessary. Mrs. Fred Payne’s somewhat similar
outfit showed up the finer for its delicate tones—
lilac and deep cream, the brighter color forming
the top-tier ; it was banked up at the shoulders by
a sparkling ruffle of frosted chiffon.
“Big John Forrest sported a tender silver-grey
top hat at the meeting. The Western blazes and
picnic-sprees have reduced his girth a degree or
two, and the coat that once met just half-way
across his commissariat now buttons bravely.
He stepped out like a spirited old gander, and
turned special blandishments on the daughter and
niece of Treasurer Turner, telling them the after-
noon tea-gossip of the Western States.
“ Miss Cynthia Brooke’s chiffons were rich. An
extra cascade formed a lengthy finish to the back
of an impressive millinery structure. The exi
treme in wide effects was realised by the much
pouched and voluminous gown. Turquoise
jewels had something to say in securing the result
of a charming lace frontage. Mrs. Norman
Menzies shook ner little mop of fair hair under a
wispish French toque. George Lauri and his wife
were one of the most solemn-visaged couples on
the Terrace strut.
“Dolores sang on Saturday night to an extra
smart audience in the most becoming garb she has
yet worn on the Melbourne platform—a cream
soft silk allowing of a little pink frivolity in the
Yandyck skirt incisions. The outline of the
figure was clearly defined, the usual tawdry and
bizarre trimmings being mercifully abolished.
Elbow sleeves were so built as to add length, and
yet leave the figure its shapeliness. It made a
new, delightful, up-to-date Dolores, and she
scored better results than ever. In the audience
the fragile Mrs. Frank Clarke set off her pretti-
ness by entire black mountings. Mrs. Willie
Manifold, also black-garbed, looked nevertheless
bright and frolicsome. Mrs. Edward Miller wore
an extraordinary powder-pink cloak of great cost-
liness, with a tall Medici collar that kept the
already lofty head of the matron on a swan-
stretch.
“ A comic feature of the she-suffragists’ meet-
ing at the Town Hall on Friday night was Miss
Vida Goldstein’s downrightness. She voiced, in
strong terms, her surprise and disgust at the
feeble espousal of The Cause by the individuals
who had preceded her. There was too little blood
and thunder in their attack for her taste. The
audience looked at the limp figure of Demosthenes
Trenwith, who still labored with his breath after,
his thunderous howl for The Cause, and who
mopped his beaded brow continously, and
wondered however the young feminine fire-eater
could find it in her heart to reproach an exhausted
man under such circumstances. The crisp, curt
style of speech adopted by Miss Goldstein since
her globe-trot in America, threatens to develop
into permanent acidity. But her points are often
well taken. The 'effete body’ of the Upper
House came in for wrath, and Mr. Nicholas Fitz-
gerald suffered in particular. That silly old
politician, said the lady, ‘ revelled in a particu-
larly appropriate name, Nick-or-less.’ And she
referred to the ‘dirty work* of the ‘Upper
House, ’ and divided the women non-voters into
three classes—fashionable women, ignorant
women, and—emphatically—bad women.
“ The naicest women the State boasts are pro-
moting a Gymkhana this week. Polo supporters
are mostly garnered from the wool and cash ranks.
Recently Kooyong course was purchased, and the
inclusion of the 16 leading horsewomen of the
State in the Gymkhana lists should assure a
fashionable attendance. Mrs. Everard Browne
returns to the saddle to compete in one race. So
far, Toorak backs Miss Betty McMullen —a fine
young rider, famed for the dash, pluck, and bold-
ness, of the Brush clan. Black-eyed Mrs. McMullen
was a Brush.
“Bishop Clarke makes his first professional
appearance in Melbourne next Sunday, at the
shabby, old St James’s Church, where his three
predecessors preached their introductory sermons.
Probably this will be the last time but one when
the verger will turn money away from the doors
of Melbourne’s Anglican pro-Catnedral. Only on
such special occasions do more than a handful of
people gather together to acknowledge their sins
in Little Collins-street. The decayed tabernacle
is spiritually dead. It stands on valuable ground,
and Bishop Clarke will (D.V.) live to see it
deconsecrated and converted to godless business
purposes. On that solemn last day the original
episcopal throne of Bishop Perry will be removed
from the St. James’s sanctuary, and presently
the crypt will expand into a spacious cellar where
the new proprietor will store liquor.
“ A little lot of believers, including two bridal
pairs, who are making it a honeymoon trip, set
off from Victoria last week to join Prophet
Elijah Dowie’s community of Zionites. The new
city of Zion threatens to fall into liquidation
before these fatuous disciples of the nubbly-headed
Prophet arrive upon the scene. Dowie, at latest
dates, was beset by workmen shrieking for their
pay. Presumably there is a certain amount of
building material still in hand, but the laborers
can get no more cash from Elijah D. His pro-
mised city was to be built upon a foundation of
‘tick,’ which is a good practical expression of
Faith, anyhow. If Elijah’s ‘tick’ is exhausted,
the Zionic Faith has evidently gone bung.
“Vapour baths have been added to oertain
massage parlors, so the female beautification pro-
cess begins to extend below the Plimsoll mark.
One wonders if the male client will follow suit.
He already takes kindly to face massage and nail-
polish. One point of grace the popular masseuse
accords to her man client, and that is cleanliness.
He comes, freshly-tubbed, for his finishing bloom.
He glows healthfully after an hour s massage
treatment. The she-client, on the other hand,
lets grease and molecules accumulate, and pre-
sents a neck motley from hair-dye effects. Her
massage includes a large amount of plain wash,
before the more delicate instruments of the toilet
are resorted to.
“ The Pursey Ohirnsides followed the rest of
their clan homewards last week. With their
consignment of children, nurses and maids, they
made the most imposing family group on board
the India. The departure of several Society folk
swelled the gangway The parting kiss between
smart friends grows obsolete. At the anchor-
weighing of the India the most careless ‘ Ta-ta ’
marked the farewell of good-fellowship.
“ A French cartoonist, garnering grist for Paris,
was much impressed with Yes-No Reid. On the
sea-voyage from Westralia he did a little private
snap-shotting. One day, as George snored in the
Summer noon, and the musical mouth dropped
into the rounded proportions of a ping-pong ball,
the artist took up a position for camera attack,
' when the wife of the slumberer sounded the note
of alarm . ‘ They are taking a mean advantage.
It isn’t fair. They’re taking you asleep, George.’
The ‘ old bird ’ gave an aggrieved look, shook his
fat head in reproach, and fell softly asleep again.
His, troubles ! For a comic politician whose
living depends on being comic, the best thing
is to be as comic as possible.
* Melbourne Society lose one of its most sen-
sible, intelligent, and bountiful members by Mrs.
Sargood’s departure to the Big Smoke. Always
quiet in her style of dressing, always plain and
simple in her manner, she made no bid for
supremacy in dross ostentation, but was a
strong, steady ruler of a smart set. She did good
turns to many poorer society friends. There are
ever so many fine acts recorded to her honor.
“A R.C. religious order is likely to be a bidder
for Rippon Lea if that extensive establishment
comes under the hammer.
“ In the big ‘ Geisha ’ audience smart folk were
few an I far between. Miss Beatrice Smith wore
her dark masses of hair built up over a colossal
frame. This, the latest, is not to be had in Mel-
bourne at present, so every recent voyager has
been requisitioned to mail one from Paris. The
hair shops will soon swarm with this line. It all
helps in the general ‘ blow-out ’ effect of the 1903
flutterer. She is even wearing shoes two sizes
over large, and has to stuff the nib-shaped toes
with wadding.
“There was not much to wonder at in a recent
case of imposture on a Collins-st. dress-builder;
so much credit is given the smart bride-elect. A
penniless bride can clinch a good bargain with the
dress-monger if only she can supply credentials
that the spouse-elect is a man of substance. The
system of post payment is so arranged as to allow
of a gradual settlement of the bill, on the agree*
raent of continued custom. Also the impoverished
bride-elect can secure goods cheaper on the pro-
mise of patronage when she is Mrs. Dives. There
was nothing startlingly novel in the smart swindler
ordering an expensive trousseau on the strength
of an imaginary wedding to come.
“ Strange effects are to be seen, this Autumn,
in velvet and felt combination—mostly in millin-
ery. A hussar cap, broader than the original
military mount, comes into vogue. Altogether,
harder and severer styles of hat are to prevail
than heretofore. In the department of dresses,
pouched fronts hold good. So do the wide-open-
ing ecclesiastic sleeves, and the elevated hips.
These are to be attained, in lieu of natural
provision, by pneuniatio pads or homelier frills of
stiff muslin. In frocks the joinings that once
aimed at concealment now invite attention by
heavy strappings. Fantastic buttons do nothing
but decorate. Hooks and eyes do the real work-
in concealment. The round skirt, affording plenti
ful knee room, spans out into a train that, caught-
up the directed way, does not interfere with the;
hang of the article.
“At the Durbar, as at our own de&uf-of-the*
Commonwealth show, officials and their con-
nections had all the best of it. Wealth per se or
social prestige, unsupported by official standing orr
representative position, failed to score. Better on,
such occasions to be a Federal frump, or even a.
municipal marm than merely a wealthy wool-
queen or high-stepping smart-setter. This ex-
plains why some of the Australians at the Durbar
enjoyed themselves so much more than others.
Miss Ruby Madden, as daughter of Victoria’s
C.-J. and sometime Acting-Governor, put in her
Delhi time in ‘ Lord Kitchener’s Camp,’ regarded,
as the choicest site for Government guests, except,,
perhaps, the Viceroy’s diggings. On the other-
hand, our only Bart.’s wife, step-sister and sister-
in-law were glad to find a niche in an hotel or im

PERSONAL ITEMS.
James Howard, who, if he lived until
March 5, would (allegedly) have celebrated his
102nd birthday, died at Edi (Vic.) on February 22.
He arrived in Sydney in 1828.
The name of Bullwinkle cropped up in the
customs prosecution of Robert Reid and Co. In
this connection it may also be mentioned that a
citizen named Prigg is mentioned in Sydney
Directory.
The 71st commemoration day of King’s
Schoo l , Parramatta, brought an “old boy ” of
1832 back to memory. Harry Gordon, one of the
three still living men who attended in the first
quarter (the other two are Rev. John Watsford
and Mr. J. S. r utter), attended the anniversary.
A Sydney undertaking firm displays in its
head office window a huge framed portrait of
Chamberlain, lhe man who has done so much
towards the advancement of the corpse-burying
business deserves this appreciation.
Alleged that preacher and faith-healer
“Elijah” Dowie, of the Purple Wings and the
new Zion City and the Zion Bank, is in great
financial difficulties, and that the new sect seems
likely to come down with a run for the want of
sordid cash.
The latest individual to have a memorable
shortage in his cash was, as usual, a zealous Sun-
day-school teacher up to the date of the trouble.
Time is rapidly approaching when a Sunday-
school teacher will only get a job shifting cannons,
or anything else he can’t shift far.

Maoriland papers are getting down to the
bedrock of the personal business, and one “ great
weekly ” solemnly prints a four column picture of
a back view of the Mayor of Christchurch fishing
in. a North Island creek. Probably the next

N.S.W. Justice Simpson is piling up a
judicial record. He has already severely repri-
manded from the Bench Chief Justice Darley,
Cardinal Moran, and the Solicitor-General. If he
can add the scalps of the Anglican Primate and
the Premier, he will have proved his judicial rigor
with impartial offensiveness.
There was a formal protest from, at least,

this year “to lead the Bar” by virtue of his office.
“ A junior barrister, who even now only earns
£lOOO a year, taking precedence of me," scornfully
snorted one K.C. who used to earn his £lO,OOO a
year, and, perhaps, could do so now if he kept his
neck to the yoke.
