The most frightful outburst ever known
from the Maoriland bench erupted recently when
Judge Conolly discovered that most of the jury
he was addressing were fast asleep. Not till
Tongariro splits in twain and the whole Hot Lake
district sinks in ruin to the depths of somewhere
is anything like it expected again in Maoriland.
R.C. Archbishop Carr, of Melbourne, has
said the most sensible thing we have so far noted
on the Bible-burning controversy :
He denied that the Roman Catholic Church was respon-
gible, even if the books were burnt by a French
missionary, and declared that if the statement in the
telegram was true, which he did not admit, all Catholics
would regret the occurrence.

lie. He “went for” Lyne, and said, amongst other
pleasantries: “ Mr. Lyne reminds me of Don
Quixote tilting at windmills, but at tilts
at other things besides windmills. ” Yes,
chipped in an ancient dryblower, he sometimes
tilts at windbags —like yerself an knocks em
badly, too ! ”
“ Melb.” : Fitchett retires from the
Methodist Conference Presidentship in a blaze of
glory and envy. Not long ago a Methodist
clergyman, with a busy charge and apparently
about £5 a week, asked me if I could introduce
him to any newspaper work. “No was the
reply ; “It is my bread, not extra bunco as for
you and Fitchett.” This indicates the mischief
to the Cause, the Holy Cause, through Fitchett
being absorbed in enormous secular business and
transacting his theology with the fag-end of his
mind.

Dr. Samuel Marsden, first Bishop of
Bathurst (N.S.W.), and grandson of Samuel
Marsden, the second colonial chaplain, used to
relate that, in one of his visitations out back, a
girl on a, station asked him to marry her. The
Bishop inquired the name of her intended hus-
band. She did not know it, but asked the Bishop
to wait until she went and ascertained. They
were casual even in those old days.
Cardinal Moran, in a recent speech, did
himself the mistaken honor of supposing that
Protestants regard him as “a wily cove.” This
may or may not be right as regards the “cove,”
but it is altogether wrong so far as concerns the
wily.” His Eminence may have moments of
wiliness, but as a rule he is an inconsiderate bull
rushing at a gate, and that is how most of his
troubles arise. The term “cove” is undignified
anyhow. Who, for instance, ever pictures the
apostles as 12 coves in Galilee ?
J. W. Osborne, who died recently in Cali-
fornia at the age of 72, was one of the staff at
Melbourne Flagstaff Observatory in the early 50’s.
When afterwards an officer in the Lands Dept
he invented the process of photo-lithography by
W the departmental maps are still produced,
and which was the basis of the modern photo.-
engraving on zinc. Having patented his in-
vention in various parts of the world he estab-
lished works in New York and Washington. Of
late years he was connected with the scientific
department of Stafford University, California.
Wiremu Nahira Tehoika, a Maori big-wig
at Kaiapoi (M.L.), who died recently, was one of
the survivors of the famous Siege of Kaiapohia,
when Te Rauparaha swept through the land on
the wings of sudden death. That marauding
person took the pah, but a large portion of the
garrison got safely away through the swamps, and
made their escape into Otago. In all probability
Te Rauparaha would have met his Waterloo at
the place where the tweed cloth now comes from,
but for the incapacity, or something worse, of the
chief Taiaroa, who was in the National Scout or
Judas Iscariot business in those days.
Vic. ex - Premier Peacock is a typical
instance of the hold politics get on a man once he
has been in the thick of them. Nearly twelve
months ago, the Smiler was known to be in com-
munication with certain moneyed English people
who wished to entrust him with the oversight of
their very large mining interests in Victoria.
Peacock, at that time Premier, wouldn’t take the
job, but negotiations have since been carried or.,
and there is now talk of his going ’Ome next year on
the business. But the Laugher is held back by the
necessity for “chucking” politics, and his bid for
the Federal Senate seat was due to nothing else
than the desire to have some sort of official stand-
ing. The only trouble is that when he announced
himself as a Senate candidate Peacock said
emphatically that whatever happened he could
not keep on Vic. Opposition leadership. At that
time, he felt absolutely certain of getting the
Senate seat. Since his defeat, he has carefully
forgotten his announcement re his physical unfit-
ness for party leadership,

Lieut. C. F. Fletcher, was recently proved in
England at £910,000, was at the time of her mar-
riage to her first husband (a young Stephen of the
well-known family of that name) a very pretty,
bright-complexioned school girl. Nor had Stephen
left school. Mrs. Stephen divorced her husband
after four years of wedded life, and not very long
afterwards was married to Lieut. Fletcher, who
was then visiting Victoria. Under Fletcher’s
will, the two sons by the first marriage (Conway
and Thos. Grant Stephen) are left £200,000 and
.£150,000 respectively, subject to the life interest
of the widow.
An old gentleman, resident near Molong
(N.S.W.), sold a farm a few years ago, and ever
since has carried the proceeds (about £700) in bank
notes about his person. Some days ago he lost
the roll of notes, bjit made no mention of the fact.
He searched the road cn which he had travelled
that day, and, failing to recover his wealth, de-
termined to await developments. A schoolboy
picked up the money and handed it to the police.
A couple of years ago a laundress at Molong had
a pair of pants to wash for the old gent, and in a
pocket of the trousers she found a roll of notes —
the same lot. Then, also, the old inhabitant
wasn’t making any fuss about his lost wealth, but
just awaiting developments.
The late Joseph Evison, during his
journalistio career, did one thing especially
worthy of praise. He helped to “bust” the
“Rev.” Worthington’s “love-feast.” Some ten
years ago, when Evison went to Christchurch
(M.L.) to edit a new paper, that unholy fraud’s
Agapemone was in full swing in the little City on
the Flains. The leading feature of the cult
appeared to be that you must by no means despise
your neighbor’s wife. Evison published particu-
lars of Worthington’s American career, and traced
out his local doings also, and though the preacher
had a “front” compared to which that of the
Equitable building isn’t a circumstance, and ex-
plained that he had repented of all these things,
he was never quite the same fraud again.
Superintendent Garvin, who is recom-
mended by Mr. Fosbery as his successor in the
N.S.W. Inspector-Generalship of Police, is more
an office-constable than a street-man. He was
for many years clerk to the super, in Bathurst.
His best-known work outside was in the Mugge-
ridge murder case, at The Rocks, on the Bathurst-
Orange road, for which Henry Jarvis was executed,
in the 70’s. The murderer burned the body and
scattered the ashes. Mr. Garvin gathered them
up, not a soup-plateful, produced them in court,
and convicted Jarvis. David Buchanan, who de-
fended, declared that, if Jarvis had held his
tongue—he confessed, on conviction —he would
have upset the verdict for want of a corpse.
This was Chief-Justice Martin’s first sitting; he
had been sworn-in that morning.

ft transpired, had indeed

was placed at length in the box. The course of the
defence was then to assert that at the time George Man-
ley Smith married the respondent he had another wife
alive —a probability so remote that no spinner of fiction
could surely have invented it. Yet this was proved to
have been the case. The woman’s maiden name, by an odd
coincidence, was Smith also—and she died; but her
brother proved that she lived four years after the
marriage of her husband with respondent. So the issue
of the trial was that the first marriage of the Coun-
tess was quashed, greatly, no doubt, to her own amaze-
ment, and the Earl and Countess remained, in spite of
albcomplications, Earl and Countess still
“ 00100 ” on a wild-haired man of science :
Inclement Wragge is lecturing in Vic. country towns on
“ A Voyage Through the Universe.” Starts off with a
spout of loyalty, in which he manfully avers that a
scientist must necessarily be loyal to the empire, wanders
through a universe of chaos in which rant, scriptural
quotation, French phrases (always translated for the
audience), scraps of Latin, repetition of adjectives, and
gibes at John See form the staple. He works the lantern
himself, and throws on the screen a series of pictures for
which he himself displays boundless enthusiasm. He
states, with reference to the refusal of the See Govern-
ment to pay his liabilities in connection with the Kosciusko
Observatory, that he is waiting for a particularly bad
disturbance, without a single good point in its character,
and, when it arrives, he will get his revenge by christen-
ing the disturbance “ See." But, supposing Mr. See gets
there first, and calls his next big shortage “ Wragge."
The story of a brown man’s fall:—
London Bankruptcy Court has been discussing the sad
oase of Prince Victor Duleep Singh—gross debts £100,141,
assets £607,682, “ of which £600,000 represents his claim
against the India Office in respect of his late father’s
estates.” Victor Duleep, who is seemingly much the
same sort of Prince as his compatriot Ranji, attributes
his failure to the fact that £7,000 a year doesn’t suffice to
keep him going in his “ proper position.” Meanwhile
the India Office has withdrawn the allowance, and the
dark gentleman is lying low in France, after the usual
manner of any impecunious British “ toff." An interest-
ing story. Hundreds of thousands of Indians live in a
chronic state of want for a little rice to keep them going ;
a great mob of London unemployed made a demonstration
in Trafalgar Square last week. And a black prince
nurseling of the India Office goes insolvent on £7,000 a
year. His schedule describes him as “ late of Hockwold
Hall, Brandon, Norfolk.” Duleep, until he went bung,
was a Merrie England lord of the manor, and the local
rustics ducked their heads when he flashed his teeth at
them.
The following gem, received from Mel-
bourne, is evidently meant as a kindly apprecia-
tion of Prof. Elkington, of that city:—
Pundit Elkington, of our University, says he does the
historical work of nine professors and 13 lecturers—9 -f
13 =22 solid Germans. He carries his weight with the
ease of a Brougham, and is tolerably familiar on the
Collins-st. Block, the University Club, and Society, with
a big S. His view of history is the Bovril of the books,
but those terrible 22 Germans, each only the 22nd part of
an Elkington, will say, “ Freund I this is only the his-
torical novel. You have to begin again at the A B O,'”
whereupon each digs his teeth again into his minute
scrap of leather to chew. For 24 years, since 1879,
Elkington has been absorbed in his Voyage over the Im-
possible, or trying to drink up the Ocean. He proudly
claims that he is statesman-making. Where are they ?
Is the Whittington Bashaw of Morocco one ? What is
history? All knowledge! Elk., is Jove above Laurie,
Tucker, Gregory, Kernot, and co. ? But if history is only
chawing up books, then Cherry, amid the Bacteria, is 22
times greater than Elkington.

A recent issue of the War Cry, com-
menting on the distress of the. unfortunate
Mallee cookies, bids them: “Praise the Lord,
there will be no drought in Heaven.” Average
cookie would be better suited if he were assured
that the drought had broken up in Sheol.

During a period of four months, Walgett
(N.S. W.) Stock Board paid bonuses on the scalps
of 13,607 crows, 703 wild pigs, 81 native dogs, and
227 hawks. The payment on the big amount of
crow-scalps broke the bank of the board. 1 his is
a sure sign that work is extremely scarce and
unemployeds very plentiful in that district. Only
scarcity of the usual bush-work out-back sends
large numbers of men to scalping, honey-hunting,
and other open-to-all games. Ih the same manner
a specially heavy yearly gold return of a State is
invariably a reliable token that employment is
scarce there, and numbers of unemployed have
been driven to eke out tucker, and, if lucky, 3s,
Od. a week at fossicking.
Three residents of Takaka, Nelson (M.L.),
recently discovered in a small wooded gully a very
large bullock. Upon investigation it appeared
that the animal had been there for several years,
its egress being blocked by a fallen tree across trie
only practicable outlet. The log was removed
and the animal driven into the settlement. It.
was very wild when first sighted, but after being
sworn at became very quiet and could be handled.
It is thought from its amenability to cursing that
it is a working bullock that strayed and became
trapped as described. In the narrow gully it had
worn paths to the barred opening, and these were
quite a foot deep. The animal is reckoned to go.
12cwt., and is in fair condition. It’s a good thing;
for that bullock that it could not think through,
all the years of its confinement.

A tall natural-history yarn :
Was recently visiting- a friend (on the Murrumbtdgee)
who is a bit of a fowl-fancier. Oat of a three-gninea
“sitting” he obtained the disappointing result of four-
young ducks. Bad enough ; but one morning, to his in-
tense disgust, one of these had mysteriously disappeared,,
and next day another had gone. Early next morning he
and I were on guard over the remaining two ducklings,
and noticed a large jackass hanging round Presently
this bird flew into a neighboring trae, and when we
approached to get a shot we came across a young jacko.
finishing the remains of yesterday s robbery. Captured
the youngster and put it in a large cockatoo-cage, which,
we placed on the lawn. In about an hour the big goburra
returned with a large worm in her mouth, took up a posi-
tion near the cage and conducted herself as if endeavor-
ing to entice the captive away. She remained thus all
day without ever coming near enough to the cage to
allow the youngster to take the worm. Next day she did
the same thing until about mid-day when, making a great
row. she suddenly flew off. Old Billy, the blackfellow,
said : “ lie come back, feed—then this fella die. And,
sure enough, after a time she did come back with some-
thing, which she took straight up to the cage and gave to
her progeny, which ate whatever it was with great gusto.
In an hour the young jack was dead, apparently poisoned
by its own mother. Old Billy said : “Me tell you right.
He fly over there, take a green bit out of grass and B ive
to this fella ; then this fella dead.”

A hawker (what a lot of life an open-eyed
hawker must see !) tells The Bulletin of a myste-
rious experience:
I was camped one night on
Burwood-road, near Black
Flat (Vic.). Burwood-road
runs from the Yarra Bridge
at Windsor to Fern-tree
Gully, where it joins the
Gully-road and meanders on
into the ranges and the bush.
I awoke about 5, just as day
broke, and was making over
to the nearest house for some
water when I noticed what
looked like a man and a
woman toiling towards me
up the long hill from
the gully. As they came
on, I saw they were really
two women and a little
girl—a pretty little thing,
beautifully dressed. As it
was Monday morning, I
reckoned they were Mel-
bourne excursionists who
had missed the Sunday night
train, and, being slumped,
were walking the 18 miles
back to town, but the sturdy
way they negotiated the hill
didn’t strengthen that sup-
position. About 100 yards
from me they stopped, and
the little girl slipped through
the fence of an orchard
and gathered some cherries.
They sat on the roadside
and ate these, then came on
and passed me unnoticed.
Immediately afterwards they
stopped again, and the
little girl knelt down in the
middle of the road and ap-
parently said a prayer. Then
all three got through the
rails into the paddock where
I was camped. I became
much interested, and fol-
lowed them. They went a
quarter-of-a-mile from the road,
when the little girl, at a sign (from
first to last, as far as I could make
out, none had spoken a word), sat
down on a log, and the two women
went on another 100 yards. 1 fol-
lowed, and retreated at the blush-
ful spectacle of both ladies com-
pletely disrobing, swapping garments and
re dressing themselves. Then they took up the
small child again, got out on the road and strode
(“strode” is the proper word) on towards Melbourne.
1 had my breakfast, thinking hard. After breakfast I
strolled over to the “dressing” spot to see if by chance
they had left any clue. I found a new purse, containing
half-a-sov., two shillings and threepence, and the return
halves of Ist class tickets from Hawthorn to the Gully 1
Then I thought considerably more. Why did they walk?
Why change clothes ? I put in the Age an attractive advt.,
for three days running, as to a purse found. No answer.

Extracts from letter from a settler in the
Trangie district:
We have in money 2s. 6d.; rations—solb. flour, 21b. tea ;
nothing else. ... I had a bit of meat upon Christmas
day. Have smoked for 20 years ; had to give it up. My
storekeeper has closed his store, having a lien"over my
furniture. My house, &c., are insured for £430, and my
wife and five little ones starving—one a baby, and cannot
buy it any food. My wife, who is a native of Sydney, 25
years old, is wasting away before my eyes. My black boy,
whom I have reared for 17 years, would not stand starv-
ing, and cleared for Queensland. ... I would give any
storekeeper in Sydney a lien over my crop for rations. I
don’t want to beg anything—God forbid. 1 will pay
back. If I get any crop this year it will mean £750, so l
need not beg only for food. Sir, it would bring tears to
your eyes just to really see and know what is ruling. I
work hard all day, come home, and the little ones are
getting thinner every day.
“Caro” : Once watched a creek (a bend
of the Lachlan) ‘‘come down,” as the bush saying
is. It had been dry for months. All day we
waited, for we heard the river was rising. An
hour before dark a yelling boy on h°rseb ac k
announced it was round the bend. We nea. l
know I expected to see a tidal wave foaming nke
,a beer bottle, so scuttled for high ground. Ihe
first premonition of its coming was a
low swishing sound, as of wind mov-
ing through a gully, made by the
-water twitter-
ing through
millions of
dead leaves.
Then some-
thing resem-
bling a black
snake began to
move uncer-
tainly in the
bed of the
creek, widen-
ing every mo-
ment. Wells
had been dug
at intervals
in the dry
bottom, and
after gliding
round these, the black
water at first trickled
cautiously over the sides.
It increased till, with a
booming roar, a sheet of
foam shot high in the
air, leaving but a whirl-
ing eddy behind. A
crumbling, dirty wave
flooded the lower part
of the creek with the
crisp purr of water over
long-dry sand. “Slap!
slap ! ” came each tiny
wave till the dusty
"banks crumbled. Eddies
raced giddily round tree
boles, from whose
branches crickets shrilled
Tiitifully. And still the
little black finger ran before; then a smell—an
awful musty stench—drove us from the banks, and
we rushed across the paddock to watch the first
trickle of water over the weir, and hear it hiss
on the blistered, drought-seared stones.

“6x8”: Be drought. Tarella Station, in
Western N.S.W., has been keeping drought
records for 30 years. In 1887 the gauge showed
21.26 in.; next year there was but 3.l2in.—Nature
had apparently exhausted itself. Last year easily
beat this with 4.07 in. The average for the 30
years is 10.31 in.; for the last seven years, 6.69 in.
Walloo, in same (Wilcannia) district, takes the
drought-cake, with a record of not a single frac-
tion of a drop of rain from August, 1901, to July,
1902!

“ E.S.S.”: The par re platypus (B. 14/2/02)
is slightly misleading. The burrow of the platy-
pus always has two entrances, one above water
and one under water. The former may be any-
thing from a few inches to six feet above water
mark, and is mostly hidden among tall grass or
rushes ; the latter connects with the burrow only
a very little way in from the top entrance. The
burrow is a long, narrow tunnel running up the
bank, and ranges from 20ft. to 50ft. in length,
terminating in an oval-shaped nest, the whole
formed somewhat like a water-bottle with an
elongated neck. The nest is lined with weeds,
grass and fibrous roots. The animal lays usually
two or three eggs, and breeds in early summer.
The platypus is excellent eating, and is relished by
aborigines, who spear it in the streams, or dig it
out of the burrows. It is not strictly a nocturnal
animal, as most naturalists aver, though it courts
shadows ; I have seen it disporting in rivers and
creeks at all hours in the day. From personal ex-
perience I should say that it feeds and swims
about for a few hours, has a good camp, and then
takes to the water again, day or night being
immaterial to it.

“Petramos": Wheat doesn’t come into
Orroroo (S.A.) as it used to do in days gone by,
and local buyers here have to squint through office
windows and send forth scouts to watch for the
coming of any stray cocky's waggon. Recently
saw two agents waiting on the outskirts of the
town to give a first bid. When the waggon
arrived, however, a third buyer was sitting up
with the driver. He’d met the waggon further up
the road and bought the load ten minutes pre-
viously.

For several weeks Dalby Herald had
been freely advertising and blowing up a pro-
jected Dalby issue of the Queenslander, and had
itself guaranteed to take 300 copies of the issue in
the interests of the district. In due course the
issue appeared, and it contained a yarn to the
effect that the “editor of the Herald at last
election had a special issue of his paper for print-
ing recording his (anticipated) victory. That was
the only issue of the Herald which never came
out.” Then a rampant Dalby editor reviewed the
Dalby issue of the Queenslander It is no
credit to that journal ; it’s got up badly ; the
written matter is wretchedly flat and partisan ;
illustrations tainted with prejudice ; best stores,
hotels, etc., conspicuous by their absence ; not
one representative man is photographed ; and the
feeble claw-prints of the local mopoke are over it
all.”

“ A.8.T.” : Re the rabbit as a “ squatter.”
His propensity in this direction is decided by Ins
color. The common gray rabbit finds protective
ground colors so abundant, that h© is evolving a
constitutional inertia that would do credit to a
domain dosser. Disturbed suddenly, he will squat
right away if the alternative is a lengthy sprint.
The “ bright tawny ”is more wary. It is harder
for him to get a background. Generally he takes
no risks, heading straight for cover. Disturbed
one day, he did 50 yards hard, then arriving at a
point between me and a piece of reddish sand-
stone —the only piece in sight—he went down as
if shot. For a moment I thought he had “ bur-
rowed.” Creeping up I found him hugging the
earth about 3yds. away from the stone, in which
he did not lose faith till I tickled him with a
stick. I have never seen the black or white rab-
bit squat long. They seldom leave the immediate
vicinity of the burrow and are always the first to
get under when danger threatens. They seem to
feel their natural disadvantages very keenly.
* * • *
At the experimental farm attached to
Rose worthy (S.A.) Agricultural College elaborate
tests are made in the use of artificial fertilisers.
This year’s results are, generally, that when a
crop is left for wheat the extra yield pays for the
fertiliser and leaves a little in hand, though no
much ; but when cut for hay the extra yield pays
for the fertiliser four times over. Of course, hay
is at a phenomenally big price this year.
WE TRIED IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Cooking is not the advantage it is made out to be.
Cabbages and lettuces serve the human economy very
much better when they are consumed raw than when
cooked.” — People’s Newspaper.
We camped afar from tanks and wells, did Billy Smith
and I,
And waterholes were scarce as prayers, and pretty near
as dry ; t , ,
And of the many thousand things whereat we used to
swear,
The cooking gave the others points, and won with points
to spare ;
For when you weren’t after fuel, collecting bits of scrub,
You’d have to carry billies full of lovely liquid mud.
And so the words—the printed words—that up above
you’ll see
Seemed like an angel’s voice to us, and filled our hearts
with glee ; ,
For round behind the firewood lay (1 wish it lay there
now)
A cabbage that we’d borrowed from the garden of a
Chow. ,
“Hoo-bloomin’-ray!” said Billy Smith, “I wish wed
known before. , .
Just chuck along the Chinaman, we’ll eat the villain
raw.”
We started on the mild repast, a nasty clammy ball,
With slug% and caterpillars mixed, but yet we ate it all,
And thought it wasn’t quite too bad, as worse things
might have been,
Till Billy asked me if we had a dose of chlorodyne ;
And, ere upon the summer breeze his piteous accents
died,
I felt as if I had a sort of hurricane inside.
But needless now to dwell upon the horrors of the tale.
So o’er the later details let us draw a kindly veil;
But for the benefit of all I’d clearly like to state
That Billy is athirst for gore—and so is Billy’s mate:
And if we ever get a show, we'll rend w'ith tooth and
claw
The men who'say that cabbages are better eaten raw

“ F.C.” : My private opinion, that the
real farmers of Victoria-though they don’t
call themselves such—are the men round
Melbourne, who make a living off 20 to 40
acres by workmq the soil, received confirma-
tion from a man I met lately. He bought
40 acres two years ago, out beyond Bur-
wood. It is notoriously poor land-lightly
timbered with box, and growing mostly spear-
grass in the natural state. Last year he
cleared and grubbed 12 acres,, laid down
grass for stock on five of these, and put in
° at ?- From . these he has now
stacked 20 tons hay. He is clearing the rest,
has fixed fowl-runs, and reckons this year to grow
and strip enough wheat and maize to feed 200
head of poultry all the winter. Am watching
the experiment with interest.

Ormi : Saw one afternoon, some years
ago, a crowd of people collected in Swanston-st.,
Melbourne, watching a hawk comfortably perched
on the topmost pinnacle of the Town Hall, tearing
to pieces and eating a tame pigeon it had caught.
1 he hawk could only have been reached by a gun-
shot from the roof, and it seemed to know the
municipal regulation against the use of firearms in
the city.
* • • .
The vitality of the turtle common to Aus-
tralian rivers is extraordinary, A farmer living
near Orange (N.S.W.) recently found a turtle in
the only hole of drinking water left. He chopped
its head off close to the shell and threw it into
some bushes. Seventeen days later he found the
headless turtle walking about some yards from
where he had thrown it, and with the remnant of
the neck sun-dried like old leather.

Catching beetles gives lucrative emplov-
hundreds of youngsters, and to many
$ tramps as well, during the cane season in
parts of Queensland. Ihe more agile and hawk-
eyed last season made as much as 6s. a day.
When the bonus for beetle scalps was initiated,
the insects were counted by the receivers, but the
collectors brought them in such vast numbers
that the expedient of paying by weight was
adopted. Then the nippers soaked the catch over-
night, and the increase of expenditure on beetle
destruction was so marked that soon the old
method of counting was reverted to.
• • • *
Nature (13/11/'O2) strongly advocates the
general adoption in Australia of the rabbit-
destroying methods by which Mr. W. Rodier, of
lambua (Cobar, N.S.W.), has cleared his own
station of the pest:—
Mr. Rodier’s plan is based on the well-
known law of nature that polygamy is
favorable to the increase of offspring and
polyandry is unfavorable. Rabbits usually
live in a polygamous state. One male im-
pregnates a number of females and produces
a large offspring. Mr. Rodier proposes to
convert this polygamy into polyandry by
catching the rabbits alive and killing all the
females, while the males are turned out
again. If this is done, the male rabbits be-
come more numerous than the females, harass
the females by their constant attentions,
and injure their powers of breeding. Thus
the offspring becomes continually less
numerous. That this result will follow is
illustrated by the case of public women,
who seldom bear children and never pro’
duce large families, and by other facts well-
known to science.
The ordinary course pursued in trapping
rabbits, in which all that are caught are
killed, so far from diminishing the evil, is
much more likely to increase it. The great
maj'ority of the rabbits captured are males,
because the male rabbits have the habit of
congregating in certain spots called “ buck-
heaps." In these spots they are easily
caught by the trappers, who are, of course,
only anxious to kill as many as possible and
to obtain the fees offered for their destruc-
tion by the Rabbit Acts. Thus the males
becomes diminished in numbers and the
breed becomes increased.
Mr. Rodier’s plan, which is certainly
theoretically correct, ought to be tried by
the authorities on a large scale. It would
be easy to fence round a few thousand acres
in one of the worst districts and see what
effect will be produced by capiuring the
rabbits alive and killing only the females.

“ 00100 ” : Shot a ’possum on
Deep Creek one night in February', and
although it was killed immediately, its
prehensile ring- tail kept it hooked to
the bough all night. Next morning it
was still suspended until I shot away
the bough with a Winchester bullet. I
shot a mate of the aforesaid ’pcs uni,
and the latter fell at once. Took him
to the camp and left him there all night. In-
the morning I tried to see whether he would
still hook, hung him on to the ridge pole
of the tent. To my surprise, he hung as if
he had grown there, but later in the day his tail
seemed to straighten and down he fell.

A certain well-known mining town has a
peculiar race of jurymen, whose custom it is to
re-argue with tongues and fists every case outside
the Court. Hence the local paper records with
great gusto:—“Our town is very quiet since the
jurymen departed for home. Most of them left
town without a scratch.” The local feeling evi-
dently is: “We are going down-hill fast. We
are not the town we once were.”

“ Major Was watching (lie various
unsuccessful tricks to shift a jibbing horse in the
niain street, Bathurst, t’other day, when an old
veteran in the ‘‘gods ” yelled out, “ Lift up one
of his front hoofs, hit the frog hard with a rock
and let the driver chop him with the whip at the

before a couple of discomfited and astonished
spectators could pick themselves out of the dust.

“ C.W." re “ indeterminate sentences
Perhaps something like this would be worth con-
sidering. When a man was convicted, instead of being
sentenced for a fixed period, a money penalty would be
set for him to work out. He would be given certain use-
.l' 0 do, and have, firstly, to earn his own board
and lodging, and secondly, in order to get out, to earn
enough to pay the penalty. If he broke certain fixed
rules the penalty might be increased. Under these cir-
cumstances, the offender with any reform in him would
get his score paid off as soon as possible and regain his
freedom. The work itself being useful would probably
have a good effect on him. Habitual criminals, being in
perpetual revolt against the law, would probably never
come out.

ADDED VITALITY.
Thousands of men and women are walking about, pale and haggard,
wondering why they don’t succeed, or why they are depressed, and Lok
bad, and have no energy, no hope. The whole thing is simple enough. A
locomotive with bad coal won’t keep time, and a horse with bad food won’t
pace well. A man or woman who does not put good fuel into tl t rystem
to keep up expended energy is a child. We must keep up the vital forces.
The mind and body want added vitality in some form or other. There is
no escaping that. But the vitality must be vitality: that is, it must be
the real thing—a perfect tonic for the nerves and a blood builder. We
cannot get it in full quantity with our food, so we mu t take it in a pre-
pared form. Fisher and Co. come to our rescue, fortunately, and offer us
the best and purest thing of the kind yet produced in their “ Fliospherine ”
(registered title). It is a wonderful restorer. It seems to travel like
quickfire to the energy cells of both mind and body, and makes one feel
buoyant and brisk, and fit for any work.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Queensland Govt, has now decided that
Kanakas working under three years’ agreement
are not liable to pay income (or poll) tax.
Carruthers’ political organisation has a
soap-man as president and a graveyard-warder as
secretary. Emblems, a bar of soap and a coffin.
The Vic. railway revenue is falling at the
rate of £2OOO a week —£118,093 decrease on last
year up to Feb. 20th —and Bent asks, piteously,
“What can we do ? ”
The Freetrade party is still babbling about
the blessings of the “ open door ” in commercial
matters. There will be a devil of a draught if that
door is left open much longer.
Sydney Daily Telegraph is always crying
about “the Melbourne-dictated policy” of
Protection, but never mentions as “ Sydney-dic-
tated ” the policy of bogus Freetrade.
Strong pressure is being applied to the
N.S.W. Government to induce it to cut down old
age pensions to a miserable 7s. 6d. a week, and
some, at least, of the See Ministry are said to
favor the idea.
The other day an angry deputation of
deluded navvies, at the Waranga (Vic.) job,
which Messrs. Irvine, Bent and Cameron were
inspecting, threatened personal violence to the
Ministers, but finally let them sneak away to an
accompaniment of savage insults. Things like
that are liable to happen to a Bent Ministry.
A RED RAG TO A (JOHN) BULL.
(The editor of London Daily Mail say his paper’s criticism of Australian finance was based on the articles in a Sydney weekly newspaper.)
THE BULLETIN: “ Dear me, I was merely desirous of warning the lamb , and Eve frightened the lion into a fit !”
Vic. politicians hold the view that had ex-
Minister M'Kenzie gone for re-election for the
Anglesey seat he would have been sent to Parlia-
ment again, the “perversion of his moral sense”
counting for nothing.
Senator J. C. Neild again tells the public
how N.S.W. Government might have cheapened
the expenses of the old age pensions administra-
tion by accepting his offer to act as commissioner
for nothing. But the Reid Ministry fell over the
£3OO or so it paid Neild for investigating the old
age pension question for nothing, and Reid’s suc-
cessors aren’t taking any risks.
Victorian Tories are already arguing with
enthusiasm that the Minimum Wage Law should
beat once abolished because the Factories Com-
mission recommended its abolition,_ and that the
Compulsory Conciliation and Arbitration idea,
which the Commission proposed in its stead,
should only be instituted after long, deep, slow
thought and statesmanlike consideration.
From Victorian “Factories and Shops
Laws ” report:—
There cannot in the circumstances of the time be any
return to the old conditions of freedom of contract in
factory labor.
Melb. Argus tells a solemn truth :
It has been very much easier to get loan money than to
get taxation money. The very classes which urge
Governments to borrow are the most strenuous objectors
to being taxed to meet the interest bills.
Complaints of circumlocution in the work
mg of the Vic. Lands Department suggest that
the departmental policy is purely obstructive and
disheartening as regards the outer circle of selec-
tors. It is different with the inner circle. Some
applications for surrender of mallee leases take 12
months or more to complete. But a surrender
to Minister M'Kenzie, for instance, could be com-
pleted in 12 hours.
So utterly has the Victorian railway sys-
tem collapsed under Bent’s management that,
though the wool and wheat traffic is very small
this year owing to the drought, it is impossible to
find trucks enough to carry even what freight is
offering. Bent is sweating and sacking to make
good the railway deficit, while the deficit increases
every day because produce, for the carriage of
which the public is willing to pay, is accumulating
and rotting all over the State, and seems to
become more and more of a fixture every day.
A swindling London importer (made in
Germany) has been fined £9300. A cheerful com-
ment on the Australian Tory doctrine that
Protection is a natural inducement to fraud!
The so-called Freetrade policy of Great
Britain makes no difference to the importer’s con-
science. Therefore Protection, in so far as it en-
courages local manufactures and checks importa-
tion, actually lessens the inducement to fraud
which Fatman so bitterly deplores.
Scheme for the colonisation of the New
Hebrides by Australia (which lacks population it-
self) does not seem to be prospering too well. A
recent report: —
Four of the new settlers took passage back to N.S.W*
. . . because of fever and other causes. . . By the Titus
a father and family came to join the eldest son, who was
supposed to have a home ready, but found that he had
sold out and gone back to Australia. Some of our neigh-
bors seem quite satisfied as yet, determined to hold on in
spite of a little fever. The rainfall this year is below the
average, being barely 63in.
At the end of a congratulatory smirk at
the success of the voluntary conference between
the Victorian shearers and squatters, the Abgus
plaintively asks : ‘ ‘ Why could not all similar
matters be dealt with in the same way ? ” The
Gippsland coal-owners, who lately refused a con-
ference with their locked-out employees, ought to
be able to answer that question. Why doesn’t
the Abgus plump for oompulsory “dealing with
such matters in the same way ? ”
When Prendergast denounced Vic. Lands
Minister M'Kenzie’s personal jobs in the As-
sembly, M'Kenzie resigned his portfolio. When
the Committee of Inquiry found against him he
resigned his seat. But he hung on to his grazing
licence, his position as member of the Executive
Council, and his free railway pass till Toutcher
and others began to press Irvine with awkward
questions. Then he handed them in. In a man-
ner of speaking, he fell by three instalments.
In the Victorian Assembly,
t'other night, Prendergast arose
and drew a truly harrowing picture
of Vic.’s worn-out locomotive,
whereupon Bent got wild —he gets
wild easily, and is reported to be
infuriated even in his sleep —and
protested that he never cut wages
down ; that politicians were not to
blame for the state of the engines,
&c., &c. The report of the chief
mechanical engineer on the state of
the said engines is richly humorous
reading. A portioi “There are
55 engines which are quite un-
service-able, and should be broken
up. Fifty-four others are not worth
heavy repairs, and should be gra-
dually worn out. Sixty-eight others
are still serviceable, though inferior
in usefulness, making a total of 177
engines out of the present stock
which are either inferior or obso-
lete and out of running ”(!) In
place of these 55 engines which
couldn’t go if they tried, and the 54
which could try but might not go,
and the 68 which might go if they
tried very hard, it is proposed to
build 39 new engines ! Those 177
decayed locomotives were “ per-
manent and reproductive works,”
too!
In its intense desire to turn the tables on
Philp and co. the Brisbane Courier actually
admits now that white labor, at a fair wage, can
compete against the Kanaka in the northern cane
fields. After this anything may happen.
Whenever a land values tax is mentioned
in Victoria the Tory wails about the “poor
farmer,” and “ taking the men off the land.” The
1901 municipal valuation of Melbourne city —not
Greater Melbourne —was £1,384,381, on an area of
less than 10 square miles ; that of the 11 largest
shires, Alberton, Bairnsdale, Echuca, Lowan,
Mildura, Orbost, Omeo, Portland, Wimmera,
Wycheproof, and Yea, totalled but £468,552 —
about one-third of the valuation of Melbourne
city, on an area of 19,357 square miles. It’s the
Collins-st. and Bourke-st. farmer who would pay
the land-values tax.
A letter from Hay (N.S.W.) :
About five or six weeks ago a gentleman came to Hay,
representing something or other he called the “Citizens’
Reform League," convened a meeting, and preached
about politics and reform and economy till after 10 p.m.
The chairman (Mr. Gibson, mayor) who presided at that
meeting said he was very much in sympathy with the
League and its objects, and especially that part of its
platform which dealt with economy—that they must
economise in this State in every possible way, or words
to that effect. The representative formed a branch
of his League at Hay. A few weeks later the
Government started to economise, and the railway service
to Hay was cut down from six to three trains per week
because the line was run at a heavy loss to the State.’
Now the citizens of Hay are up in arms against this
“ shameful proceeding.” So, on 15th Feb., they held an
indignation meeting, and protested in strong language
against the Government’s economy. The same gentle-
man presided at this meeting, and the same gentlemen
spoke, and the same audience clapped and stamped and
said “ Hear, hear.” This unanimous desire to retrench
the other fellow is sickening.
HALF A TRUTH EACH.
LONDON, Feb. 16. —The unemployed difficulty is assuming a serious
aspect in London. Three thousand workless men held a demonstra-
tion in Trafalgar-square yesterday, and passed a resolution calling
upon the Government to provide work. The speakers attributed the
distress prevailing to the war.
FREETRADER: “I tell you it’s the war!”
JINGO: “ You’re a liar! I tell you it’s Freetrade!"
‘ Jack Shay " writes :
Bulletin asks (7/2/’O3) why has the Mongrelian fat press
ceased yelling impending ruin to the sugar industry.
Well, a local financial institution has a nearly worn-out
refinery, and a fractured mill or two, and an acre or two
of sugar-land yet on its hands, and the ruin cry might
stop the sale of them. That is my explanation.
Premier John See now appeals to the
people of N.S.W. to rise to a “higher plane of
patriotism ” —which apparently means that they
should agree to sink party differences, to say no
more about the State’s extravagance or its faded
credit, and to lie as one man about the splendid
condition of the country so that the big loan may
once more be forthcoming. A very few weeks
ago John was proud of his isolated position upon
that higher plane, and denounced the people
down below, who foretold the slump that has
already come to pass, as false prophets, pessimists,
Jeremiahs and “calamity howlers.” Now he
feels lonely up on the higher plane, and wants
the howlers to come up and keep him company.
The gentle Kanaka again. Six days’ busi-
ness at Cairns (Q.) police-court:—
Wednesday, Jan. 21. —A Kanaka fined for drunkenness
and obscene language, and a Chow charged with vag-
rancy (remanded).
Thursday, Jan. 22. —A Kanaka, absconding from hired
service (remanded).
Friday, Jan. 23. —Two nigger assault cases (adjourned),
and a Chow charged with selling grog to Kanakas (re-
manded).
Saturday, Jan. 24. —Two Kanakas fined for drunken-
ness.
Monday, Jan. 26.—A Chow sentenced to six months’
hard labor for selling grog to Kanaka; three Kanakas
fined for drunkenness, and one Kanaka remanded on
charges of disorderly conduct and-assaulting a constable.
Tuesday, Jan. 27. —Annie Sou Sing (no color specified,
probably a half-caste Chow female), fined 3s. cab hire for
being drunk ; two Kanakas convicted for drunkenness; a
Chow convicted for selling grog to Kanakas (remanded
for sentence) ; and a Kanaka fined for assaulting a con-
stable.
A Maoriland correspondent :
The newspapers here are rather full of Seddon and Fiji
just now. Our Premier is represented to have made
strong overtures to the Imperial Government about the
tyranny at present obtaining in Fiji—how the popula-
tion was decreasing, the trade shrinking, etc. We are
told that Seddon’s representations have received due
attention, and that an improvement is anticipated in
consequence thereof. One wonders why Seddon failed to
make similar representations regarding Ireland. There
the population is declining much more rapidly than
in Fiji—in fact, nearly 600,000 people have left
there since 1891, and nine-tenths of them were
below 35 years old. There the Constitution, such
as it is, is suspended, juries are packed, rack-renters
made peers, evicters decorated, while one-third of the
country is devoted to cattle-grazing. Seddon, the man
whose heart bleeds at the sight of human suffering and
misgovernment, recently travelled through Ireland,
accepted hospitality from the heads of the rack-renting
brigade—and never uttered a word against the mis-
government which he must have seen—which, in fact,
must have bitten him if it were a dog. These facts did
not escape attention here at the time, but since Seddon
has returned he has sung “ The Wearin’ of the Grane ” a
few times—and all’s well again.
Sydney D. T. plumps solidly for the maim-
ing, if not the destruction, of the N.S.W. old age
pensions system, urging
That even with the most rigid adherence to the letter
of the law, and the elimination of all cases in which im-
position of any kind has been practised, no substantial re-
duction in the annual vote is possible without amending
legislation.
But
a new class of State-dependent has been created, involving
an annual expenditure of alarming proportions, without
relieving existing charitable institutions to any
appreciable extent. In addition the Act has helped to
encourage unthrift, imposition, and actual fraud, besides
loosening ties of kinship and weakening moral obliga-
tions.
The principle of the minimum wage is also being
attacked generally by the Freetrade press—to
whose economic beliefs it is, of course, abhorrent —
and altogether it looks as if the N.S.W. Labor
Party were about to find out very shortly the folly
of its ostrich-attitude on the financial question.

in'e. I used to suffer with very severe pains through my
back and chest, and had a very nasty cough. I was in

he could do no more for me, and that I was as well

, The minute by N.S.W Labor Minis .f
Perry practically suspends the Shearers’ Accom-
| modation Act for an indefinite time. There had
already been a suspension, and afterwards an ex-
tension of that suspension till March 31, and the
present minute carries the suspension into the
distant future. It is so customary now in N.S. VV.
to find Acts that put employers to trouble or ex-
pense suspended, while Acts pressing on workmen
are always in full operation, that it is time some-
one raised the question of a mere Minister’s right
to hang up the law in this off-hand fashion.
Bruce Smith has been lecturing to Sydney
women on how to use their votes to the best
advantage of the Smith Tory party, and, among
other things, has been ringing in his usual adver-
tisement for the Cheap Jap. Also, he urged the
Australians (in order that they may better appre-
ciate the Jap) to get over the habit of despising
other races. It was very good advice, though
with too much Jap in it. And if Bruce Smith
and other politicians hadn’t urged the Australians
so furiously to despise, hate, curse, and slay the
Boers, it would have been a much better instance
of Bruce practising what Smith preaches.

The Owe’Sullivan, holding forth at
Michelago (N.S W.), has again been explaining:
The Ministerial party taught the doctrine of hope,
confidence, and progress. They believed that New
South Wales was so rich in resources that there was
ample room for development, and settlement by popu-
lation. They had an abiding faith in the credit and good
name of the State, and therefore did not hesitate to bor-
row money at 3} per cent., and to invest it in public
works, railways, tramways, sewerage works, artesian
wells, water conservation works, etc., which returrjed
from 4to 5 and 6 per cent. Only a few days ago the
Railway Commissioners admitted that the Newcastle
tramways were returning 5 per cent.
Well, what does it profit N.S.W. if one miserable
tramway is returning 5 percent. ? The real point
is that the See-Owe Sullivan Ministry is borrow-
ing money at 4 per cent., and spending it on
works that average perhaps 2 per cent., and the
above yarn is a gross and shameful misrepre-
sentation which comes perilously near to absolute
falsehood.

For Sale.
TASMANIA.
NORTH WEST COAST.
rnHE VAN DIEMEN’S LAND COMPANY is prepared to
J- receive tenders for the renting of portions of their
estates situated at the Surrey Hills and to let parts of the
Hampshire Hills, and farms near Burnie.
The lands consist of rich basaltic soil, well-grassed and
timbered, and abundantly watered by numerous per-
manent rivers and streams.
The Surrey Hills Block, extending to 150,000 acres, has
lately been re-surveyed and divided into 11 Blocks vary-
ing in extent from 2000 to 24,000 acres, to suit the re-
quirements of those tendering.
The Hampshire Hills Block (10,000 acres) will also
shortly be surveyed, and will be dealt with in smaller
lots.
The lands are very favorably situated and are served
by direct railway communication with the Port of Burnie
whence there is a regular Intercolonial Steam Service.
This railway passes through the full extent of the
lands to be let and provides ready means for the trans-
port of cattle and goods to Mount Bischoff, Zeehan, and
the Western Mineral Fields.
It can also be arranged to let, if desired, smaller cleared
farms near the Coast, or houses to suit requirements
would be built in or near Burnie.
TENDERS FOR LEASES OF THE
SURREY HILLS RUNS
for a term of seven years from the 31st March, 1903, will
be received on or before the 14th March.
’ Full particulars as to conditions of lease, etc., may be
obtained at the offices of the VAN DIEMEN’S LAND
COMPANY, BURNIE, TASMANIA, where a plan of the
property may be seen.
Applicants may tender for all or any number of the
runs stipulating that only one or more of their tenders
mav be accepted.
The highest or any tender may not necessarily be
accepted.
Bv order of the Court of Directors,
A. K. McGAW, Manager.
Burnie, Ist February, 1903.

FACILE PRINCEPS.
BUCHANAN
SEMPER IDEM.
The Sydney amateur crack, Arthur Snrryth,
will next month celebrate his 47th birthday. Consider-
ing Smyth’s years, and the fact that he weighs over 16st.,
his recent performances on the Sydney cricket ground are
unique. Mounted on Dunlop tyres, Smyth has put new
amateur world’s records from $ to 10 miles.*
Messrs. Pateson and Cobcroft, auctioneers,
commission and live-stock agents, of 231 Sussex-
street, Sydney, are very well-known names in
Sydney commercial and produce circles. The
partnership was formed only in Sept, last, but
progress has been extraordinary. All the old
friends of the two well-known names have stuck
to the partnership; besides which many orders
have come from outside sources. The firm will
not of course do all the business of Sussex-street,
but it will do a very good share.*
“ Vul.”; Dr. Cresswell, president of
Melbourne Board of Health, advocates a diet of
raw meat for all persons who suffer enervation
from the Australian summer. There’s nothing
so beneficial under the circumstances, he says;
and a good “blow out” of underdone grilled
steak will often do wonders for an enfeebled
system. A reputable Sydney doctor told writer
some years ago that anyone with a tenderness
for underdone meats should take a pint of
kerosene at frequent intervals—a pint, say, immedi-
ately before the meal, and another immediately
after. But why he should do this that Sydney
doctor didn’t explain.

What England Thinks.
Words of Advice.
It is always interesting to think that many of
the customs and traditions which are strictly
adhered to in a new country like Australia are
the outcome of a long line of tradition in Eng-
land. Simultaneously the same things that
happen here are occurring in England, and oft-
tirngs a message comes from the old country to
her children across the sea bearing a message of
hope and inspiration, and encouraging them in
the work they have set themselves to do. Such
a message comes from the aneient city of Bristol.
Mr. E. Bawn, of 23 Ashwell-terrace, Ashley-
road, wishes his message to be blown to the four
corners of the Empire. “I want all men to
know,” he says, “of the wonderful good Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills have done me. For
many years I have suffered from indigestion
and pains in the back, which ultimately caused
my liver to be totally deranged. So bad and ill
did I become that it was impossible for me to do
my work. My condition was almost hopeless, and
it was not until I took Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills that I experienced the slighest relief for
my sufferings. Then, strange to say, within 48
hours of first taking them, the pain and agony
that had been mine for years, began to leave
me. I continued ,jvith the treatment, and nay
ultimate was a matter of surprising
quickness. Now lam perfectly cured, with al
the blessings that health alone can bring. ¥ou
may rest assured that I shall never be. without
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.” Dr* Aloises
Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood purifier,
and a positive cure for biliousness, indigestion,
constipation, headaches, sallow complexion,
liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples an
blotches, and for female ailments. Put up in
amber bottles and the full name blown thereon.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Sydney “books” who hurried over for the
first day (Saturday) of V.R.O. autumn meet were un-
lucky. Backers had a royal time at Flemington. First
favorites annexed four of the six races carded, while
heavily-backed second choice (Chantress) for the New-
market won. But at Canterbury Park (Sydney), same
day, ’twas the other way about, and the raucous-voiced
fairly screeched with joy at the manner in which all but
one winner came their way. The big race went to
Equator, about whom 20 to 1 was available.
■ • • •
Little Chantress saved tipsters in general from
utter disgrace. They all gave ner a place among their
selections, and luckily she got a good run. With
Chantress out of the way the first six horses in the New-
market Handicap would have been Independence,
Wairiri, Boaz, Rose Petal, Mairp and Fairy States —all of
them at 20 to 1, or longer odds.
• • • *
Abundance deserves a medal. No other dual
Derby winner has confirmed his “ form ” by capturing a
V.R.C. St. Leger since Trident cleared.the board in 1886
and ’B7. It wag a novelty to see a blue-ribbon horse main-
tain his reputation in the St. Leger, as Abundance did
last Saturday. The time (3.8£) was very good, consider-
ing that the winner led from start to finish, and was never
bustled by his nearest follower. Evidently Abundance
on Saturday had become himself again, or very near it,
tholigh he looked no bigger than he was. in November,
and not quite as blooming. Ikey Mo. admitted his doubts
on the subject by laying even money against the son of
Beanfeast, but Ikey had been misinformed by the touts.
When the track-watchers get struck by the fact that a
horse of Abundance’s class is distinctly “ off ” —he must
have been much below par a while ago—they are not dis-
posed to think him capable of rapidly coming on again.
• • • *
Last year Patronus finished third for the
Brunswick Stakes on the first day of the V.R.C. Autumn
Meet. This year the son of Patron carried 231bs. more
and won the same race by four lengths in faster time. A
difficult fellow to get thoroughly wound-up, Patronus is
one of the very best on his best day. Possibly trainer
Bradfield will prefer him to his stable companion, The
Victory, if they both start for the Champion, which is
not to say that a trainer’s preferences are always
wise. Scobie expected F. J.A. to beat Sweet Nell for the
Sires’ Produce Stakes, but the cute filly beat her stable
companion. And the Newmarket running of Hicken-
botham’s three candidates —Tip Top, Fishery, and Inde-
pendence—was an awful satire on “ stable information.”
Tip Top, starting first favorite, repeated his flash-in-the-
pan performance of last year. He led the field and then
died away. Fishery also failed to come with a rush when
wanted, whereas Independence, the least fancied of the
stable trio, finished like a racehorse in hot pursuit of
Ohantress.
• • • «
Interesting that, while Chantress started second
favorite at 6 to 1 for Newmarket Handicap, she was the
only nag in the race wanted by backers at Canterbury
Park (Sydney), where one well-known punter—prominent
for years—put a big amount on her, till at last 3 to 1 was
the best price obtainable. Comparatively speaking,
Purser, Tip Top, Sir Leonard andco. were totally ignored.
Heaps of Sydney money was invested on the strength of
a dream talked of in every sporting haunt. The dreamer
(employed in a Sydney bank) dreamt, three weeks ago,
that Chantress would win and that he’d die before the
race ; he died on Wednesday. This is reminiscent of the
oft-quoted Ballarat dream of years ago when Nimblefoot
won Melbourne Cup.
• • * •
Crack M.L. nag Achilles cost present owner
160gS. as a yearling and up to date has gathered 10 races,
representing £2500 prize money There’s heaps of coin
in horse-owning if you own the right horse —which requires
much luck to do.
• • • •
The Y.R.C. St. Leger promises to provide the
usual farce which makes one wonder why good money
is still wasted over what is literally a one-horse show.
The brown filly by-Bloodstone out of Colonial Barmaid,
nominated by F. Lowen, having been left in by an over-
sight, the field is now reduced to three runners,
Abundance, Great Scott and Rienzi. . As a means of pro-
viding sport one Chinese funeral is worth many St.
Legers.
* • • •
Peculiar methods of jock up on Lockstitch,
winner of Novice Handicap at Kensington pony meet,
last week, brought about cancellation of his license and
dismissal from the ground. Pity stewards didn’t try to get
at the reasons for the boy’s behaviour ; ’tis any odds he
didn’t act entirely on his own. Others were the real
offenders. Jockeys, to live, must do as they are told.
• * * *
Winner (Claremont) of two races at Woolloon-
gabba (Q.) electric-light meeting, t’other day, is by
Bowmont from Maroubra, and was foaled 10 years ago.
* • * •
Crack M.L. pacer What, who had done the
mile in 2.18, recently covered 2m. in 4.43 and then
dropped dead. Heart and lungs were found badly
diseased. Dead nag’s latest time squelched Vickery’s
4.45 accomplished three years ago.
• • • •
At Perth (W.A.) recently Ikey rebelled against
parting with Bgs. a day for a license and swore he’d quit
the country sooner than pay anything above sgs. Cne of
the three proprietary clubs gave way and another
weakened to 6gs., which concession was scornfully
spurned. Then the tote was called into request. Owners
and trainers subsequently combined and threatened
direful things unless Mo. was re-admitted, whereat he
was given another chance to pay up to the usual tune,
and did—a split having occurred in the camp meanwhile.
There are so many of the bejewelled in the West that
their licensing fees often exceed the stake money given
away. p - . • . *
The Chinese have an explanation of their own
re the frequency of police raids on Chow. gambling
dens. They assert there is a great discrepancy between
the moneys known to have been seized and the amount
sworn to before the magistrate. At the high-toned club
there may be much gambling, but the stakes aren’t
necessarily on the table and seizable, and that makes a
great difference in the profits of the raid.
* * » *
There’sgoingto be a delightful row when the pro-
posed Australian rifle team for Bisley is picked. Victorian
shooters, owing to the success of their cracks at the last
English trip, consider they are entitled to send a majority
of the team ; while the other States want the crowd
chosen according to population and amount of money
contributed. In fact the general idea seems to be to see
that a rep. goes from every State, and not to send a first-
class team to mop up the prizes and elevate the mana of
the Kangaroo in Bull-land.
* • • •
Those responsible for the financing of Lord
Hawke’s team are certainly on the look-out for the
bullion, and have arranged fixtures with N.S.W., Vic.
and Queensland. S.A. Association replied that it could
not arrange a match. The Maoriland trip is said to have
been lean with regard to the gate, and Australian busi-
ness is being pushed with an energy which indicates that
more than expenses are wanted by somebody.
• • • •
Vic. sadly lacks a wioket-keeper for inter-State
fixtures. Hastings tried to fill the breach, but did not
fit too well. Monfries made a better showing against
S.A. Blackham was Vic.’s great stumper in early days,
and he faded away before the advent of Johns. When
Johns faded, a worthy successor failed to materialise.
* m 9 •
Maclaren’s request to the M.C.C. to wait till
the end of 1904 before he brings out his next team must
be read in the light of an interview with a London Maid
man, to whom he said that, as the Australian hard
wickets knock out his bowlers so, once every four years
was often enough to make the trip. This, if carried into
effect, means that the English team would travel here
with the returning Australian XI. of 1905.
S.A. team against Viotoria, in the last inter-
state cricket match of the season, contains only Giffen,
Hill, Reedman, Travers and Fred. Jarvis of the old
stagers. The last-named comes into the eleven again by
reason of fine all-round play in club games. Hack,
Newland, Claxton and Kirkwood, were always sure of
their places, and the oolts Jennings and Gehrs' are given
a further trial, Waters being dropped. With" Jonah ”
gone it looks a very weak bowling side.
* * • *
Ernest Jones left Adelaide last week for the
West, so that his first-class cricket is probably at an end.
Figures compiled by the Register show that he took 60
wickets in test matches for 29 runs each ; in Sheffield
Shield games, 209 for 26 ; and in three tours in England
327 for 19. He revived fast bowling in Australia in 1892-3,
and has had practically no rival ever since. At his best,
about 1896, he was very deadly, and often enough two of
the best wickets would fall to his first over. His fielding
at mid-off was, of course, marvellous, and as a bat he was
better than his reputation, especially when away from
the Adelaide crowd, which insisted on his “ knocking the
cover off.” He made 66 twice against Vic., and 82 against
N.S.W., in December, 1898.
* • • *
Canterbury (M.L.) rose to the occasion splen-
didly when local left-hander Dan Reese accumulated
that century against the Englishmen ; he got a purse of
sovs. and a bat. *
* • • •
There was another attempt to wipe out the
Sydney Cricket Ground octopus last week at a special
meeting of the N.S.W. Cricket Association. But it
fizzled out. It was an effort to regain control of the
ground, and although there are many members of the
Association avowedly in favor of the proposal, there
were but a couple who stood their ground. Altogether
the Association is as weak-kneed as the Trustees are
hungry, and the public who looked for the “ lead ” from
the Association can now rest assured that no help will
come from that direction. What is really required is the
municipalisation of the ground and the abolition of the
present gang of trustees and officials.
* * * *
Though Vic. Trumper opened in Sydney grade
cricket with 336 he has since gone down three times for|22.
• • • •
Sydney first-grade cricket is in its final stages.
Supremacy will rest with either North Sydney, Sydney
District, or Waverley. First-named two are in a better
position than Waverley, who, to win, must come out atop
in next couple of matches while North Sydney gets de-
feated twice—rather a remote contingency, but possible.
• • • •
The Hill family of cricketers establishes a record
in the number of players of one name who represent one
club; there were five of them playing for East Torrens
(S.A) in competition.
» ft * ft
Middleweight fight at Sydney National S.C.
last week between soldier Jack Thompson and Jim
Richardson was all Thompson’s way. Richardson was
only there to be slaughtered, mainly through great
comparative weakness —for his foot work and baulking
often baffled t’other chap badly, and onlookers wondered
how Thompson beat “Snowy” Sturgeon. Referee
M’Donald interfered in the third round and gave the
match to Thompson. The vanquished man, inside 10
years ago, beat the then aggressive fighter “ Pheasant,”
who had just previously given Billy Hefferman a sorry
time of it. Richardson has since spent six or seven years
in Africa and England fighting with varying fortune—
more reverses than otherwise—and seems to have lost
power completely. Getting down to middleweight limit
had a little to do with his poor shape, but not all—the
man has deteriorated physically.

Marvellous ’Murka! Two years ago Yankee
papers reported one-time Australian middle-weight pug.
wonder, Jim Hall, dying of consumption, with only weeks
to live. Latest mail brought information that he was
“ in good health and weighed 1701 b."
* • • •
Inter-State tennis in Adelaide went, as usual,
to the Victorians, by 13 rubbers to 8. It stood at one
time, however, at 6 and 8 all, and if Bowen, .the local
champion, had not been out of health, the result might
have been different. The Victorian six, though not the
strongest possible, was well balanced. Four ladies also
made the journey, and in this department S.A. won
anyhow by 12 to 0.
• • • •
Competing against Read has apparently im-
proved S.A. swimmer Moon wonderfully. Hundred
yards premiership on Feb. 16 saw Read beat Moon in 1.8.
Same result and same time were recorded for a challenge
race between same pair (Thursday, 26th), yet on Saturday
Moon turned the tables by a yard in 1.4 4-5 (much the
speediest time either man has ever notched), and, in a
200 yards’ handicap, Moon, with 3sec. start from Read,
scored again. Is it really improvement, or did the water
make Che difference ? Races won by Read were contested
in Adelaide City Baths (fresh water), while those which
fell to Moon, Saturday, were swum at the Semaphore—
sea water. Read is much broader-chested than Moon,
and consequently has greater floating capacity, which
would tell in fresh water.
• * * •
Queensland’s swimmers threaten N.S.W. pres-
tige. Times accomplished in recent tests at Brisbane,
with a view to choosing local representatives for the Aus-
tralasian championships this month, revealed excep-
tional quality. Reported that Frank Gailey, three times
in one week, broke 2.30 for 220 yards. If this be correct,
Dick Cavill may be all out and then not win. The
world’s record (Lane’s) is only 2.28 2-5.

• • • •
North, Brisbane Hamers Swimming Club’s
“ spec.” in bringing Australasian champion Dick Cavill
over as a “ draw ” for the 220 yards championship of Q.
provoked a big kick from Brisbane swimmers and swim-
ming people generally, who considered the action
would affect Australasian championship carnival this
month, which it is the Q.A.S.A.’s turn to engineer.
Three speediest Bananalanders (brothers Gailey and
Hetherington) refused to start. Conference of reps, from
the continent’s swimming governing bodies, to be held
between the 14th and 18th inst. in Brisbane, will probably
consider the advisability of restricting State champion-
ships to bona fide residents of the States concerned, and
thus shut down on “ pot hunters” from other places and
the much too prevalent practice of speculative clubs pay-
ing “ exes” of so-called amateur swimmers brought from
one State to another for gate money purposes. As things
are now it is in Dick Cavill’s power to scoop every swim-
ming championship of every State, and the clubs con-
trolling these events would be only too glad to foot his
hotel and travelling bills generally the while, where’s
the encouragement to local men ?

Bike-racing—in special matches, at any rate
is growing exceedingly hideous; and the Taylor v&r BUB
“ anything-and-everything ” contests afford instances in
point. Last Saturday, Taylor and Morgan met in Mel-
bourne in a £lOO match of three heats of a mile each.
The first mile was run in 4.20 l-ssec., and, considering
the record for the same distance is somewhere about l.zu,
the awfulness of the thing is clearly shown. Yet many
sections of the public clamor for this kind of thing, ana
deride a walking match over the same distance. The day
of bike sports in Australia recently appeared to be in its
zenith, yet such exhibitions prove it to be in the last
stages of decay. Taylor beat Morgan in the first two
heats, however, and scooped the boodle.
• * • •
E. Durst writes : —“ Apropos the drowning of
two men whilst fishing off the rocks north of Little
Coogee (N.S.W.), it is astonishing what dangers and dis-
comforts this class of fisherman —‘ rock-scrapers go
through ‘ doing-in ’ the day. Early in the morning to
sunset these habitues will stand, solitary, at the end
of a 30ft. bamboo-rod or schnapper-line, with the
result at the end of the long day of a few black
fish and diabolical-looking, barnacled rock-cod. The
rock-scraper is most consistent. In the height of
summer, when the sun is coming off the water
like heat from red-hot iron and frying the seaweed
hanging from the rocks at low tide, you’ll find him there,
behind his rod, silent, waiting for the cork to bob. He 11
be there, too, hurling seawards a much-diminished line,
in the depth of winter, his back to the icy showers of
pool-water whirled up by the biting westerly winds ; al-
ways gazing seaward watching for the ‘ bumbora.’ Only
when it is upon him he races for safety. Sometimes he
waits boo long; then something happens as on a recent
Sunday. Despite his alertness an untopped roller
sneaks up occasionally and the next minute he’s the
centre of tangled lines and a surging mass of water.
And (provided he isn’t washed off), before the sea has
poured from the rock he’ll be back on his ‘ spot’ fishing
with a complacence and patience that would shame a
Chow or a much-boiled Job. The point of reef where these
men were washed off is acknowledged by J scrapers to
be the most dangerous in the locality, and, if one gets in
there, ‘ it’s a case.’ It is popular because a strong cur-
rent sweeps into this point, then curves away to sea, and
the scraper stands a better chance here than in some
other places of hooking a fish that no self-respecting
person would give 2d. for. It is strange that ‘ rock-
scraoers ’ are generally non-swimmers."

Old-time sculler of prominence, Tom Kemp,
brother of Peter Kemp—ex-world’s champion—dropped
dead in his boat, last week, while fishing, aged 53.
• » * •
Sculling people look to to-day’s struggle on
Parramatta River between Dick Tresidder (Newcastle)
and Double Bay crack Harry Pearce as a more than pos-
sible precursor of a big boom. Both men are in the best
of fettle. Pearce has had world’s champion Towns as
trainer, while Chris. Neilsen has prepared Tresidder.
Pearce scales 12.2 and latter 12st. Expected that
Tresidder’s great staying power, as exhibited in previous
races, will stand to him, unless Pearce gets too far away
early, which he’ll need to do to make sure.
Not a “Wily” Cove.
One speaker at a recent Protestant demonstration
against R.C. Cardinal Moran was reported to have
described that over-energetic gentleman as a
“wily covehe took the trouble to correct the
report, and to state that he said “ wily foe.” It
would have been of more credit to his perspicacity
if he had corrected the “wily”—for a more blun-
dering and foolish person than Cardinal Moran is
not before the public eye in N.S.W. just now.
His letter to Works Minister O’Sullivan on the
St. Patrick’s Day Holiday question shows that.
Premier See refused to proclaim St. Pat’s day as
a public holiday—not for the excellent reason
which he might have given, that in present cir-
cumstances, people who wish to “ honor patron
saints” could best do so by putting in a day of
extra hard graft and lifting trie country a trifle
towards honesty, but in a somewhat weak and
hesitant way. Then Cardinal Moran wrote to
the Owe’Sullivan :
I would most emphatically ask you to request the
Premier to reconsider this decision. You will bear in
mind that those who just now are objecting in the public
press to St. Patrick’s holiday are the same individuals
who every day endeavour to heap insult and obloquy on
your Ministry. Not one of them shall be ( sic —in moments
of emotion the Cardinal’s grammar seems to get a bit off
the perpendicular) propitiated by the insult which,
through the weakness of the Government, is now offered
to the Catholic body. On the contrary, your enemies
will be emboldened by the unexpected victory to attack
you more persistently, and to secure your overthrow.
Should Mr. See persevere in refusing to make any con-
cession for St. Patrick’s Feast, it must be idle for him, I
fear, to expect that the men and women of Irish parentage
will cast their votes for himself and his friends when the
day of trial shall come.
And the Premier and the Owe’Sullivan were
foolish enough to show no resentment at this
cheeky intrusion of the priest into politics—this
attempt to put the See Cabinet before the country
as an institution which is “run” by the Cardinal,
and which must do as he says or else it will fall in
its “ day of trial.” What the occasion demanded
was a straight intimation to Cardinal Moran to
attend to his church and his millinery and other
matters which come within his province, and to
leave politics alone. That would have been a
good thing for the Government and a good thing
for Cardinal Moran, who doesn't yet seem to have
thoroughly learned the lesson that nobody—of his
own flock or of any other religious flock —wants
his blundering intrusions into public business.
At Poverty Point.
“ I oan’t quite rid myself of the idea that Mr. Wilson
Barrett ought to have been a popular preacher, and not an
actor at all. So determined is he to keep both psychology
and literature out of the piece (‘Alfred of Engle-land')
that he has written it himself.”- London Academy,
There is a lot of nonsense talked as to the salaries paid
to theatrical performers. The chief comedian, the
highest salaried man, of one leading company, is under a
£lOOO a year contract, although his friends confidently
assert that he draws £4O per week. The leading lady,
tenor and baritone draw much smaller salaries.
Kate Vaughan, the slim, golden-haired danseuse of
yore, has gone where all the good Gaiety Girls go. Kate,
together with her sister Susie (lately in Australia with
the Broughs) and two other tall girls danced a glorious
can-can quadrille at the old Surrey Gardens (London).
From there the Sisters Vaughan were engaged to can-can
at Drury Lane, lovely Kate being the star of the quartette.
She was a revelation in modest short skirts and high
kicking—black tights, black gauze outfit, and golden hair
a-hanging down her back. The sweet vision migrated to
the Gaiety as a matter of course, and was included among
the “famous four" principals—Edward Terry, Nellie
Farren, Royce and Kate. There she danced very little in
comparison with her former exertions, but she got a lot
of salary for doing it. In after years, when she danced
hardly at all, she was a charming burlesque actress of the
quiet sort, and eventually she ran a comedy co. and
played leading parts. At the time of her marriage to
Colonel Wellesley (he divorced her a few years back) it
was reported that the Colonel would become Duke of
Wellington when somebody died, in which case Kate
Vaughan would have conferred great distinction on the
British Peerage. Such women ought to be duchesses.
Wallace King, on arriving at San Francisco from Aus-
tralia, was taken to the detention hospital. The poor old
tenor-falsetto is losing his memory as well as his reason.
If he wanted to sing “Sally Horner in the Corner” he
would probably warble some broken fragments of “My
Pretty Jane." How old is the West Indian darkie?
Some 15 years ago, when he first came to Australia, he
was spoken of by Frank Lincoln as a man who had been
quite a famous vocalist in the long ago. San Francisco
papers refer to his “ acting ” and his “ jokes.” He never
acted or joked in his declining days at Rickards’s. He
just stood up—a diffident ancient very much gone in the
knees—and sang like nobody else. “Sally Horner” b«
Wallace King was an inimitable thing.
The other night several hundreds of Chinese, women
and children, packed themselves into Sydney Empire
theatre to enjoy a Chinese “ opera.” Matters went celes-
tially until some hoodlum at the back yelled “ Fire,” and
in the stampede which followed three Chinamen were
injured and a lot of furniture was destroyed. There
wasn’t any fire. The alleged white man who raises a false
alarm of fire in a crowded theatre isn’t exactly the sort of
mate this paper would like to go prospecting with. And
Sydney Evening News refers to the affair as a huge joke.
Conjurer and illusionist Davis—just returned to Mel-
bourne after a successful tour round the less-known
parts of China—relates how, at the invitation (or com-
mand) of the Viceroy of Yeu-Sho, he gave a show before
that potentate’s numerous wives and friends, and was
afterwards invited to supper. The Viceroy then sug-
gested a game of poker for “ small stakes,” and after
several games, played with varying fortune, Davis dealt
his opponent four kings and himself four aces, and
various other fine hands. Having accumulated on his
side of the table over £5OO Davis stopped the game and,
after informing his losing host that he could win as he
liked, pushed all his winnings back across the table.
The great man was much impressed and offered to pay
Davis liberally if he would teach him some of his
wonderful deals—doubtless with an eye to future games
with brother viceroys and mandarins.

Denny aforesaid.
“ Melb.”: The Calculating Boy does Inaudi s feats,
except that he cannot manage anything like such
enormous masses of figures. The boy’s boss, presumably
his father, explains how the additions and multiplications
are done from the left, instead of the right, with a saving
of work. He shows how anybody can fix the days of the
week, as offered in the problems. An ordinary person
can do any of these in three minutes, with a slate First
you have to fix in your head a series called the Golden
Numbers. Then there is a division process. The re-
mainder gives the day of the week. Thus Ois Saturday,
I is Sunday, and so on. Some of the old-fashioned Church
of England prayer-books explain this process in their
calendars.!
“ A. 0.": Re par. about menagerie at Watson’s Bay.
The first Zoo was run by a man named Billen (1847 to
1856). When he died, his widow took over the stock, but
hashed the show. He had been a lion-hunter in South
Africa and other parts, and caught most of his own
troupe. Among other eccentricities, he drove two zebras,
tandem fashion, to town. Widow Billen, after slaughter-
ing the zoo, opened a waxworks in George-street. [This
narrative becomes more difficult as it goes on. Is it
really implied that Widow Billen slew the lions and other
trifles personally with an axe? If so, what size of a
woman was she? —Ed. B.J
