The latest development in connection with
Sydney’s North Shore Bridge—which will pro-
bably be built so me day—is the alleged offer of “a
large company in Great Britain” to the
bridge and take payment in Government deben-
tures in lieu of coin. The original tenders were
referred back for modification some months ago,
and are due again on March 18. If the alleged
offer really exists it should take a load off the
Government’s mind. In any T case the bridge will
be built out of the usual borrowed cash, and this
is really a chance to borrow the cash without the
usual publicity and obloquy.

The N.S.W. State Government is now
wasting good cornstalk money in the law-courts
trying; to demonstrate that the proviso in the
Constitution that the Australian Government
shall not tax “property” belonging to a State
covers “goods” also. It wants to have it de-
clared that everything a State Government
imports must come in free of duty, and, if the
State Government chooses to act as sole and
general importer on behalf of the people, then
everything must come in free. N.S.W. loses
nothing by paying duty on its State imports, for
it gets the money back at the distribution of the
Federal surplus, but the habit of being sour and
nasty to the Commonwealth Government urges it
on to spend the people’s money in digging a big
hole and then filling it up again.
The costly and useless law proceedings set
on foot, with a “strong bar” on each side, to
decide whether the State of N.S.W. should pay
duty to itself (as it gets the duty back from the
Federal Government that is practically what it
amounts to) on its own imports began humorously
enough. When the court started its three-hours’
argument on the point of jurisdiction, Acting
C. J. Stephen thus carefully delivered himself—
I understand, in a general way, that the Collector of
Customs is an officer of the Federal Govt., and not of the
State at all. I may be wrong, but that is my impression.
Of course his Honor very well knew he was
stating an appallingly obvious fact, and the court,
which pondered thoughtfully and judicially over
this utterance, knew it too. But the Bar must
live— at least it thinks so ; though why it should
live only the Bar knows.

The recently initiated opposition to
Queensland’s Continuous Government by Brisbane
Courier is proving a thorn in the side of the
Jrnilp gang, in so far that the public is now being
treated to truths which were hitherto carefully
kept dark, much to the profit of Philp and Co.
At Toowoomba show, t'other day, both Premier
I blip and Minister Dalrymple confined their
remarks almost solely to a bitter, heartfelt curse
on j*'^ Courier. The Courier, Observer,
and Queenslander, said Philp in great anger,
were run by one man with three opinions, said
pP ,I ? I °ns being those of E. J. Stevens, but be
(Philp) didn’t care a rap for Stevens’ political
opinions which, at worst, could do the Govt, no
harm. But, in reality, Philp’s gang has held office
so long mainly by the support—that is, the sus-
tained lying—of the Courier and Co., and now
its days are numbered unless it can patch up the
quarrel.

The criminal sittings now current in Ade-
Mide have introduced a new terror for the inter-
state evil-doer. Hitherto, his offences in other
States have been a sealed book, but by an Aot of
last year they can now be enquired into, and a
convicted criminal may have to face a further
charge of “having been convicted of felony at
Sydney in August, 1900.”

An uncivil correspondent-
I put in a day lately at Wellington (M. L.). It is the
smallest of the four big towns of Maoriland—the seat o
Government, and little else. “Apartments,” “Board
and Residence,” and the gentler hint of “Something
House ” show that one householder out of every three
keeps boarders. The town is ugly—no gardens, houses
jammed on to the street, antiquated trams, no citizen-
ship. From one point of view, the most prominent thing
among the Gothic tips of the roof of Parliament Build-
ings is a huge ventilator, leading into the Lower Chamber,
and painted red. I have an idea that the inside would be
found of a brilliant blue. Not far away is Parliament-
street ; it consists merely of several flights of stairs. I
also struck something new in descriptive signs. Stretched
across a window was a mysterious white garment, with
frills and lace boundaries ; an envelope pinned above
read “Ladies’ Nurse."
A letter about justice in British New
Guinea: —
It is oftimes said that murder is no crime, and, to judge
bv the sentence passed on the native for the deliberate
and premeditated killing of the resident magistrate at
Mekeo (B.N.G), it must appear to be a meritorious action
in some people’s minds. The punishment meted out to
this cold-blooded murderer was two or three years
imprisonment. Said imprisonment consists now in acting
as orderly to the gaoler in Samarai, and the miscreant
can be seen walking about daily, full of self-importance.
Truly an awful and awe-inspiring example to other
Papuans for the killing of a white man ! Speculation is
now rife as to whether Government will pension the
prisoner on completion of service, or simply make nun a
policeman. The odds are in favor of the latter, as it will
give him further opportunity of distinguishing himself.
Not generally known that the deceased officer was,
during Sir W. MacGregor’s time, dismissed the ser-
vice by the Council with the Chief Judicial Officer as
chairman. The Administrator was absent on a short trip,
and on his return reinstated him without any explanation
to the Council. Was in Port Moresby at the time and
naturally looked for further developments, but the re-
buff was born by the Council with Christian fortitude.
Harry Morant and his sorry fate, told of
in “ Bushman and Buccaneer ” (now in its second
edition). The history of the Breaker and his
verses and letters, including photo, reproduction
of the Pietersburg gaol MS. Price one shilling.
Booksellers and Bulletin office (post free Is. Id.)

Indigestion results from taking too much
food or some article of food of difficult
digestion. There is a feeling of distress and
sometimes severe cramps of the stomach,
nausea, vomiting, and sometimes purging,
but most often constipation.
The vomiting is beneficial as it relieves the
stomach of the indigestible food, and when
the bowels remain normal and there is no
cramping pains of the stomach, no medicine
whatever is required. All that is needed is
to let the stomach rest, take no food until
hungry, and then only a small amount of
milk toast, until the stomach is strong enough
to digest other food which should be taken
sparingly at first. When there is constipa-
tion, two of Chamberlain’s Stomach
and Liver Tablets should be taken to
move the bowels, and one tablet each follow-
ing day for three or four days until the
bowels become regular. If Chamberlain’s
Stomach and Liver Tablets act two freely
on the bowels, take only one tablet every
other day.

‘ Half-Double " r It has often been remarked of late
ru a r S v tbat the mfluence °f the Age is diminishing, and
that it is not the property it was a few years ago. A
glance at its recent Saturday issues, however, tells a
different tale. The 20 pages are stodgy with advertise-
ments, and the whole paper fairly reeks with prosperity.
The political unfairness of the Age is generally
set forth as the cause of the alleged loss of prestige.
.No doubt the paper is scandalously unfair to its
opponents. Its policy is to kill a political opponent
in . the shortest time, and in the most unfair manner
possible. It is certain that the rebuffs the journal
has received would have damaged it if there had
been a rival morning daily. At present, for all practical
purposes, there is none. The Argus exists only for the
purpose of showing what a good people’s paper in all
essentials the Age is. During the week ending Feb. 7
the Argus every morning patted the House of 48 Rich
Landlords encouragingly on the back for some fresh
attack on the reform movement, winding up on Saturday
with a nauseous set of political notes to the same effect,
until even case-hardened Argus readers began to feel
sick. Twice has the Argus had a chance to cut in on the
Age. When it dropped to a penny, numbers of Age
readers thought it must be better than their penny paper,
but. the Argus didn’t hold them long. Again, in the
Speight-Syme trouble the railway men in a body left the
Age for the Argus. Two big provincial Age agents told
me that they lost all the railway men then ; but that,
without a noticeable exception, they all came back.

“ Barbara ” on the servant-girl question :
I have employed servant-girls, and 1 know their ways ;
and I have been a servant-girl, and know that side, too.
In six weeks I lived in six houses as a “general, select-
ing six types of mistress. In one house that of a titled
Australian of repute and style—the inferiors were con-
stantly endeavoring to get some of their back pay ; and
the food was wretched. In another place, where fish
supplied the Friday’s dinner, I was given one sardine for
mine. Next came a “ Christian household, in which we
had abundant spiritual diet, but very short commons
otherwise. Here the kitchen-girl worked for her clothes
only. Never work for a “ high-class Christian family, the
female head of which is generally a cattish old woman
who thinks a girl is "not respectable who toils for her
living. My other places, notably a laborer’s home, were
excellent. Good food, a good bed and reasonable wages.
On the other side, 1 have employed girls at 12s. per
week who were absolutely useless; and in all my ex-
perience of helps 1 have only known about a dozen who
could wash dishes cleanly. I’ve been handicapped in the
bush with a large house, hungry men, a sick patient, and
a woman-servant, who for six* weeks wore one pair ®*
unwashed hose, and couldn’t boil potatoes without
burning them. I have had a cook who threatened to
brain me because 1 objected to her boiling a treacle-
pudding in the same pot as the corned mutton and
lsn’t it possible to form a domestic servants’ union,
protective of both employer and employee, and controlled
by competent leaders, possessing the power of disquali-
fying a member who is proven to be a thief; also the girl
who is unduly insolent to her employer or incapable of
fulfilling duties for which she engages on the strength of
a reference. The union should fix a standard wage for
capable “generals,” laundresses, housemaids, occ., and
endeavour to prevent the “ scabbing ” of “ lady-helps,
who “ slush ” for ss. or 6s. a week. It should fix another
wage for the weaker ones, who require light places ; and
make it a strict rule that no servant shall work for un-
remunerative wages, shall sleep in a comfortless room, or
receive more prayers than food.

EVERY HUMAN ACHE
Is RHEUMATIC In its origin.
Rheumatism is caused by TJrio Acid Poisoning'.
Goat is caused by Urio Aoid Poisoning.
Neuralgia is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Lumbago is caused by Urio Acid Poisoning.
Sciatica is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Blood Disorders are caused by Urio Aoid Poisoning?
Gravel is caused by Urio Acid Poisoning.
Stone is caused by Urio Acid Poisoning.
Bladder Troubles are caused by Urio Acid Poisoning,
WHAT IS URIC ACID?
Ukic acid is one of the many products of the wasting of the body. Every moment we live the
body is wasting, and it is the duty of certain of our organs to remove the waste matter.
THE KIDNEYS.
To the kidneys is allotted a large portion of this task. The kidneys of the average person filter
and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this quantity of urine should be
dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains, in weight, of uric acid, together with other
animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the kidneys from
any cause are unable to do their work properly, some of these substances remain in the system, and
become actively poisonous. _ Uric acid is the most dangerous of these poisons. When it is deposited
in the joints and tissues, it produces Rheumatism, Lumbago, Sciatica or Gout. In other
cases the blood, being contaminated, is rendered unfit to absorb nutriment freely and we suffer from
Anaemia, Neuralgia. D gestive Troubles, Blood Disorders and Debility. Sometimes the
uric acid is deposited in the kidneys themselves, and then the frightful pain caused by Stone,
Gravel, and Bladder Troubles is experienced.
Thousands have kidney disorders, and suffer from urio acid poisoning, without knowing the cause.
The most common indication that there is anything wrong with the kidneys is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A more certain test to make is, to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a
covered glass, and let it stand until the next morning. If it is then cloudy, shows a brick-dust like
sediment, if particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color; then the kidneys are not healthy,
and no time should be lost in ensuring their restoration of Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some
less serious but more painful illness will be the result.
Warner’s Safe Cure.
About thirty years ago, Medical and Scientific men, realising the importance of the efficient
removal of urio acid, and other waste poisons from the system, made special research with the view to
discover a remedial agent. They realised the fact that it was necessary to discover a medicine which
would act specifically upon both the liver and the kidneys, as, when the kidneys are affected, the
liver suffers in sympathy, and vice versa. The outcome of their research was the medicine now known
throughout the world as Warner’s Safe Cure. This medicine possesses the desired property in the
fullest degree, and though over a quarter of a century has passed since it was first discovered
no remedy has yet been developed of like power. It acts equally upon the kidneys and liver’
restoring them to health and activity, when all urinary and biliary poisons are of necessity
removed from the system in a natural manner. Warner’s Safe Cure consequently cures Rheuma-
tism, Gout, Backache. Lumbago, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia Gravel
Stone and Bladder Troubles, Anaemia, Debility, Indigestion, Torpid Liver. Even
Bright’s Disease yields to treatment by Warner’s Safe Cure.
Mr. T. J. Castner, of 8 Victoria-street, Paddington, N.S.W., says “ Some time ago I was
under medical treatment for severe pains in the back. The doctor who attended me wanted me to
undergo the operation of crushing a stone in my bladder, but I did not like the idea, although the
pain 1 was suffering was severe. A friend advised me to try Warner's Safe Cure. I did, and? after
taking some three or four bottles, I began to feel better, I continued the course, and, thanks to this
medicine, l became free from any trouble whatever,
A treatise containing particulars relating to Warner’s Safe Cure, and the diseases it cures will be
sent free by H. H. Warner ah‘d Cd.-, Liirrited, Australasian Brantb, Melbourne, Vic.

Alice M.G. : You bought Smith’s Creek shares
at 18s When they were selling at 235. your broker told
you that a syndicate had been formed which was going
to put them up. to 30s. They are now 16s. 6d. A lice,
where art thou ? You’ve missed the bus. Glenflne South.
If you can sell these at a profit do so.—— C.H.U.. u is
not fair for you to abuse your broker for buying dredring
shares for you. It is his business to do what you ask him
to do—his not to reason why, his but to go and buy.
» • •
The mining industry is Queensland’s great
stand-by just now. Charters Towers supports a popula-
tion of 22,405 people. The revenue collected amounts to
£13,061, and 1781 miners’ rights were issued for the past
years. Value of gold produced from thejuines by miU
treatment was £784,054, an increase of £78,164 on_the
previous year. Tailings treated by cyanide totalled a
value of £274,931, while alluvial produced 13500 z. Total
value of gold output for the year, £1,101,467, not in-
eluding concentrates sent to Aldershot.
Smith’s*Creek (N.Q.) closed its second half-
year on Jan. 10, and the dressing plant got to work three
weeks afterwards. The co.’s funds are gone I *”*
directors have made satisfactory arrangements pending
realisation of concentrates. Adelaide, where ?v
the shares are held, is still divided into two bitterly
hostile camps with regard to mine and stock , now t e
mill will have a talk, though it will not straighfroff decide
the thing that most needs deciding where N.Q. ventures
are concerned—that is, whether the stone lives down or
not. It needn’t surprise anyone to see some of the
reserve shares shortly put on the market.
Bellvue Prop., Mt. Sir Samuel (W.A.), ia
gradually coming into favor with investors. Co. has_a
20-head mill running, and is turning out JL
150 Doz. monthly. The developments at the three levels
opened up proved so satisfactory that it was decided to
increase the plant by another 40 head and work the mine
on a large scale. This new mill was ordered some time
ago, and, to meet the extra demand for ore which its
erection will entail, a new main shaft has been started
and is already down 80ft. The new mill will not get to
work till well on in the year, but, once it gets g° ln K. bo.
should jump into the front rank of gold-producers. Near
the surface the ore was considered to be very refractory,
being impregnated with copper, and a lot of money was
wasted in erecting a fancy plant, waB
started-in fact, was never completed. That the refractori-
ness was more imaginary than real is proved by the fact
that the tailings contain only 6dwt. pf gold from 30dwt.
ore, showing an extraction of over 80 per cent. At the
lower levels it is found that the copper gradually dies out
and the ore becomes very free milling. Co. recon-
structed a year ago with a working capital of £70,000
cash, and, as it has since worked at a profit, it is finan-
cially in a very sound position. #
Lake View South (W. A.) has bought the Robin-
son mine, Kanowna, and intends thoroughly prospecting
it Above the 100 ft. level the mine turned out 34,149
tons of ore for 31,4510 z. of gold, worth £llo,o°°, and
paid £BOOO in diva. Co. then broke up, and the om«
has been under tribute for the past two years. At the
200 ft. level the reef is fully 12ft; of solid quartz, but the
value is very low, gold only occurring in rough specks
here and there. No work has been done below this level,
so that the mine has never had a chance of proving ltB
real worth. For the sake of the extremely patient share-
holders in the South it is to be hoped that this new
venture* will prove an unqualified success. Major Judd,
who runs the show should be in a very good position to
judge, as he has had control of the Robinson mine for the
past two years. # •
Kanowna Consols (W.A.), a reconstruction of
the Pride of Kanowrra and Waldon's Find, ha 9 again got
to work after a spell of enforced idleness. Additional
capital of £2650 has been subscribed, of which only £ll2O
is available for mining. With this sum the manager
reckons that he can put the mine on a payable basis, but
he is a sanguine individual. The money will be gone in
three months with very little accomplished. Co. owns a
10-head mill, and has treated several thousand tons of
ore intermittently, but has never had enough working
capital to open out properly. Mine is splendidly situated
on a hill between the North and Q.E.D. leads from which
such an enormous amount of alluvial gold was won four
years ago. # # m
Cosmopolitan, Kookynie (W.A.), which has
been hampered in its developments by water difficulties,
has now got its pump fixed, and no further trouble is
anticipated. Developments at all the levels opened up
are proving satisfactory, but, owing to the water trouble,
it has been impossible to keep ore reserves much ahead
of the mill. The new manager Mr. H. A. Shipman, late
of Stratton’s Independence, Cripple Creek, is knocking
things into shape, and, before long, the gold output of
the mine should be materially increased. Co. owns 455
acre* or more than all the Kalgoorlie gold producers
combined. All the work hitherto done has been on the
Englishman reef, and, as the exploitation of this ore body
is considered quite sufficient for one co. s it is proposed to
float a subsidiary co. to work the Altona reef, on which a
little prospecting has been done.
* • • *
Great Boulder (W.A.) is starting in earnest to
open up the 1300, 1400,1500, and 1600 ft. levels at which
very little work has been done hitherto. At the 1600 ft.
level the lode, where tested, is 16ft. wide, and Is worth
about loz. per ton. The 1200 ft. level, which was sensa-
tionally rich at the start, has maintained a good average
throughout, and is one of the best in the mine. All the
levels below 600 ft. are practically untouched, so that the
mine has 1000 ft. backs in reserve. What that means can be
tranced from the fact that co. has already treated 400,000
tons for 783,000 z., and distributed £1,332,000 in divs. with-
out even depleting these upper levels. As a mining in-
vestment it can easily be seen that this co. offers a unique
opportunity. Its only fault is that a lot of itsalleged
assets should be written off out of profits, as they are
absolutely valueless. Were this done co. s position
would be unassailable.- Of, course it must be borne m
mind that the ore now being treated is of considerably
lower grade, and the gold of much poorer quality, but,
giving that in, many dividends are in sight.
Lake View Consols (W.A.) is opening up fairly
satisfactorily, the total ore-reserves now being estimated
at 91 000 tons, containing 74,0000 z. of gold, or (say) a
good 15 months’ supply. The average of the ore is only
164 dwts . but as the costs have been -materially reduced,
this grade of ore can be profitably treated. The main
shaft is down below 1400 ft., at which depth a level s
being opened up. The Perseverance lode shows payable
values at 700 ft. and 1000 ft., but is poor at 800 ft., while
the L V lode at the 1000 ft. and 1200 ft. levels is practically
valueless where tested. The mine is so patchy that a
rich bonanza may be found at any time. The Elmore oil
treatment plant is nearing completion, and should be in
a position to demonstrate its virtues, or its vices, next
month. . ,

Vicious curates in metropolitan churches
are seriously considering whether they should not
bring their working conditions and remuneration
into the Arbitration Court. The sweating
of' lower-class clergy is as bad as that of
drapery assistants. Those at the top of the
profession are on velvet, have their trips to Purope
when they like , and dangle their fingers in the
hands of royalties, but the men who do the real
graft of religion pressed to get new soles to
their boots. When the amalgamated Sky-Pilots
Association brings its case before the Court and
demands a living wage it should be a notable
episode.

They have quaint notions about infectious
illnesses in Otago (M.L.). Scarlet fever.had been
going the rounds in the district, and little Jennie
and Jock had been taken ill while on a visit to
aunty, and had gone safely through the disorder
on her premises. When they arrived home after
the illness, each dear child clasped a sealed en-
velope as a present for ma, On opening the
envelopes that parent discovered the peelings off
the skins of Jennie and Jock which aunty had
carefully preserved, so that no part of those chil-
dren should be lost.

The present curse of Maoriland is hat-
badges. You are out of the social swim unless
you go about labelled. Besides school badges,
there are old boys’ colors for most schools, and
every athletic club has its monogram —cloth of
silver or gold, according to the social tone of the
institution. The volunteer pins a gun-wheel or a
bugle on his cap. Even the youth attending a
shorthand class wears a monogram of the cheap
and nasty “ Commercial Academy.” The first
thing to be done when a new football club is
started is to settle on the club’s hat decoration.
Of course, to the public at large, most of them
mean nothing, but the wearer seems to derive a
lot of comfort from the label.
A social etching—by “The Man ” :
•* It is the last time ”... he murmured ; and his voice
broke into falsetto in spite of himself. Decidedly he . had
not practised enough. The girl buried her face in hei
handkerchief, hysterically moved. He hastened to give
voice again. “ But when I return ”...
The woman brightened inwardly : perhaps he meant
Business after all. The silence was fraught with ex-
pectancy. ......
“ I shall certainly call,” he concluded with
caution, wondering inwardly as to the next move. A
pause and he remembered. The passionate embrace . . .
Lacerated with hat-pins he extricated himself with
some difficulty, and with a shrill cry of “ Farewell!
turned majestically on his heel. He was forced to repeat
the manoeuvre, recollecting that he had still to take her
“ I have been premature,” he reflected, and the retreat
began. Inwardly two minds were doubtfully wondering
if theafifair had‘to be repeated i: external conversation
discussed sea-sickness and the iniquity of cash drapers.
At the historic gate a fully-matured voice from the
balcony solved their doubts, advising his fair companion
to step inside sharp and wash her head if she wanted to
that blessed night; her pa’d been waiting two hours foi
the bathroom. „ , ..
“ Nothing matters,” he mused disgustedly in the
shadowy street. “Nothing matters at all”.. .
Then he ran a very purposeful half-mile to catch his
train.

The Need.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
U.K.
When I am grey and stark,
When down into the dark
I grope purblind;
Then, lonely in the dread
Of dead among the dead,
Can I be comforted,
Though God I find ?
For in that shadow far,
Where all things shadow are—
That greyness drear—
I want no God unknown ;
I need some spirit lone
Whose grief is like my own,
Homely and near.
God dwells in loneliness ;
To Him in my distress
I cannot cling.
I ask no soul divine,
Remote, patient, benign,
But, ah ! a heart like mine
Remembering.
In that abyss I seek
Some humble thing and weak,
That faints and fears—
To meet in that lone place,
That is not Time nor Space,
A quiet human face
That has known tears.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
The Irresolution of Steve
Affleck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The grass-coated hollow known as “Bander’s
Crossing ” had always been a favorite camping-
place for men on their way to and from the shear-
ing sheds in the western plains of New South
Wales. It was—coming from the South—the last
of the rises, and when the camp-fires were alight,
and flickering on its smoke-stained boulders, it
was a cheerful scene enough.
So thought Steve Upton, the gigantic, red-
bearded man, comfortably reclining there one
night, and reflectively smoking. He had
“ done ” the sheds, and was now bound
home, where his thoughts were centred. His
meditative mood had detached him from two
other men, who lay quietly talking a little dis-
tance from him. One of these was Bill Napper,
his mate and neighboring selector; the other,
Zack Orton, a man hailing from the same dis-
trict, outward bound.
But quietly as they spoke, neither Napper nor
Orton allowed for the intense stillness which sat
upon the scene. Steve had paid no attention,
however, until he heard his name mentioned;
and then, with a slight inclining movement, he
listened keenly.
It was Orton who was speaking. “Well, I can
only give ye what they say,” he observed, lighting
his pipe afresh with a burning faggot: “I got it
from the missis, and you can bet she’d ’a’ ferreted
it out pretty well. Anyway, she says as all the
neighbors knows of it.”
P’raps,” said Napper, meditatively, spitting
accurately at a twig ; “it may o’ny be a larkin’
acquaintance, arter all.”
Orton shook his head: “Folks say not. They
has it that Upton’s missis has all sorts o’ excuses
fer runnin’ inter town, and drops inter this ’ere
Barker’s orfice quite easy and nateral, and they do
let on—well, mind ye, you unnerstand me ? I
wouldn’t swear to it in no court —but they do
chuck it about that he runs up ter the s’lection
sometimes in the evenin’. He’s a smart sort ’o
chap, too, writes fer the paper, and likely ter be
takin’ with women. Take it which way ye like,
she won’t be more nor half Upton’s age. How do
ye get over that ? ” he added conclusively.
Napper mused darkly. “By crikey !” he ex-
claimed, with emotion, 4 4 if any missus o’ mine was
ter do a thing like that, and me away, so ter
speak, a-graftin' f’r ’er, I’d—l’d ” but, no ade-
quate solution for this contingency presenting
itself, he savagely booted a log into the fire.
Steve had listened with scarcely a movement.
Even after the voices of the two men had ceased,
and they were stretched in slumber, he remained
in the same attitude of silent, expectant recep-
tivity. He shifted his position at last, picked up
the pipe that had fallen from his mouth, and tried
to think and to reason ; a difficult matter to his
slow-moving, decision-lacking mind.
Five years before, when farming a selection 200
miles from his present place, life had marked out
for Steve two main sorrows : first, the disappear-
ance of his only son, George (on the finding of
something wrong with the accounts of a small
local institution for which he acted as treasurer),
then the death of his first wife. Like many men
of great physical strength, Steve was sensitive to
a degree, and his shame and misery drove him to
action. He changed his name of Affleck to
Upton, moved out to a part of the country where
he was unknown, and, solitary and broken in
spirit, made a fresh start. A few years later the
country side said something ought to be done for
poor, untaught, bright-looking Jennie Bailey,
daughter of an ignorant, thriftless widow, whose
death had left her homeless —and Steve, accepting
the responsibility, married her.
For 12 months Steve and Jennie had lived in
the state which passes as happiness, and in that
interval Steve had learned to love his wife. His
nature was too deep for demonstrative affection ;
with him this found vent in a natural delicacy,
respect, and chivalric bearing. One dream he had
missed—that a child might be born to him to
replace the son he had lost.
* * * •
44 Seems ter me, Steve, ye’re kind o’ fergettin’
where ye are, ain’t ye? If ye want bacca, why
don’t ye sing out?” Napper said angrily, pitching
his plug over. “Am I ter smoke alone, or what ?
44 1 b’lieve lam gettin’ a trifle dull,” said Steve,
apologetically, fumbling for his pipe. “Ye see, I
slep’ last night only what ye might call middlin
good.”
Napper suddenly became thoughtful, his slack-
looking face, set off with wisps of whisker, taking
on an ominous solemnity, while his pale eyes stole
furtive glances at Steve. After a few minutes of
silence, he remarked judicially, taking his pipe
from his mouth :
“Puttin’ it as between man and man, and
without no necessity fer you to answer if ye don t
want to discriminate yerself, ye can say if ye
heard what Zack Orton says to mo last night.
St ev e colored, and winced visibly at this open
«wt shame. “Yes,” he answered
aw kwardl y , I_l happened to hear.”
Well, now we know where we are!” cried
Napper relieved “ and th’only question fer you
& oi n’.ter do?” and he
learied back and eyed Steve critically.
Ihis hit the centre of Steve’s difficulty. “I
am t, in a manner o’ speakin’, quite made up my
mi “ ’ 16 observed, tentatively.
. , ! Jeered Napper, “and you ain’t likely
to. Yell get fooled all round, and then start
tergivin and overlookin’! Look ’ere,” he went
on, severely “let every man deal it out to his
own missis in his own way—that I maintains—but
ier the crawlin goanner, what’s took advant-
age o you bein’ out o’ the way—well! ’e’s no
1! ? 6 • My Gawd ! ” gasped Mr. Napper,
suddenly realising the possibilities, “’e might ’a
bin after my missis! See ’ere, Steve,” he went
on, darkly “you know me, don’t ye? Right!
JNow there s on’y one thing you’ve got ter do, if
you re a man mind, I say, if you’re a man—you’ve
got ter drill a hole in this skunk ! If you don’t,
the chaps round us ’ll say you ain’t got the sperit
of a magpie You hear me? Well, now you’ve
got it straight ! ” And, knocking the ashes from
nis pipe decisively, Napper jumped up with an
ugly expression on his face, and began to saddle
up.
The two men were silent as they rode on.
Steve was now ruminating on the problem Napper
had put to him. It was habitual with Steve to
? n anc * con .sider any exterior solution of a
difficulty, as a relief to the harassing working-out
of one for himself. And although constitutionally
a man shrinking from violence—yet without
personal fear there was something in this sugges-
tion which, while it would have shocked him
under ordinary circumstances, now appealed to
his racked mind as being reasonable. The very
sense of his own fair dealings rose up in support
of it, and when he thought of what he had endured
in the past, how all his hopes had been turned to
gall, and how this last hope of all, upon which the
precarious balance of his happiness was poised,
had deceived him, a sort of savage determination
took possession of him. The man’s sense of jus-
tice, too, began to cry out to him, and he was
filled with a deep scorn of the enemy who had
stabbed him in the back. And then Napper—he
of the loose face and the wispy whiskers—had
raised the question of his manhood, and this
rankled bitterly.
And a keen light replaced the usually mild
expression of Steve’s blue eyes, and a novel air of
alertness, hurry, determination, accompanied his
movements. He became restless in camp, im-
patient of delay, and eager to be moving. Each
day his grim, resolute aspect became sterner,
warning even Napper from jest or personalities.
On the evening of the fifth day they instinctively
drew rein at Wilder’s Gap, whence lay before
them the open stretch of country where their selec-
tions were.
Napper looked curiously at Steve. “Well,” he
remarked; “about that matter we was speakin’
of ; ye’re goin’ to do it, ain’t ye ? ”
“If I find out as it’s true, Bill,” replied Steve
with tense determination ; “ Yes ! I am !”
“Right!” said Napper, encouragingly. “Better
come on ter my place first.”
The men commenced the descent. An ac-
quaintance was passed now and then, but they
did not stop, keeping the horses to their pace,
and exchanging no words. Steve pulled up and
waited at the slip-rails when they arrived at
Napper’s—he knew what they were for. “I
won’t be long! ” called out Napper as he dis-
appeared in the darkness. In a few minutes he
returned afoot, carrying a gun. “I’ve put a
good charge in it,” he said cheerfully, as he
handed it to Steve, “and as ye’ll take him
close up, it’ll do.”
Steve dismounted, and they followed the fence
that led to the house. As they approached it
they saw there was a light in the sitting-room,
which pleasantly illuminated the red blind.
Fastening the horse, they proceeded cautiously to
the building. Reaching within twenty yards of
it, they saw the shadows of two persons. Steve
knew one, and Napper recognised the other.
“That’s’im!” he whispered excitedly : “he’s
a settin’ on the couch by the winder ! walk ten
paces, and ye’ve got ’im !”
Steve hesitated. His usual moment of ir-
resolution —supreme and overmastering, this time
—had come to him !” “ I—l don’t some’ow like
it, Napper,” he said nervously, his face haggard
in the moonlight.
“What!” sneered Napper, bitterly: “d’ye
mean ter say, arter all, that ye ain’t got the
pluck ?”
“It ain’t that!” panted Steve, wretchedly, but
goaded to action, —“hows’ever, we’ll walk ter the
back. I—l’d rather take him front ways !”
“Don’t matter ’ow ye take dogs like them!”
snarled Napper, as they crept on.
Steve led the way through the kitchen, the
sound of two voices reaching his ears. The door of
the sitting-room was shut and, holding his weapon
in his right hand, he opened it smartly, and
followed by Napper, crossed the threshold. Seated
at the table he saw his wife who, hurriedly rising,
uttered a loud, terrified scream. Not deterred by
this Steve, with blanched face, quickly raised his
weapon, and pointed it at the young man facing
him, who now sprang to his feet.
“ Father ! What do you mean ? ”
Steve reeled and swayed, his arms dropped
before him, and the gun fell with a crash to the
floor. For a few minutes the two men looked
into each other’s faces, the father’s regard being
fixed, staring and dazed.
Then the young man spoke with bitterness:
“Father, I don’t deserve that! Even the law
doesn’t call what I did a shooting matter. What
I owed I’ve paid with interest, and they’ve over-
looked it, so I hoped to square it now with you.
I’ve seen you in the town several times, but I
suppose hadn’t the courage to speak ! ”
“ But what ’a’ ye bin doin’ here in this house ? ”
demanded Steve, hoarsely.
The young man did not answer, but looked
confusedly at Jennie, who stood flushed and
embarrassed. Raising her eyes unsteadily to her
husband, she said : “ Steve, ye know I hadn’t bin
taught ter—ter write, and, you bein’ away so long
—I thought I’d ’’—glancing at some writing
material on the table —“find someone to give me
just a lesson or two. Ye see”—smiling and
brightening—“l’d never written ye a—love-letter
—had I?—and—and, besides’’—looking at him
shyly, and lowering her voice—“there was some-
thing to tell ye—about myself—that you had
wanted, and only I could write it, and—and ”
She could not finish, for at that moment, Steve,
with a new light in his eye, and a cry from his
heart, folded her to him with a capacious arm,
and thrust out a grip to his son, which made him
wince.
Napper looked from side to side, helpless and
bewildered. “By Gawd !” he said, wonderingly.
He stooped and picked up the gun, and his roving
eye, chancing to light on the cat, contentedly
blinking from a cushion, he spat unerringly at its
left whisker, and stole on tip-toe from the room.
GEORGE MARTIN.

Itchiness of the Skin.
Ever have any irritation of the skin ? There
are many forms of it, any of them bad enough
to tax your patience. Haemorrhoid, a plague
of the night—no rest for the sufferer from that
complaint. Eczema, too. Hives don’t sound
dangerous, but they cause much misery to
those unfortunate enough to be troubled with
them. Doan’s Ointment is “a wonder” for
any such trouble. No troublesome irritation
of the skin can resist its healing, soothing
influence. Lots of Sydney people know this
now.
Mrs. Silva, 56 Duke-st., Woolloomooloo,
says : —“ A year or two ago I informed you of
the immense benefit I derived from the use of
Doan’s Ointment. My little girl had a skin
trouble which affected the leg. It was not
unlike ringworm, only a lot more spread out.
It was so irritable that she scratched it until
it became raw. We applied a lot of ointments
at times, but they did not seem to benefit it.
When I made the statement which has
appeared in the Sydney papers, Doan’s Oint-
ment hadn’t quite cured her, but had done her a
wonderful lot of good. I gave that testimonial
for the marvellous way this Ointment used to
bring ease, but since then the Ointment cured
it thoroughly. We used two pots and she has
been free of the rash over twelve months now.
We think the world of Doan’s Ointment.”
Doan’s Ointment is splendid in all diseases
of the skin, eczema, piles, hiv sores insect
bites, chilblains, etc. It is perfectly safe and
very effective. Very often two or three pots
have cured chronic cases which have resisted
other remedies for years.
Doan’s Ointment is sold by all chemists and
storekeepers at 2s. 9d. per pot, or will be
posted on receipt of price by Foster-
McClellan Co., 76 Pitt-st., Sydney.
But be sure you get DOAN’S.
Going Home.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’ve driven her through the great storm-billows
screaming,
Till creaking bolts and wrenching rods and bars
Cried in their anger, like Hell’s voices seeming,
We’ve cruised and swung and dreamed beneath
the stars.
Through boiling fo: m
Her screws have roared sea-measures wild and
hoary,
But now they hum a tune —another story—
A song of home:
“ We’re going home,”
Through storm and night they sing— through mid-
day’s glory,
“ We’re going home.”
Down in the engine-room worn glands are leaking ,
The shafting shivers and the cross-heads jerk,
And one great voice of steel calls hoarsely,
speaking
Of weariness and heavy, endless work.
Her funnel’s gray,
And every spar that rocks and sways aloe her
Echoes the music of the blades that love li r:
“Nor’ard away,”
“Nor’ard away.”
Hark how they sing and labor as they move her
Nor’ard away !
Bravely the steamers steer. With reckless motion
They clear the Heads and sweep to meet the
breeze;
But there is nothing mightier than God’s ocean;
Steel fights but conquers not its howling seas.
That pale-starred dome
Of Heaven that smiled upon them M their going
Will see them ploughing back, soft smoke-trail
blowing,
Making for home,
Making for home,
With white, white froth at stern and fore-foot
showing,
Making for home.
When we have done with Life—its laughter and
its groaning—
Ghost-ships will come, ‘and at the set of sun
Will call and call. And then with engines
moaning,
And one loud song of deeds of valor done,
They’ll lift the foam
With shafting shouting at its toil deep under;
They’ll sing this song, and tear the seas asunder,
“We’re going home,
We’re going home. ”
From steel and sea ’twill rise in tones of thunder—
“ We’re going home /”
H.L.

Rheumatism.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“It’s goin’ ter rain ter-night,” said Bob, as he
struggled to fit his hip into a hollow he had rooted
in the ground, 11 I’ve got them shootin pains.
“Well, yer not goin’ ter lug them blankets off
uv me even if yer got locomotor ataxy. Lie still.”
Cuttabri paused and spat through a hole in the
tent. “ There’s two ways uv curin’ rheum’tism—
snake-ile an’ gum-leaves. Yer have ter drink the
snake ile. It’s a fact. Wunst I called at a
cockie’s place fer a drink. I couldn’t talk wi’
thirst, an’ the cockie sez, ‘Go ter the hut, an get
some tea.’ I went in an’ scoffed a pint uv brown
stuff I sees on the table. It wusn’t tea ;it was
snake-ile for rheum’tism. I never drunk nothin’
fer a week after, an’ I never had rheum’tism since.
“ But gum-leaves is best. Wunst me an’two
fellers—Kison an’ Trott—went pioneerin with
cattle on the Diamantina. We had a lotuv seeds,
an’ we sowed pumpkins there, an’ they grew up
grand Well, a few months after comm there,
we went shootin’ ducks on a big lagoon. We was
goin’ ter fire at some ducks, when suddenly we
sees a pumpkin floatin’ up ter them quickly.
Presently one duck disappeared, an’ then another,
till four had gone. We was knocked bandy. How
did the blessed pumpkin get there, an’ where was
the ducks goin’ ? /Kison picked up his rifle an
bored a hole'through the top uv the pumpkin,
an’ if that thing didn’t fairly gallop oyer the
water awhile, an’ then dive under some bushes. Of
course, me an’ Trott dropped it was a nigger
who’d scooped out a pumpkin an stuck his head
in it, an’ waded in after the ducks. But Kison
was superstitious, an’ we thought wed pull his
leg. ‘Strange!’ says Trot, ‘I think Ive had
enough shootin’ terday.’ ‘Wot was it, sez 1,
frightened like. Kison said nothing jhe was one
uv them cow-brained coves that keep silence on
principle, so’s ter get ahead uv yer somehow.
We didn’t know him then. We pitched a lot uv
ghost-lies that night, an’ nex’ mornin’ Kison
looked straight at us, an’ sez he, ‘ D’ye think that
pumpkin wusn’t an ord’nary V ‘I don’t want ter
know wot it was,’ said I, sorter shudderin’. After
that Kison seemed real scared. ,
“Meanwhile the natives took on great with the
pumpkin idea. After a lot uv persuadin, I
enticed Kison one evenin’ down ter the lagoon.
Trott had a lot uv blaoks ready in the water with
pumpkins on, an’ when we come they all dived,
an' Kison went away full gallop. That night he
sez, ‘There's something wrong about here. Them
pumpkins must be the spirits or somethin uv
murdered blacks,’ an’ he seemed real mis a bit;
about it. We never thought he was such a bandi-
coot before ; but, yer see, we didn’t know him.
After awhile he become so scared he wouldn’t
move after dark, an’ we had ter do all the jobs at
night, whioh become a damn noosance.”
“ Soon after a lot uv blacks got rheum’tism real
bad fr’an wadin’ in the water, an’ had ter lie up.
But the queerest thing was that Kison got
rheumatism just then, toot He kept on sufferin
fr’m it fer mouths, an’ he would allers get bad
when we was workin’ hard at the cattle. He got
real useless. Well, one day me an’ Trott wus
paasin’ the blacks’ camp when we hears a great
vabber an’ sees a heap uv rneumatic blacks
moanin’ awful, like’s if the’ wus goin’ ter be killed.
We found out a great doctor wus comin ter cure
’em an’ judgin’ by their groans, the cure wus
pretty rough. We had left Kison laid up that
mornin’, and we’d told him we wouldn t be
back till late. However, we’d finished early,
an’ we decided ter stalk a few ducks
fer poor old Kison. When we got ter
the lagoon, wfFsees a pumpkin bobbin’ about, so
we waited. Presently the man waded out—an
knock me blue if it wusn’t Kison ! We nearly
died. He didn’t see us, an’ we crept ter the back
uv the hut an hour after an’ sees him eatin’ grilled
duck with two gins brushin’ the flies off him. The
flamin’ hound did this while we was graftin’.
“ But Kison slopped hisself by bein’ too careful.
He even pretended to the gins he had a funny
sort uv rheum’tism, an’ they believed him, an
reckoned they’d cure him, too. When we
got home next evenin’ Kison was missin
Just after tea we hears a sort uv screamin
far away. We agreed we’d see wot it
wus, an’ after a two-mile walk, we comes upon
a fearful sight. It wus a sort uv corrob'oree. The
black doctor, a big nigger in a ’possum-rug,
jumped an’ yelled an’ waved a boomerang m the
light uv an immense fire About 50 painted
devils in front follered his lead. Presently they
stops sudden, an’ a sort er thunder comes boonnn
down fr’m a hill near by. All the gins Tell flat on
the ground ; they thought it wus ole Aick, but it
wus only niggers whirlin’ notched shingles. Mean
trick, wusn’t it ? Then the painted crew rushed
towards the rheumatic blacks, who wus lyin in a
heap in the dark, bound-up. They screamed
horrible, an’ struggled, but they wus drug down
to the fire quick. Suddenly, over all the noise,
we heard a fearf&l yell an’ a stream uv curses. It
wus Kison —there wus no mistakin’ his special
swears anywhere. The black doctor then
rolled away the firesticks, an’ a lot uv them
brought up gum bushes, which he buried
in the red-hot earth. After a lot uv
corroboree they oollared four rheumatics, lncludin
Kison, an’ tied them down on the hot ground, so s
the eucalytus steam fr’m the roasted bushes
would rise inter them. The ground wus pretty
warm, judgin’ by the voice uv Kison The four
kept on shriekin’ for ’bout half an hour, an’ then
we interfered an’ had Kison loosed. Ihe blacks
wus very friendly with us, an’ ses they wus dom
it fer Kison’s good, bein’ persuaded by the two
gins, who loved Kison a lot. Ugh! there sa
dashed greenhead bit me on the calf! _ I’ll fry his
liver fer breakfas’ termorrerif I find him.”
“Wot become o’ Kison?” asked Bob.
“Well, yer see, Kison an’the other blacks wus
quite cured uv their rheum’tism, but he didn t
stay long with us. He had a sorter dreamy idea
that we put the blacks up ter scorch him. Yer
don’t think I’d do sich a thing, do yer ? But yer
can’t lick gum-leaves for rheum’tism, though.”
A. P. ROGER.
Love’s Sweet Dream.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Grigson was engaged in proposing to little Miss
Jones.
“ You have refused to marry me, Miss Jones,
for three evasive reasons,” he said, with mild
indulgence. “One, I tie my shoes in granny-
knots. Two, my taste in theology does not suit
you. Three, I don’t wear a beard to hide my
neckties. But your real reason I can guess. You
are a shy bush flower and shrink from a gay city
life by my side? It’s your modesty, diffidence,
and reserve. Am I not right ? ”
Miss Jones closed her book with a bang.
“You may hear my real reason,” she said
briefly. “I refuse to marry you, Mr. Grigson,
because there is too much variety in the species
already.”
*• W —what? ” said Grigson.
“ The undesirable types of civilisation must be
allowed to die out,” said Miss Jones, “and only
the highest types of humanity reproduce their
kind. Why should you and I repeat the mistakes
of our parents, and multiply the ricketty ? My
figure is an agony of angles ; I have the shambling
gait of the woman-like ape; my face is a half-
round square ; my ears don’t match; my nose is a
reproach ; there is not enough skin on my features
to contract; and I neither stand, run, jump,
climb, nor swim as the mother of a race should do.”
“And yet I am willing to marry you!’’said
Grigson, hilariously.
“As for you,” continued Miss Jones, “ a heavy
parcel would make you bow-legged Your mouth,
nose and chin are bunched together monkey-
fashion. The healthy savage turns his toes in—
you turn yours out in an abject manner. You are
slower than a horse, duller-eyed than a hen, and
weaker than a baby elephant.”
Grigson opened his face, but Miss Jones waved
him to silence.
“ The progeny of such a pair,” she said, “ would
have no teeth, no hair, no color, no muscle, no
thumbs, no great toes, no waist, no calves, no
organs of defence or attack —no sense.”
Grigson rose and walked feebly to the door, but
forgot his hat and had to go back for it. Miss
Jones was turning up page 1333 of “The Origin
of Species,” and did not hear him.
For the first time in his life Grigson felt the
need of a tail to drag dejectedly behind him.
M. LLOYD.
The Halifax (Q.) correspondent of The
Federal, writing in refutation of allegations that
white eane cutters were not a success in the
Herbert River district, states that
A gang of eleven white cane-cutters started at M'Nade
plantation, and with the exception of one man, who had
to give up through illness, they worked the whole of the
season, never losing any time either from “ sickness,
drink, unreliability, attributed laziness, or otherwise."
Their average for the season was 10s. 6d. per day, and
this in the face of obstacles thrown in their way by the
C.S.R. Co., which kept them short of trucks.
“Mus.” tells the Tale of a Wardrobe :
It was in a fashionable Sydney boarding-house. He
wns a little old man with heaps of money. His wife had
been his lady-help, or typiste, or companion, or some-
thing. She had prettyish eyes, and played and sang with
what Mr. Jephson, who belonged to the Liedertafel,
called abandon. Almost every evening she told her hus-
band that he looked tired, and advised him, kindly but
firmly, to go to bed and have a good night’s rest. After
he had gone, she would go to the piano and spread her-
self out. One evening, when she was singing a love-song
—with young Mr. Ffollet turning over the leaves, Mr.
Jephson hanging on to the back of the chair, and Mr. St.
Maur drawing the cork of Mr. Jolliboy’s bottle of cham-
pagne—we heard an awful crash overhead (in the old
gentleman’s bedroom) and we all rushed up pell-mell.
But the room was undisturbed, except that the wardrobe
was lying front downwards on the floor, with swear-
words coming out of its inside. It took all the boarders
and the Japanese boy to lift it. Then the doors flew
open, and there was the old gent hanging on to a peg by
the neck of his nightshirt. He said something about
“ having a lark ” with Mrs. S. Not being accustomed to
the place, he had evidently got entangled, and in his
struggles had capsized the wardrobe. Mr. St. Maur
calmly observed, “ Permit me to assist you off,” and Mr.
Jolliboy said he’d heard of skeletons in cupboards.
“ Fools, all!'” hissed the old gentleman—and we “got”
precipitately.

On Our Selection, written by A. H. Davis
(“ Steele Rudd ”) and published by Tub
Bulletin Co., has earned the title of “the
jolliest book ever printed in Australia.”
There is laughter on every page ; yet the
book remains grimly true to the facts. As
a picture of life on an Australian selection
it has never been surpassed. Since it was
published, little more than two years ago,
upwards of 12,000 copies have been sold at
the comparatively high price of 6s.—making
I
an Australian publishing record. The reaso'n of the" sale is’not far to seek : the
people who have bought the book know they have got value for their money ;
and they recommend it to others. The price is comparatively high because the book is expensive to
produce. It is well printed, and strongly bound in cloth in good style. There are numerous pictures
by such well-known illustrators as Mahony, Leist, Lambert, Fischer, Fullwood, and Vincent From
cover to cover the book is Australian—the product of Aus-
tralian workmen. If it were produced by cheaper labour
in England, it would cost less, and could be sold at a
cheaper rate. The publishers prefer to employ Australian
labor only. When you buy On Our Selection you pay
a little more than you pay for an English book ; but
you get 100 times the Australian value. “Steele Rudd’s”
book has gone all oyer Australia, and wherever it has
gone it has become the treasure of a household, often
the delight of a township. People never leave off
reading it until it is thumbed to rags ; and then they
buy another copy. The characters of “Our Selection”—
“Old Dad,” “Joe,” “Dave,” “Sarah,” and the rest —
have become famous throughout the country j and their imitation by weaker writers proves the force
of their grip on the public mind. Any bookseller will sell you the book; or if you send a postal
order for 65., with Bd. postage, direct to Tub Bulletin office, it will be forwarded to any address
by return mail.
